PAKISTAN
PUNJAB PRIVATE SECTOR GROUNDWATER DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
1. SECTORAL BACKGROUND
A. Introduction
I . I          The groundwater use has been a major factor in the growth of agricultural production in
Pakistan over the past two decades. Groundwater tubewells not only supply additional water but have
provided flexibility to match canal water supplies with crop water requirements. Before 1960s the
development of the groundwater tubewells was limited to open dug wells operated by animal power
mostly outside the canal command areas. Large scale development of groundwater resources was first
attempted by the Government under Salinity Control and Reclamation Projects (SCARPs program) for
controlling waterlogging and salinity. Under the SCARP projects, about 13,500 publically owned and
operated tubewells were installed. In areas underlain by fresh groundwater (FGW), water from SCARP
tubewells (STWs) was used to supplement canal supplies.   SCARP tubewells had a significant
demonstrative effect in starting private tubewell development in fresh groundwater areas. As a result,
private tubewells (PTWs) grew rapidly and there are now more than 300,000 private tubewells in the
country. With rapidly growing private sector and declining performance of SCARP tubewells, there is
need for redefining the role of the Government in the groundwater development. Hence, redefinition of
this role in Punjab's groundwater development sector is the main objective of this project.
1.2            The project builds on the experiences of SCARP Transition, Irrigation Systems
Rehabilitation. On-Farm Water Management programs and Private Tubewell Development Projects. This
staff appraisal report is based on the preparation report' by the Government with assistance of UNDP2
and the findiiigs of Pre-Appraisal and Appraisal missions which visited Punjab during July, 1994 and
June, 1995. respectively.
B. Agricultural Sector
1.3            Although the share of agriculture in the economy has been slowly decreasing, it is still
the backbone of the economy, employing more than 50 percent of the labor force and earning (directly
or indirectly) 70 percent of export revenues3. Total cultivated area is 21 million hectares (ha), with
overall cropping intensity of 101 percent. About 55 percent of the cropped area is under food-grains,
the most important of which are wheat and rice. The main cash crops are cotton and sugarcane, grown
on 12.4 and 4 percent of land, respectively. Cotton and high quality rice (Basmati) are important export
crops, and Pakistan is one of the leading cotton exporters in the world. Other major crops include
fodder, fruit and vegetables.
Third SCARP Transition Project. Project Preparation Report, Associated Consulting Engineering (ACE), May 1994.
Preparation was funded under. Investment Project Preparation in Water Sector --Umbrella-1l (PAK1881016) project financed by the
Umted Nations Development Program (UNDP).
Agricultuial policy and strategies for growth are the subject of a recent Bank report, "Pakistan: A Strategy for Sustainable Agricultural
Growthi". Report No.13092 PAK, November 3, 1994.



1.4           Most of the poor live in rural areas. Agriculture has particularly strong interaction with
rural poverty. Flight from rural areas is a contributing factor to urban poverty. Although absolute
poverty in Pakistan declined from 40 percent of rural population in the early-1960s to 25 percent by the
mid 1980s, this reduction is much less than East Asian countries with similar agricultural growth rates.
Agriculture is the primary user of natural resources --land and water resources. The development in the
sector has a direct impact on the incidence and pattern of poverty, natural resource management and
environmental protection, andi therefore, on sustainable development.
1.5           The sector has generally performed well, growing by more than three percent per annum
over the last three decades. The sources of growth have changed over the years: during the 1960s,
growth was mostly the result of the rapid uptake of the improved seed, fertilizer and irrigation packages;
during the 1970s it was the intensification of water and fertilizer use; and in the 1980s it was
improvement in crop management and incentives. The Government of Pakistan (GOP) has recently
launched a major economic reform progranm with the objectives of adjustment and growth. Agriculture
would have to play an important role in this process. However. there is limited scope for expanding
cultivable land or irrigation supplies and future growth has to rely on more efficient use of limited
resources. There is a particular need to ensure efficiency and sustainability in the management of land
and water, which are the two most important natural resources.
Irrigation Sector
1.6           Pakistan's agriculture is almost wholly dependent on irrigation --irrigated land supplies
more than 90 percent of agricultural production. Agriculture in most areas is not possible without
irrigation because the climate in Pakistan is arid to semi-arid witlh low and variable rainfall. Annual
rainfall over much of area is not more than 150 mm per annum, and evaporation is very high, ranging
from 1,250 mm to 2,800 mm per annum. However, there are abundant surface water resources in the
Indus Basin, which covering 566,000 km2 (70 percent of the country), is the major source of Pakistan's
water. The Indus Basin Irrigation System commands about 14 million ha which accounts for the bulk
of the agriculturally productive land. Annual river inflows average about 180 billion m3 of which about
130 billion m3 are diverted to canals. The Indus Basin Irrigation System is composed of three major
reservoirs (Chasma, Mangla and Tarbela)4, 19 barrages, 12 link canals, 43 canal conmnands and over
107,000 watercourses. The length of the canals is about 58,000 km, with communal watercourses (W/C),
farm channels and field ditches running another 1.6 million km.
1.7           In the Indus systein, river water is diverted by barrages and weirs into main canals and
subsequently into branch canal, distributaries and minors. Open, free-flowing outlets (moghas) regulate
water flow from distributaries and minors to W/Cs. Watercourse command area (chak) is a complex
miniature irrigation system with an average length of channels of 16 km. Water distribution to the field
is governed by a weekly time rotation (warabandi) based on the size of the land holding.
1.8           The canal system is also a major source of recharge for the groundwater. In fresh areas,
groundwater is pumped by public and private tubewells to supplement canal supplies. About 11,000
publically owned and operated STWs currently pump approximately 9 billion m3 annually. Pumpage
from STWs, however, is declining. On the other hand, with rapidly growing number of private tubewells
(PTWs) pumpage from groundwater is over 60 billion mi. STWs pump water in the communal
watercourses and it is distributed through the warabandi system. PTWs are mostly located on the farms
and farmers have more flexibility in their operation. Water supply from public tubewells is relatively
less significant --less than 15 percent of total groundwater pumpage or equivalent of less than 7 percent
Total live storage capacity of three reservoirs is about 18 billion m'.



of canal water diversions. However, public tubewells require about half of Government's irrigation
operation and maintenance (O&M) expenditures.
1.9            Flat topography and lack of well-defined natural drainage in the Indus Plain create a
surface drainage problem. The problem has been compounded by the construction of roads, railways,
flood embankments and irrigation systems that obstruct natural flows. The irrigation systems were
developed without provision of drainage. Over the years, about 14,000 km of surface drains have been
constructed in Punjab and more recently in the tail reaches of Rohri and Nara canal commands in Sindh.
The surface and subsurface drainage systems are inadequate. Under SCARPs, attempts were made to
meet the subsurface drainage iieeds through deep tubewells. A limited area is provided with tile drainage.
1. 10          About 2.2 million ha of irrigated land is outside Indus canal commanded area (cca),
scattered in relatively small parcels with water coming from open wells, tubewells, lift pumps, karezes,
springs, and small diversions. Generally, water supply in these system  is uncertain and varies with
season and location. Farmers have a major role in the management of these systems and some of these
were developed by communities themselves. Normally, these system do not suffer from waterlogging
and salinity problems.
1.11           While the irrigation and drainage systems are central to Pakistan's economy, they are
facing severe problems.  Despite Government's substantial budgetary input, the system is facing a
shortage of resources and suffering from severe and worsening operational problems. In addition to water
scarcity, major problems facing the sector are: inefficient management of water systems (that is low
delivery efficiency, and inequitable distribution); waterlogging and salinity; over exploitation of
groundwater in fresh groundwater areas: inadequate O&M; and insufficient cost recovery. The average
delivery efficiency is reported to be about 35 to 40 percent from the canal head to the root zone, but
actual is even worse. Most of the losses are in the watercourses. The loss of such a major part of
surface water not only reduces available water for crops but it also contributes to waterlogging and
salinity. Another serious problem is inequitable distribution. Excessive losses compared with the original
desigin also meanis that water does not reach the tail end of the system. Moreover, illegal pumping from
canals adds to the inequity of distribution. Poorly maintained watercourses have the highest incidence
of illegal diversions. Many of the above problems are worsened by an excessive Government role with
inadequate participation of the beneficiaries in the system management.
C. Groundwater Potential and Development
. 12           Most of the Indus Basin has been formed as a result of alluvium deposits derived by rivers
from the miountaini ranges in the north. These alluvial deposits consist principally of fine and medium-
grained sands and silts. although there are discontinuous lenses of silt and clay. Their depth ranges from
450( m in upper Punjab to about 60 m in lower Sindh, where it is layered with silty and heavy deltaic
sediment. There is a vast unconfined aquifer of around 6 million ha covering most of this area. The
water table is mostly high and has been rising due to lack of drainage. The hydrogeological conditions
are mostly favorable for pumping by tubewells. Groundwater quality is variable, ranging from fresh near
the major rivers to highly saline further away. In some areas, groundwater is saline even near the rivers
due to local salt deposits. In total, about 79 percent of the area in Punjab and 28 percent of the area in
Sindh has fresh groundwater5 which is suitable for irrigation. However, the residual sodium carbonate
(RSC) which results from use of groundwater is often high. Exclusive reliance on groundwater can lead
to a build-up of salts. resulting in binding of the soil structure, reduced water infiltration into the root
zone and falling yields. Ideally, groundwater should be mixed with canal water for irrigation use.
Total dissol ed salt less than 1 .200 ppm and electrical conductivity less than 2 mmhos.



-4-
Public Tubewell Development
1.13          By the 1950s, large areas in the Indus basin became waterlogged and soil salinity
increased, adversely affecting the agricultural productivity. Government involvement in the groundwater
development began with efforts to control the "twin menaces" of waterlogging and salinity by providing
drainage. The Government embarked on a series of SCARPs in the late-1950s aimed at lowering the
groundwater table by providing "vertical drainage" through large capacity. deep tubewells (60-140 liters
per second capacities and depth of 40-120 meters). Because of better economic returns, priority was
given to locating SCARPs in the areas where groundwater quality was suitable for supplemental
irrigationi, making the drainage a by-product in effect.  Over the last three decades about 13,500 STWs
have been installed by the Government in 36 projects covering a gross area of about 3.7 million ha. Most
of the STWs were equipped with electrically powered motors and deep turbine pumps with a relatively
large capacity of about 3 cusecs (.085 cubic meters/second).  Almost 75 percent of all STWs were
installed in the Punjab --8,000 in fresh groundwater areas and 1.800 in saline groundwater areas. In
saline grounidwater areas the STWs pumped water into drains or, when this was not possible, into the
larger canals. In FGW areas, water from STWs was normally pumped into watercourses and used for
irrigation along with surface water sL, plies. Bank involvement in public tubewell development was
limitetd to ilstallation of STWs in saline areas of Punjab under SCARP VI project (Cr. 0754-PAK).
1.14   Performance and Sustainability of SCARP Tubewells.         STWs were generally successful
in meeting their objective of maintaining the groundwater tables at desirable levels and providing a
supplemilentary source of irrigation. Their performance, however, declined over time due to poor
management and inadequate availability of funds for O&M. Presently, most of the STWs are very old,
equipment has worn out resulting in frequent breakdowns, and well capacities' are significantly below
their original designs. In Punjab, about half of STWs are below 50 percent of original capacity and only
15 percent have more than 80 percent. The utilization of STWs at present is considerably lower than it
was originally and water supplies have become unreliable due to the factors mentioned above and
prolonged blackouts due to power shortage and an unreliable public grid.
1.15          The O&M  costs of STWs have increased mlainyfold due to rapidly increasing power
tariffs, wages and mainitenance and replacement requirements.  Presently, the Punjab Irrigation
Departmenit (PID) is responsible for all O&M  costs of STWs. These cost include electricity charges,
repair and mainteniance, operation of special workshops for repairs, salary of a full-time operator and
higher managemiient, and other establishment costs. Funding for O&M of these tubewells is provided to
PID by the Government of Punjab (GOPunjab) through non-development budgetary allocations.
Expenditures for O&M of STWs in FGW areas of Punjab during fiscal year 1994-95 were almost equal
to O&M expenditures on canals while actual requirement were even more. Budget allocations for O&M
of STWs in 1995-96 exceed the allocations for canals. Annually they are more than Rs. 150,000/STW.
1.16          Water charges (abiania) are based on crop acreage and type of crop. In Punjab, farmers
receiving water from canals as well as from STWs are liable for paying double water charge (double
abianaia). (twice the amount of farmers receiving water from canals only). However, present water
charges only recover about 35 percent of provincial O&M costs on the irrigation and drainage systems.
Since O&M cost of STWs, per unit of water supplied, is much higher than canals, the provincial subsidy
for the O&M of STWs is many times more than for canals. Despite these expenditures water delivery
service from STWs has deteriorated due to institutional deficiencies. These include lack of motivation
and accountability of agency staff, high levels of political interference and rent seeking, as well as
inadequate conicerin for the needs of users.
Specitic capacity ot the well detiined as discharge per unit leIngd Ul drawdown, e.g. cusecs per foot of drawdowit



1.17           The void created due to institutional weaknesses is filled to some extent by informal
farmers' groups which have sprung up in STW areas. These groups look after daily operations, such as
switching on the pumps' and regular maintenance. They also carry out repairs using local mechanics,
electricians and other services from the local markets. Funds for these repairs are usually collected on
an ad hoc basis from the benefiting farmers. However, farmers find it difficult to carry out repairs
involving the main shaft of turbine pumps as it requires specialized equipment available from PID
workshops. Nevertheless, they do follow up and assist PID in expediting such repairs.
1.18           The operation of STWs in the public sector is unsustainable due to institutional
weaknesses as well as financial and economic reasons. The institutional weakness essentially arise from
centralized management and lack of transparency without any formal participation of beneficiaries. Due
to the overall resource scarcity Pakistan is facing presently, it is becoming increasingly difficult to
allocate adequate funds for operation of STWs, and replacement of old ones. The economic cost of
pumping water by elect:iuity operated STW  is much higher (Rs. 0.20 per cubic meter) than diesel
operated shallow tubewells (Rs. 0. 10 per cubic meter).
Private Tubewell Development
1.19           A virtuil explosion in the private tubewells development followed the introduction of
tubewell technology by the public sector. Currently there are over 300,000 PTWs installed for irrigation
purposes, of which 80 percent are in the Punjab supplying 40 percent of all irrigation water. PTWs are
predominantly small shallow wells which use locally manufactured engines and centrifugal pumps. Their
average capacity is one cusec (0.028 ml per second) with maximum  size of two cusecs.  More
importantly, as yields and input levels rose, so did the importance of timely and adequate water supplies.
By installing PTWs, farmers reduce their risks of crop failure and improve yields by smoothing out
natural and operational fluctuations ;n water supplies from the canal system. Even in the STWs areas
of Punjab there are more than 50,000 PTWs. While returns to tubewells are high, the utilization rates
are very low. PTWs are used by both headenders and tailenders, although the tailenders rely more on
groundwater. Most PTWs are owned by medium to large farmers with holdings of five hectares or more.
Presently, there is neither any mechanism for allocating groundwater rights nor for regulating its use.
Any land owner can install a tubewell and begin pumping groundwater and in some areas there has been
over-exploitation due to anarchic private tubewell development.
Community Tubewell Development
1.20           There are a very limited number of community tubewells (CTWs) in operation, many of
which date back to the early 1960s with roots in the cooperative movement. The CTWs are set up and
operated by informal farmers' organizations with day to day management entrusted to one person who
is paid an honorarium. Like STWs, CTWs pump water into watercourses and this is shared either on
the basis of fixed turns (varabandi), on demand, or a mixture of both. The basis for levying charges
also varies. Charges are levied either on the basis of irrigated land, hours supplied, or both.8 Within
the CTW  command, farmers also supplement supplies through PTWs. Recently, about 550 CTWs in
Punjab and 150 CTWs in Sindh have been installed under Second SCARP Transition Project (Cr. 2257-
PAK). Farmers' Organizations (FOs) have installed these CTWs, with project assistance, and accepted
Due to powe~r imterruptions as well as low voltage the tubewells trips off and often has to be turned on several time every day. This
is otten donie hs the farmer on whose land STW is located or whose house is nearest.

expenditures and additionial amounts were then collected through a levy on land holdings. Famiers paid up to Rs. 8.0 per acre in

STWs with CTWs and the number is increasing. Therefore, in view of promising start more STWs are
being assigned for privatization under this model.
Behavior of Groundwater Aquifers
1.21            In the Indus Basin as a whole, recharge to the groundwater aquifers and extractions' are
in balance."  However, in the fresh groundwater areas of Punjab pumpage exceeds recharge and as a
result the water table is falling. In contrast, in the saline areas the water table is rising. The same holds
true in SCARP areas. In all FGW SCARP water tables are falling." This situation has reduced the
need for drainage to lower groundwater table, and unlike the original objectives of providing drainage -
-a public good, the STWs are merely supplying irrigation water which is a private good. Therefore,
government involvement in the operation of STWs is no longer justifiable.
1.22    Regulation of Groundwater Use.            In the past rising and not falling groundwater tables were
the major problem.  Therefore, government encouraged the installation of tubewells through tax
incentives and subsidies. The situation now is reversing in most of FGW areas, therefore, for sustainable
use of groundwater resources the need for enforcement of suitable regulations is more pressing now.
Existing legislation'2 include provisions for the control over groundwater aquifers, however, these were
never implemented, mainly because regulation was seen unnecessary. The monitoring, and regulation
of groundwater exploitation is a government function. However, to enable government to regulate
groundwater use successfully, its institutional capacity has to be enhanced in groundwater monitoring,
refining regulation requirements, assessments of safe cxploitation levels and allocation of rights, staffing,
technical competence, and organizational arrangements for implementation of regulatory functions. While
the proposed National Drainage Program would strengthen national capacity for groundwater monitoring,
the project would address local monitoring for the implementation of regulations, assessment of
sustainable yields from aquifers as well as capacity for implementing regulations.
D. Bank Group Assistance Strategy
1.23            The overall objective of the Bank's assistance strategy in Pakistan is to help reduce
poverty.  To address this objective, the Bank's approach highlights two closely linked challenges:
improving the inadequate basic social services and reducing constraints to sustained growth. Expanding
access to and improving the quality of basic social services are essential to improve living standards in
Pakistan, which now has dismal social indicators. In addition to assisting the completion of fiscal
adjustment, the Bank's strategy is to support the growth objectives by focusing on structural reforms,
improving the enabling environment for the private sector, strengthening inadequate basic infrastructure,
Groundwater aquifers are recharged by rain, rivers, seepage from canals. watercourses and fields. Extractions include. evaporation
and pumpage by tubewells.

reducing constraints on agriculture productivity, and protecting Pakistan's natural resource base. In
focussing assistance on public policy, institutional development, critical public investments for private
sector led growth, and strengthening the basis for private investments, the Bank is assisting the
redefinition of the public and private sectors in Pakistan.
1.24           In agriculture, the focus of our policy dialogue is on reshaping the public and private
sector roles. The Bank supports maintaining a high rate of agricultural growth, with emphasis on further
improving the link between agricultural growth and poverty alleviation, and ensuring environmentally
sustainable agricultural development. The Bank's short- and medium-term assistance will focus on: (a)
redefining the roles of the public and private sectors in all aspects of agriculture with fundamental shifts
in the irrigation and drainage subsector towards more decentralized and participatory management
systems; (b) reducing distortions in input and output prices and trade regimes; (c) improving efficiency
of agricultural public expenditures; (d) strengthening natural resource management by emphasizing local
institutions: and (e) strengthening support services to increase productivity and diversify agriculture.
1.25           In irrigation and drainage, Bank is encouraging the Government to adopt a completely
new approach.'3 Consistent with the country assistance strategy, the goals of establishing an efficient,
self-sustaining irrigation and drainage system are to be achieved by promoting the use of market-
determined incentives for improving the supply and management of irrigation and drainage services, and
by giving the private sector a greater stake in the system. The irrigation service would be decentralized
and privatized by developing commercially oriented public utilities (PUs) on a canal command basis, and
suitable FOs. and by formalizing water rights and water markets. Autonomous provincial authorities
would be developed for water delivery to PUs and handling off-farm drainage and regulatory bodies for
regulating the PUs and groundwater resources.
1.26           The restructuring of irrigation services cannot be achieved immediately, and must be done
in steps for successful implementation. The transformation of surface systems would be implemented
through a series of canal command level pilot projects in which PUs and FOs will be formed. The total
transition may take from 15 to 20 years. These pilots are under preparation in all provinces and would
be supported through other projects or sector loans such as National Drainage and Irrigation Programs.
While implementation of the strategy in the surface systems is still evolving, considerable experience
already exists in the country in private tubewell development and divestment of public tubewells.
Building upon this experience, the proposed project would be a major step towards implementing the
irrigation and drainage strategy in groundwater sector while leading the way for reforms in the
management of the surface systems. It fits into the Bank Group's country assistance strategy as it would
provide assistance in: (a) redefining the role of government in the groundwater development; (b) assigning
groundwater rights and developing regulatory framework for ensuring sustainable use of groundwater
resources; (c) improving water use efficiency; (d) rationalizing public expenditures for O&M of the
irrigation and drainage systems, and strengthening the principles of capital cost recovery in the sector;
(e) enhancing community participation, thereby, building a solid base for ultimately transferring all
irrigation systems to the beneficiaries; and (f) protecting the environment by controlling saline water
intrusion into fresh aquifers.
E. Government Policy
1.27           Since the 1980s, Pakistan has developed a comprehensive policy reform framework to
address its development challenges. The strategy concentrates on three broad development goals:
Pakistait-Irrigation and Drainage: Issues and Options, Report No. 11884-PAK. March 25, 1994.



accelerating efforts to tackle poverty and human resources development issues; strengthening enabling
environment for the private sector; and improving management of Pakistan's natural resources and the
environment. 4
1.28            The Government agrees with  the  Bank's assistance  strategy  in agriculture, and
irrigation/drainage sectors for achieving development goals. Government strategy in irrigation and
drainage sectors reflected in the Eighth Five Year Plan (1993-98)'5 is fully in line with Bank's assistance
strategy. The Government has started the institutional reforms by initiating the formation of Area Water
Boards at the canal command level and Provincial Irrigation and Drainage Authorities at the provincial
levels.  For implementation of the strategy the government has started preparation of several pilot
projects. In the groundwater sector, a major shift in the Goverrnent's policy towards greater private
sector role dates back to the Revised Action Programme for Irrigated Agriculture (RAP, 1979).16  The
Government endorsed the RAP recommendations for transferring the groundwater development
responsibility, as well as STWs to the private sector in 1980, and adopted it as a part of revised National
Agricultural Policy. This policy has been reiterated in the Sixth (1983-88) as well as in the Seventh
(1988-93) Five Year Plans. The government is implementing these policies by instituting a moratorium
on the installation and replacement of public tubewells in the fresh groundwater areas and by supporting
projects aimed at divestment of public tubewells.   The Government is strongly  committed to
privatization  of groundwater development.   During  the current Five Year Plan  (Eighth) the
Government plans to support transfer of 8,000 STWs to the private sector. Subsequent to a pilot project
in Punjab under which 213 STWs water divested, the Government is privatizing 1,346 STWs in Punjab
and 380 STWs in Sindh under the Second Scarp Transition Project.
F. Bank Group Support and Lessons Learned
Bank Group Support
1.29            Pakistan's agricultural development efforts have attracted substantial support from  the
Bank Group. To date, about 60 agricultural (and Environment) projects in Pakistan have received
US$2.3 billion equivalent of IBRD/IDA financing (68 percent IDA'7 and 32 percent IBRD), the majority
of which has been for irrigation. Projects have also included: agricultural support services (credit,
research and extension); commercial agriculture (seeds and agro-industry), forestry, watershed,
environment protection and resource conservation; livestock development; and flood damage restoration.
Bank's involvement in the water sector dates back to the successful settlement of the apportionment of
the Indus water between Pakistan and India. Until mid-1970s, the Bank assisted with the construction
of large dams and link canals to develop additional water supplies. The Bank also assisted GOP with its
program for controlling waterlogging and salinity. In the 1980s, increasing attention was devoted to
irrigation systems rehabilitation, on-farm water management, and private tubewell development along with
controlling the problems of waterlogging and salinity, and providing an outlet to the sea through the Left
Bank Outfall Drain for disposing Indus basin's drainage effluent. In addition to providing financial

assistance, the Bank has traditionally played a critical role in the water sector policies, investment
planning and donor coordination. Major sectoral planning studies have been carried out with Bank
assistance, such as Indus Special Study (1967)'8, RAP (1979), Water Sector Investment Planning Study
(1990), Drainage Sector Environrnental Assessment (1993). Bank's recent Agriculture, and Irrigation
and Drainage sector strategies are leading the sectors in new directions and its financial assistance, as well
as policy advice, have contributed to attaining self-sufficiency and export generation in the past decade.
Lessons Learned
1.30   There can be no doubt that the provision of irrigation and drainage facilities have had a major
positive impact on agricultural production in Pakistan. It is also evident that farmers generally respond
well when provided with reliable and timely irrigation service.  Nevertheless, there is room  for
considerable improvement in all aspects of planning, design, implementation and O&M of irrigation
systems. The Bank-supported projects have suffered implementation delays due to slow GOP approval
process, delays in recruitment of consultants, inadequate procurement procedures and inadequately
capitalized contractors, unresponsive managemuent, and problems in inter-agency coordination. In order
to avoid delays due to GOP approvals, the preparation of PC-I`9 was started immediately following the
project appraisal and it has been approved by the Provincial Development Working Party (PDWP).
Similarly, recruitment of project implementation consultants has been completed before Board
presentation. Bank's Standard Bidding Documents (BSBDs) would be used to reduce delays due to
procurement procedures, improve transparency and attract well-capitalized reputable contractors. For
smooth implementation as well as to allow flexibility, the project would be implemented through a
separate Project Management Unit (PMU), with a minimal staff but supported by management and social
consultants.
1.31    Beneficiary Participation.    In the past, government planned, implemented and operated the
irrigation and drainage system without much consultation with the beneficiaries. Farmers started to play
a significant role under IDA assisted On-Farm Water Management Prograrn (OFWM). Under OFWM,
formal Water Users' Associations were formed and registered to contribute to the lining of watercourses.
In Punjab, WUAs' provide skilled (masons) and urskilled labor, 30 percent of the material cost in six
installments over three years, and provide assistance in supervising the implementation. The WUAs play
a critical role in improving watercourses. However, many become inactive after completion of civil
works. The main reason for their inactivity is that Government keeps control of irrigation water as well
as revenue collection, water supply increases slightly while O&M requirements reduce. As a result,
WUAs do not have any function essential for operation of irrigation systems. Several measures are taken
in the proposed project towards developing sustainable FOs. FOs will be involved in planning,
implementation and O&M of irrigation facilities, especially the CTWs. FOs will control water supply
from the tubewells and have a continuous function --supplying water to its members, collecting revenue
and carrying out O&M without interference from any Government agency. They will remain active in
such an environment. The FOs would decide about the tubewell type (taking over STWs as such, with
modification or installing new ones, electric or diesel), machinery, size, and location of the CTWs. Other
civil works will be constructed after the successful functioning of FOs for at least one year. FOs will
collect funds, operate bank accounts, and plan and participate in the construction and improvement of
surface systems. Contrary to the OFWM in which WUAs were organized by the Government agencies,
The main purpose of die investigations resulting in the report "Water and Power Resources of West Pakistan. a Study in Sector
Planning", which became known as the Indus Special Study (1967) or Action Plan, was to develop a program of optimum
exploitation of water resources that would be available to Pakistan after implementation of the Indus Waters Treaty of 196(1.
PC-I stands for Planning Commission Proforma 1. It is GOP's project appraisal documents and its approval is a condition for
including project funding in the development program and disbursements to the implementing agencies.



- 10 -
specially trained Social Organizers working through local NGOs and village activists, will be responsible
for organizing FOs. The project will also support the training of FOs in the O&M of facilities, record
keeping of expenditure and water charges, operation of bank accounts, and water deliveries to individual
users. Moreover, the full recovery of capital costs with significant recovery up front will also lead to
sustainable participatory initiatives.
1.32   SCARP Transition Projects. The outcome of SCARP Transition Pilot Project (Cr. 1693-PAK)
and available information on Second SCARP Transition Project (CR. 2257-PAK)20 show that the
objectives of privatizing the groundwater pumpage while controlling the water tables at the desired level
have been met. Experiences of these transition projects are fully incorporated into the project design.
These include: limiting assistance for CTWs only; allowing flexibility to FOs in selecting type, size and
location of CTWs; emphasis on1 transfer of STWs as such to FOs; involvement of social mobilizers and
local NGOs; implementation by an autonomous PMU; training and a redeployment package for surplus
SCARPs O&M staff; linkage with watercourse renovations; and emphasis on monitoring and evaluation
(M&E). Encouraging PTWs to replace STWs results in anarchy, building excessive capacity leading to
over-capitalization and excessive O&M costs; danger of over-exploitation of groundwater, inequity in
water supply, as well as improper conjunctive use of canal and groundwater. Replacing STWs with
CTWs (instead of PTWs) would overcome these shortcomings2". The slow implementation of transition
projects is mainly due to conflicting interests of the implementing agency (PID) which has sole
responsibility for O&M of STWs. With transition of STWs the agency looses significant budgetary
resources and is left with staff redundancies, especially the STW operators. The disgruntled STW
operators, who mostly reside in the same village served by STW create project implementation problems
and a constant source of negative publicity for the project. These problems will be minimized by placing
PMU outside PID and introducing a package for redeployment of STWs staff (including retraining and
a severance package). M&E capacity at federal and provincial levels, which is generally weak, will be
enhanced under the proposed National Drainage Program. However, the project will also support M&E
specifically for groundwater regulations and performance of FOs. For ensuring a timely start, M&E
consultants will be recruited along with management consultants.
G. Rationale for IDA Involvement
1.33          Privatization of groundwater development is an important element of Government policy
which IDA supports.  However, its implementation is not easy due to problems associated with
elimination of STW's O&M subsidies and reduction in agency staff. IDA has been instrumental in
shifting the emphasis from groundwater development by the public sector to the private sector, based on
close farmers' involvement. No other donor has similar experience in Pakistan. IDA support is essential
for developing sustainable FOs for operation of community tubewells which are unlikely to emerge
spontaneously. Participatory management of groundwater would provide an excellent base for launching
participatory irrigation management program at the secondary level of the canal systems
(distributaries/minors). The project would also help institutionalize FOs and move towards decentralizing
the provincial irrigation agencies at canal command level public utilities. IDA's role is crucial in
achieving project objectives of reforming the institutional structure and implementing elements of the
irrigation and drainage strategy which was developed and agreed with the Government as result of
'   About 213 STWs were divested under SCARP Transition Pilot Project in Punjab. Approximately 1,346 in Punjab and 380 STWs
in Sindh will be divested under the Second SCARP Transition Project.
Based on the finding of the preparation report of the proposed project, the Second SCARP Transition Project was restructured
and about 230 STWs in Punjab were earmarked for replacement with CTWs only on experimental basis during mid-term review.

extensive sector work done over the last three years.22 IDA's involvement is necessary for redefining
the role of Government in the development of groundwater, development of a regulatory framework,
avoiding environmental hazards of saline water intrusion, and rationalizing public funds for O&M of the
irrigation and drainage systems and improving cost recovery from beneficiaries. The project would
support IDA's poverty relief objectives through its impact on growth of income of small farmers. In
addition, the project would support IDA's environrnental, public-sector management, and private-sector
development objectives.
THE PROJECT AREA
A. Physical Features
Location
2.1             The project area lies in the central and southern parts of Punjab Province and falls within
Gujranwala, Sargodha, Faisalabad, Multan and Lahore administrative Divisions (see Maps IBRD 27738
and 27739).23 It consists of fresh groundwater areas of SCARP II (including Shahpur), SCARP III
(Rangpur Unit), SCARP IV and SCARP V (Shorkot Kamalia). It also includes saline groundwater
(SGWs) area of SCARP 11 and saline areas within24 or at the boundaries of FGW areas, where they pose
risks of contaminating the fresh groundwater aquifer. The gross area is about 1.450 million ha, of which
1.188 million ha have FGW, while the canal commanded area (cca) is about 1.295 million ha of which
about 1.071 millions ha have FGW.  SCARPs in Punjab are generally between the major rivers (doabs)
and have flat topography (Annex I Table 1). Location, gross area, and cca of the SCARPs included in
the project are given in Annex I Table 2.
Climate and Soils
2.2             The Punjab (and the project areas) have a continental sub-tropical climate, ranging from
sub-humid in the north-east to arid in the south-west, with hot summers and mild winters. The average
monthly maximum temperatures range from 20C in the winter to over 420C in the summer, with May
and June being the hottest months prior to monsoon rains. The minimum temperatures fall below 5C
in the winter, with December and January being the coolest months. The area lies on the fringe of the
monsoon belt, with more than 70 percent of the rain falling during the monsoon period from June to
September. Average annual rainfall ranges from about 650 mm (Lahore) to 190 nmm (Bahawalnagar),
generally decreasing from the north-east to the south-west(Annex 1, Table 3). Conversely, the annual
reference evapotranspiration (ET() ranges from about 1,650 mm in Lahore to 1,880 mm in Bahawalnagar.
2.3             The soils have been formed from alluvium derived from the mountain ranges to the north
and deposited by the present and ancestral rivers of the Indus basin. The alluvium deposits consist
2-  Irrigation and agriculture strategies are outlined in recent Bank report. "Pakistan-Irrigation and Drainage: Issues an Options", Report
No. I 1884-PAK, March 25 and "Pakistan: A Strategy for Sustainable Agricultural Growth", Report No. 13092 PAK, November 3,
1994. Dissemination of the strategies was done through a series of national and international seminars and workshops organized by
the Bank, Government, and farming communities.
23   These Divisions include Gujrat, Sargodha. lhang, Muzaffargarh, Mandi Bahauddin, Sialkot. Gujranwala, Sheikupura, and Toba Tek
Singh districts
'    Often salinc groundwater pockets occur within overwhelmingly fresh groundwater areas and vice versa.



- 12 -
principally of fine and medium-grained sand and silt, although there are discontinuous lenses of silt and
clay. As a result, the alluvial deposits, which are from 12 to 500 meters thick, form a unified and highly
transmissive aquifer. The soils are relatively fertile, suitable for growing a wide range of crops with
good yields, particularly if sufficient nitrogen and phosphate inputs are provided. About 74 percent of
the soils are of medium to moderately coarse texture, have good internal drainage and are well-suited to
irrigated agriculture. About 13 percent are coarse-textured, with low water-holding capacity, requiring
good on-farm water management and frequent irrigations. The remaining 13 percent are moderately fine-
textured, with poor internal drainage characteristics. The implementation of the SCARP program has
reduced soil salinity/sodicity problems in most fresh groundwater areas. However, the situation is
worsening in some SGW areas. From periodic soil profile tests conducted by WAPDA, the proportion
of non-saline, non-sodic soil profiles varies from as low as 40 percent in SCARP V (Shorkot-Kamalia)
to 88 percent in Shahpur. (Annex 1, Table 4).
Water Resources
2.4    Surface Water Supplies.      The Indus River and its four major tributaries (Jhelum, Chenab,
Ravi and Sutlej) flow through the Punjab. Water from these rivers is diverted by a series of barrages into
9 large canal systems serving the project area. River flows are highly seasonal with almost 60 percent
of the total annual flow occurring between June and August. Yearly variations in surface water
diversions to the canals are reducing over time, mainly due to increasing control over the river system
by reservoirs, inter-river link canals and barrages. In the fresh groundwater areas of the project, the
average canal water supply at the watercourse head from 1988-1992 was 3.51 million acre feet (4,325
million m3) and accounted for 41 percent of total irrigation supplies. In saline groundwater areas, the
surface water supply averaged 0.86 million acre feet (1,063 million m3), equivalent to 69 Percent of total
irrigation supplies. Surface water supplies are also the main source of recharge to the groundwater which
is then reused by pumping through tubewells.
2.5    Groundwater Supplies.        A total of 5,054 STWs were installed in the project area, of which
4,233 were in the fresh groundwater areas and the remaining 821 in the saline areas. In FGW areas
about 90 percent of fresh groundwater STWs (3,860) are currently operational but only 15 percent are
in good condition, capable of pumping 80 percent or more of their original capacity (Annex 1, Table 9).
The present capacity of the STWs is estimated at 9,597 cusecs (272 m3 per second) supplying about 2.32
million acre feet (2,867 million m3) of irrigation water at the watercourse heads. Pumping groundwater
at the head of the watercourse improves overall distribution as well as application efficiency, including
that of canal water. In most watercourses the canal water does not reach towards the tail when the STW
is not operating. Therefore, tail farmers lose their share of canal water and they are left with the option
of using PTW water only. The use of groundwater without canal water leads to damaging of the soils.
In saline areas of SCARP II, about 426 have marginal quality water useable only after mixing with the
canal water. These tubewells pump water into the watercourses while the remaining tubewells having
water supply unsafe for irrigation use, discharge water into canals (119 STWs ) and drains. Total
pumpage in the saline area of SCARP II is estimated at 0.25 million acre-feet (308 million m3).
2.6           In addition, low capacity (typically 0.75 to 1 cusec) PTWs have been installed where fresh
or marginal groundwater can be obtained from shallow aquifer, usually to a depth of 30 meters. There
are about 44,000 PTWs in the fresh groundwater project areas pumping 3.01 million acre-feet (3,710
million m3). Their utilization rate of PTWs is extremely low compared with STWs --below 10 percent.
In addition, there are 1,780 PTWs in the saline zones of SCARPs II that have pockets of marginally fresh
groundwater. These PTWs pump about 0.13 million acre-feet (162 million m3). The supply of irrigation
water from different sources is given in Annex 1, Table 8 and summarized in Table 2.1 below.



- 13 -
2.7    Groundwater Levels and Trends.    Present average depths to water tables vary between
different SCARP areas. They range from 2.4 meters in Shahpur to 4.3 meters in SCARP IV in the pre-
monsoon period. After the monsoon they range from as high as 1.5 meters in Shahpur to as low as 4.2
meters in SCARP V (Shorkot-Kamalia). The trends of groundwater levels also vary among SCARP
areas. There have been increased waterlogging problems (water table between 0 and 1.5 m) in parts of
Shahpur, indicating a need for additional pumping. Conversely, water tables are falling slightly in
SCARPs 11, and are stable in SCARPs IV, and V (Shorkot-Kamalia) (Annex 1, Table 10). In saline
groundwater areas, groundwater levels are high, resulting in high evaporation and movements of salts
to the soils.

2.8    Groundwater Quality.           Most of the STWs in the fresh groundwater areas (91 percent)
produce water of safe or marginal quality, with about 9 percent producing hazardous quality water.
Although the overall salinity of most of the pumped groundwater is low, the residual sodium carbonate
(RSC) is often high, which can result in binding of the soil structure and reduced water infiltration into
the root zone. However, as most of the STW water is pumped into watercourses and mixed with surface
water supplies, the build-up of RSC is reduced. WAPDA's SCARP Monitoring Organization's records
show that there has been little overall change in water quality over the last 10 years but there has been
a decrease in the number of safe wells at the boundaries between the fresh and saline zones in SCARP
11. primarily due to movement of groundwater from  saline aquifer towards the fresh areas. Shallow
groundwater has not been monitored systematically, but its quality is usually better because surface
irrigation system is the main sources of recharge. However, in some areas PTWs water is higher in RSC
compared with STWs (Annex 1, Tables 11 and 12). Often PTW water is pumped into the field channels
without mixing with the surface water, therefore, negative effects of RSC build up rapidly.
B. Relevant Water Sector Institutions and Legislation
Water Sector Institutions
2.9            There are several institutions directly or indirectly involved in the water sector. At the
federal level the Water and Power Development Authority (WAPDA) as an executive arn of Ministry of
Water and Power is entrusted with the responsibility for: development of water resources and supply of
irrigaiioni water; prevention and reclamation of waterlogged and saline lands and drainage; flood control;
generatioln, transmission and distribution of power; and land navigation. In respect of the water sector



- 14 -
projects, WAPDA acts as an agency of the Governmeit for implementation of the schemes, which upon
completion are transferred to Provincial Irrigation Departmenits. WAPDA is also responsible for O&M
of multi-purpose reservoirs and some inter-river Iink canals. O&M of the irrigation and drainage systems,
including public tubewells, and flood control is responsibility of the PID.  Farmers maintain the
watercourses and farn chaninels. Water charges are assessed by the PID. However, they are collected
by the Provincial Revenue Department. The PID retains institutional control over public sources of water
and public drainage facilities. The role of Punjab Agriculture Department (PAD, Directorate General On-
Farn  Water Management) in water delivery and disposal is Iimited to registering the Water Users
Association, techiical support and improvement of watercourses and on-farm drainage. The Agriculture
Department in the past also offered assistance in the installationi of PTWs by renting drilling machinery.
Provincial subsidies for installationi of PTWs were also routed through PAD. However, due to financial
constrainits, these are now disbursed only In PID implemented SCARP transition areas.
2.10           The Planninig and Developmenit (P&D) Board, Federal Ministry of Planliling, and Planiling
Commission prepare five-year and aninual developmenit plans with support from sectoral agencies. They
are also responsible for project planniniig, appraisal and approval for inclusionl in the development plans.
Existing Legislation and Regulations
2.1 1          The Pun jab Canal and Drainage Act of 1 873 with subsequent amendmnents25 provides the
maini legislative basis for irrigation and drainage services. Althoughi water is a nationial resource, the Act
entitles the Provincial Government to use and control all water, rivers, streams, lakes, and subsoil water
for irrigation drainage and navigationial purposes. Even thouigil the meaning of "canal" includes tubewells,
the law  says very little about the use or control of ulidergrounid waters.  The Water and Power
Development Authority Act, 1958 extends WAPDA's control over undergrotiuid water in any region in
a province. The control is, however, in the context of controllinig waterlogging and salinity, one of the
main objectives of creating WAPDA. The Punjab Soil Reclamation Act, 1952 is most explicit about the
control of groundwater even though its objective was also to accelerate reclamation and improvemenit of
waterlogged and saline lands.  The Act gives control of all groulidwater uses except domestic and
livestock to the Pun jab Soil Reclamationi Board (clause 26).26  It provides the basis for licensing the
installationi of tubewells and authorizes the Board to close down tubewells, either temporarily or
permanently, if they negatively effect the existing use of water. Under the Act, the Board issued elaborate
rules (West Pakistan Land Water Development Board, Control over Undergrounid Waters, Rules, 1965)
for licensing the tubewell, inspection of licenses and penalties for using tubewells without a license. The
institutional structure of SCARPs and staffing was done exercising the powers under this Act. The Board
weint throughi several organizationlal structures, and in 1973, with the transfer of SCARPs to provinces,
PID  was appointed as its administrator for executing O&M  of the project, while, planning and
development fuLictionis were transferr-ed to the Punjab Planniniig and Development Board.
2.12           In the past. efforts of the Board and other agencies were focused prinarily on lowering
the water tables, and tubewells were generally encouraged for drainage as well as ir-igationi purposes.
There is no evidence of restricting tubewell developmenits throughi licensing in any area In the past.
Hlowever, the legislative basis exists for regulating groundwater use. Its implemenitation would be possible
only after defininig regulatling procedLires. creating an institutionial set-up and developing implementation
arrangemenlts.

C. Farm Size, Agricultural Production, and Marketing
Farm Size and Tenure.
2.13           The average farm size witlhin the project area ranges from about 3.8 ha in Lalhore Division
to 5. i ha in Sargodlha Division, withi an overall average of 4.4 ia. The farm  size distribution indicates
that about 69 percent of farms are less thani 5 hia, wiliie only 8 percent are larger than 10 ha (Annex 1,
Table 13). Overall, about 80 percent of farms (less thani 6 ha in size) have hialf ot the land, wiliie 20
percent farms (thiose greater thlan 6 lha) has the other half. Land lholdings are usually fragmented, witlh
each farimier cultivating an average of three plots located in different places. The average houselhold size
is about 7 persons.
2.14           About 77 percent of the farmiers cultivate their owIn land, and about 30 percent of these
farmers also rent some land. About 23 percent of farmers are pure tenants, usually renting from  larger
farmers or landlords on a shiare-cropping or cashi-rentinig basis.
Agricultural Production
2. 15   Cropping Patterns and Intensities.   There are three major cropping patterns in the pro'ject area.
The cottonl-whieat system  is prevalenit in southern SCARPs Ill Rangpur Unit; the rice-whieat system  in
SCARP IV and the northierin part of SCARP IL. and the sugarcane-wheat system in SCARPs V and most
of SCARP 11 and Slialipur. In the rtibi (winlter) season, whieat is the most important crop in each system,
typically taking up more than 50 percent of cca. Othier important wlinter crops include fodder (berseem)
and small areas of pulses. In the kharif(summer) season, cottoIn is grown on 35 percent of cca in cotton-
wheat system; paddy takes more than 50 percent of cca in rice-wheat system (about 80 percent in Basmati
and 20 percent in hiigih-yielding IRRI varieties); and sugarcane over 15 percent in sugarcane-wheat area.
Othier importanit crops grown in the khurif season include fodder (maize and sorghum), other grains
(maize, sorghlum  and millet), and some pulses and oilseeds. Orchards hlave been increasing in recent
years, the most importanit being citrus, guava and mango, with cropped areas ranging from 3 to 1 3 percent
of cca. Overall croppling intenisities hiave been rising and currently range from  120 to 145 percent in
different SCARPs. (Aniex 1, Table 14).
2.16   Crop Yields.  Wheat yields range from about 1.6 to 2.0 toins per ha, and have been increasing
at a rate of over 2 percent a year since 1980. Average maize yields range from 1 .3 to 1 .5 tons per h1a,
withi yields increasinlg by about I percent a year. Paddy yields range from about 1 .1 to 1 .5 tons per ha
and, ulilike othier grain crops, have remained more or less static in recent years. In the sugarcane-wheat
areas, average sugarcane yields are about 40 tons per ha. Cotton yields have averaged 1 .3 to 1 .8 tons per
ha in the cotton-wheat areas. Cotton yields have doubled since 1980. However, they are declining over
tihe last three years due to wide-spread viruses. Fodder yields are rarely recorded, but estimated average
berseenii yields are in the order of 25-30 toils per ha (fresh weight), about double the yield of kharif fodder
crops sucih as maize, sorghum and millet. (Aniex 1, Table 14)
2. 7   Water Input. Water supply is one of the major constrailits to agriculture production in the
project area. The crop water requirements are much higher than the effective rainfall (Annex I Table 15).
On an afilual basis, water supplies from  canals and groundwater meet only 90 percent of irrigation
rcqui4remnents (Anniex 1. Table 16).  lowever, water shortages are very acute in peak demand periods.
vlMarchl/April and( Scptemberi/October.
2. 1 8   Agricultural Inputs and Services.    Inputs, including fertilizer and pesticides, are mostly
provided throughi the private sector, with numerous small dealers supplying these items in the market



- 16 -
towns. HIowever, fertilizer imports are normally routed througlh the Punjab Agricultural Development and
Supply Corporation to a network of dealers. In the past, Government policy was to subsidize fertilizers,
pesticides and inputs; however, input supply has now been essentially privatized and subsidies eliminated,
particularly on nitiogenious fertilizers and pesticides. Fertilizer use is genierally low. Pesticide use is
signilficanit, mainilv because 60 to 80 percenlt of the cottoni crop is sprayed.
2.19           Use of improved seed has been held back by problems of availability, accessibility, and
quality. Most farimers keep their owi seed or buy inferior quality seed from  local dealers. The Punjab
Seed Corporation is the main source of improved seed for cotton, wheat and rice. Some private suppliers
have also recently started marketing seeds for cotton, maize and some minor crops. Seed produced by
the Seed Corporation is procured by the Agricultural Development and Supply Corporation and supplied
to the farmers through their dealers.
2.20           Agricultural extension services in the project areas have been strengthened under the
Agricultural Fxteisioni and Adaptive Researcih Project. Phase 11 (AEAR-11, Cr. 1 762-PAK), whichi
introduced thc traininig and visit system all over Punjab in 1987. At the district level, a Deputy Director
Agriculture is in chiarge of extensioni services, and is supported by an Assistant Director of Agriculture
at the fehsil level and Field Assistants, averaging about one per 1,100 farm household.
Marketing
2.2 1          In Pakistan, both Government and the private sector are engaged in procurement and
marketing of farn output. Government procurement through designated agencies covers all major crops
suchi as wheat, cotton, rice, gram, potatoes, onions, oilseeds and sugarcane at a guaranteed minimum price
or support price. Support prices for these products are fixed through a consultative process taking into
accounlt the cost of production, domestic demanid, supply and stock position, domestic and interinationial
prices, possible impact on the cost of living, and intersectoral considerationis. The support prices program
is designed to combat price falls ihmediately following harvest, which could force farmers with limited
storage to sell at depressed prices. The procurement agencies must purchase all quantities offered to them
if the iiarket price falls below the support price. Fundinig these purchases is often problematic.  The
program.i, throughi risk transfer, provides incentives to farmers at substantial cost to Government, which
sometlimes leads to a situation where a support price is set but no transactions take place because no
budget is provided.  Parastatals are not efficient in the handling of crops.  They also collude witl
processors or traders to share the gains from monopolistic market positions.
2.22           Farmers sell surplus production to iiidividual buyers, Government or semi-government
procuremenit agencies and agro-processing plants, or at village markets where transactions are carried out
by brokers, traders. amid commission agents. Further up the marketing chain is a wide network of iehsil
level markets through whiclh agricultural commodities from the village markets are channeled by brokers,
wholesalers amid retailers to the larger urban centers for sale to consumers and for export. The project area
has several major marketing centers at district and tehsil levels.  Present marketing and processing
facilities are adequate to handle the project's estimated outputs.
2.23           Currently, about 60 percent of Pakistan's wheat production is consumed on the farm or
sold in the open market and the rest is marketed in the public sector. Public sector procurement is carried
out bv the Provincial Food Departments and the Pakistan Agricultural Storage and Services Corporation.
About half of Pakistan's total paddy production is consumed on the farm, while most of the marketable
surplus is bougIlt by the state-ownied Rice Export Corporation and is exported. Cotton and sugarcanie are
sold, either directly or through an intermediary, to ginneries and sugar mills. Ginned cotton is conisumned
domestically as well as exported by the state-ownied Cotton Export Corporatiomi and private firms. In areas



- 17 -
wilere there are no sugar mills, sugarcanie is usually processed into gur (raw sugar) for local consumption.
2.24           Altlhoughi direct intervenition by the Government in agricultural markets has diminished,
major interventions still remaini --subsidized imports of wheat; duty and periodic restrictions on cotton
exports, aiid protection of sugarcanie. Major indirect interventionis arise from the protection of domestic
industry, whiicih penalizes agriculture througlh the impact on sectoral relative prices. These issues were
analyzed in the recent agriculture sector strategy and it is intenided that they would be addressed through
a sector operation focussing mainly on structural and policy reforms in the agriculture sector.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives
3.1            The overall project objectives are to increase the scope and productivity of Punjab's
irrigationi and drainage subsector and increase farners' incomes. Its specific objectives are to: (a) redefine
Governimienit's role in groundwater developmenlt and provide assistance to facilitate chanige; (b) develop
a monitoring program and regulatory framework to ensure sustainable use of the grouLidwater resources;
(c) develop sustainable farmers' organizationis (FOs) which can efficiently operate and maintain
groulidwater irrigation, improve surface irrigation and establish a base for participation in the management
of the canal systems; (d) increase beneficiaries' incomiies and alleviate poverty; (e) rationalize public
expenditure on O&M  of the irrigation and drainage systems as well as increase the recovery of public
expeniditures on irrigation infrastructure; and (f) avoid environimenital hlazard of saline water intrusion into
fresh grounidwater aquifers.
B. Project Description
3 .2           T he proposed project covering a large part of fresh grounidwater SCARP areas in Punjab,
would be implemiienited over a period of five years. The project is designed to meet the above objectives.
The major project components are: (a) disinvestmenit of SCARP tubewells; (b) development of a
grounddwater mon itorinlg and regulatory framework; (c) improvement of irrigation conveyanice facilities;
(d) preventioni of saline groundwater intrusion in fresh aquifers; (e) monitoring and evaluation of the
project imipact: and (f) project management, and technical assistance and training.
3.3            The proiect would be a major step towards self-sustainability of the irrigation and drainage
systems in Pun jab by developing sustainable FOs and transferring to them responsibility for O&M  of
STWs, whichi requires over forty percent of provincial current irrigation expenditure. At the same time,
distributioll of irrigation water from tubewells would be transferred from provincial irrigation agency to
the private sector. While decentralization of management and water markets in surface irrigation requires
experimentation throughi pilot projects, much experience exists on the involvement of the private sector
in groulidwater development. Tubewell water sales are common and development of FOs around tubewell
installationis is easier. Well-developed FOs and a framework for groundwater regulation would help in
the decenitralizationi of surface irrigation system  at canal command level, which would be addressed
throughi a series of pilot projects under sector loans.
Tubewells Disinvestment (Base Cost USS27.4 million)
3.4    Transfer of SCARP Tubewells (Base Cost US$12.5 million).              All  public   SCARP
tubewells presently operational in the fresh groundwater areas of the project would be transferred to thle



- 18 -
private sector. The project would provide assistance in the development of FOs and transfer of STWs to
them. Watercourse level FOs with minimum  membership of 51 percent of the water users on the
watercourse would be registered prior to formation of CTW FOs. CTW FOs would consist of a minimum
of 30 percent of the water users on the watercourse.
3.5           It is estimated that there are about 4.233 SCARP tubewells with an actual pumping
capacity of about 9,597 cusecs (Aninex I, Tables 6 and 9). Of these about 642 tubewells are in good
conditioni with effective capacity (ECAP) more than 80 percent (called Class A tubewells), about 1,077
are in medium condition (ECAP 80-50 percent, Class B) and about 1,788 in bad condition (ECAP less
thani 50%, Class C). About 353 STWs are not operational, and no data are available for about 373 STWs.
3.6           The transfer program is designed not to exceed the present pumping capacity of STWs,
and allowing flexibility of choices to the FOs. Farmers in the project area would be persuaded through
a social mobilization program  for forming FOs to take over the STWs and run those as community
tubewells (CTWs). FOs would chose the transfer and managemenit method suitable for their area. The
following are the likely options: (i) jointly take over STW as such and run as CTW (transfer mode); (ii)
jointly take over the STW  with modificalion and run as CTW; (iii) install new CTWs serving all or part
of the farmers served by STW; and (iv) replace lost pumpage with existing or new PTWs. The project
would provide assistance for CTWs only. The project would support one to two CTWs per watercourse
with a discharge capacity of one to two cusecs (average 1.5 cusecs), each pumping into the communal
watercourse (preferably at the head) with a maximum of four CTWs per STW (only for STWs serving
more than one watercourse). In case of transfer mode only one CTW would be needed as it is likely to
have sufficient capacity to serve all farmers on the watercourse.
3.7           Under the transfer mode, FOs would carry out minor repairs to starters, switches, motors,
pumpset couplings and make a security deposit for electrical connection. Under the modified mode, they
would replace the turbine pump with a centrifugal pump of lower capacity (average 1.5 cusecs) and the
electrical motor with a diesel enginie. The new CTWs are likely to be below 2 cusecs capacity with
ceentrifugal pumps operated by diesel engines.
3.8           It is estinated that (and assumed for costing and financing) all STWs in the project area
would be replaced by about 6,360 CTWs under the three transfer modes (Annex 2 Table 1). This
averages to 1.5 CTW per STW or about 1.2 CTW per watercourse or 60 percent of the watercourses
participating in the program with two CTWs each. It is estimated that about 150 STWs" in good
conditioni (Class A) would be taken over by FOs in the transfer mode. Some 1,643 STWs would be
modified and converted to CTWs. These would inciude all those working moderately well, and some of
those in poor condition. About 4,567 new CTWs would be installed. The number of tubewells under
different categories are indicative and may change during the course of project implementation. The
project would provide one tine finanicial assistance of Rs. 20,000 for modified and Rs. 30,000 for new
CTWs. This represents about 45 percenlt of the cost for new CTW and about 50 percent for modified
CTWs (Aninex 2, Table 3). The incentive structure would encourage the transfer and modified modes.
FOs would provide unskilled labor and bear remaining costs of CTWs. They would be responsible for
O&M of CTWs and deposit an amount equivalent of one seasons's (six months) O&M  in their Bank
AccouLit to be eligible for finanicial assistance.
3.9           In addition to the SCARP tubewells there are about 1,535 irrigation tubewells, in Punjab
owned by the Goverinment. Most of these tubewells are not operational. However, a significant amount
of funds are allocated in the recurrent budget to meet their fixed costs. Assurance were obtained from
Including 50 STWs in SCARP Shahpur whicih are shallow and fitted with centrifugal pumps.

GOPunjab that Government owned irrigation tubewells would be formally closed by June 30, 1998. and
GOPunjab would. (a) eliminate the provincial program for subsidizing individually owned private
tubewells in areas supplied by canal waler by September 30, 1996; and (b) initiate a study for the
preparation of a plan to phase out subsidies to the individually-owned private tubewells in areas not
supplied by canal water and where private tubewells are the sole source of irrigation water, and
implement such plan by December 31, 1997.
3.10   Community Development and Support (Base Cost US$6.7 million).          To achieve project
objectives, a basic requirement would be effective communllity participation in all phases of the
developmenit process. An essential activity is to set up, train and technilcally support FOs so that they are
able to effectively manage and sustaini the operation of CTWs. The FOs would be used as nuclei bodies
for other project activities such as improvement of irrigation and drainage systems. For developing
effective FOs, the project would support a social mobilization program. The PMU would have overall
responsibility for social mobilizationi working through a communily development and support group
(CDSG) recruited as consultancy services. The team would consist of core staff at the central level for
training, managing and coordinating the community participation program, social coordinators (SCs) in
the project area, and social organizers (SOs) working with the help of local NGOs and village level
activists. In order to recruit SOs who reside in the project areas and maintain relationship with farmers,
they would be recruited from local-level NGOs. The terms of reference for social mobilization team are
given in Annex 3.
3.11          PMU would also utilize the services of OFWM staff especially where they are involved
in the inproveinenit of watercourses. The OFWM teams would privatize 1,500 STWs, including 500
STWs on already improved watercourses, 500 on the watercourses improved under the project and 500
on ullimproved watercourses. The OFWM teams would be modified to include social organizers, who
would be trained by the CDSG.
3.12          A detailed community development plan is given in Annex 4.  The community
mobilizationi efforts would be carried out in three successive waves. First, a baseline survey of the STW
area would be carried out hy surveyors for collecting pertinent information about the STW and
watercourse comimianid. This inforimiationi would cover physical features, agricultural aspects, as well as
socio-economic factors.  Based on this information, SOs would prepare a suitable plan and initiate
dialogue with the farmers for replacing STW pumpage. The SOs would make an initial visit followed
by several return visits. The After-care Social Organizers (ASOs) would also participate in some of the
returni visits. Each SO would be visiting up to four STWs within the same geographical area at a given
time. To take advantage of the demonstrative effect, the visit schedule would be a rolling process,
whereby the SO would advance to the next closest STW. If an FO is formed for converting an STW to
CTW, the SO would arrange visits of technical staff to assist FO in deciding type of tubewell, size,
location and other technical issues. When a CTW is established by an FO, follow-up and training would
be carried out by SOs supported by the ASOs. They would assist FOs in electing/appointing the necessary
officers and assign specific responsibilities to FO members, open bank accounts, and provide training in
book-keeping. preparing and implementing water distribution and maintenanlce schedules, amid dispute
resolutioii technilques. The ASOs would make regular visits to provide support in the post-transition
period until the FO begins to function satisfactorily. ASOs would also monitor the performance of the
FOs. The work of SOs would be supervised by Social Coordinators (SCs). The SCs would also be
supported by a technical team (Technical Coordinator, Irrigation Engineer and Agricultural Economist)
and a survey team (Irrigation Engineer, Agricultural Economist and two surveyors). The CDSG would
have its main office in Lahore and four field offices (Chart 2).
3.13          Under the community development program, the project would provide support for about

445 staff years for social mobilization field staff. Of these, about 225 staff years would be for social
