hanidbook. The project would also support one week training of FOs and farmlers' leaders.
3.14   Redeployment of STWs Staff (Base Cost US$8.2 million).  The staff presently employed for
O&M of STWs would have to be redeployed as the transfer of public tubewells proceeds. Presently about
7,100 Governmelt employees are employed for the O&M of these tubewells (Annlex 2, Table 4). Of
these, about 1,000 (14 percent) who have more thani 25 years of service and are eligible for full pension
at retirement, are likely to take retirement during restructuring of PID. The project would support a
training program for about 3,100 staff (44 percent). After taking the training they can be redeployed in
other branchies of the Government or find employment in the private sector. About 3,000 staff would need
to be offered attractive incentives if they are to voluntarily leave PlD's service. IDA would provide funds
for the training program.  However, the Government would finance from  its own resources
retirement/severanice benefits for the employees leaving the Government service. The Government would
make use of skilled humani resources development specialists for the implementationi of the staff re-
deploymenit program  and for counselling and providing assistance and advice to the STWs staff.
Assurance were obtained that GOPunjab wouldJfinalize the training program and severance packages by
Decemnber 31, 1996 in consultation with the STWs staff  The GOPunjab would make provisions in its
operating b7udget/for retirement/severance benefits (as appropriate) for the staff whose employment would
cease cduring the following fiscal year and ensure that such benefits are made available to the eligible
stafjf: The statf receiving severance benefits would not be re-hired within a period of seven years from the
dlate theY cease to be emnployed by the Governnment.
Development of Groundwater Regulatory Framework (Base Cost US$2.8 million)
3.15         In Punljab, groundwater extractions have been increasinig consistenltly, as a result the falling
water tables in some fresh groundwater areas is a matter of considerable concerin. Over exploitation
threatenis the quality of the resource --lower level aquifers are generally more saline, and significant
differences between fresh and saline areas induce intrusion of saline water. As the rate of groundwater
use is approaching its potential availability, determination of groundwater rights (at individual and
comm1iulnity level) and regulation of its use are essential for sustainable and equitable use of this common
resoLirce.  The project would support the development as well as implementationi of a regulatory
framework. It would strengthen capacity for intensive monitoring in critical areas, develop capacity for
determining safe yields of aquifers and allocation of groundwater rights, as well as develop and support
for the institutionial setip for implementing groundwater regulations in critical areas. Detailed program
for the development and implementationi of regulatory framework is provided il Annex 5.
3.16          Presently, the SCARPs Monitorinig Organization (SMO) of WAPDA  monitors the
grounidwater aquifers, especially in the SCARP areas. SMO's capacity for country wide monitoring would
be strengthenied under the proposed National Drainage Program. The proposed project would strengthen
Pull jab's capacity for intensive monitorinig of the areas identified as critical for groundwater control on
the basis of SMOs general monitoring results. In addition to the groundwater monitoring database,
simulation  models would be employed in determining the safe yield of aquifers and its allocation by
distributary, watercourse and individual level. Detailed rules for control and licensing, as well as the
methodology and institutional setup would be developed. The development work would be completed in



- 21 -
first three years of the project, while the institutional setup for enforcemenit of regulation in critical areas
would be put in place during the remaining two years. As part of developing effective groundwater
regulation strategy, a few pilots would be implemented before refining the regulation procedures. The
objectives of the pilots would be to test and evolve most effective methods for groundwater managemenit,
assessment of the extent of regulation required, effectiveness of allocation and distribution of grounldwater
rights, and roles of the regulating and monitoring agencies, surface water and drainage agencies, the
farmers and their organizationis, local governmeits and NGOs in implementationi of the grounidwater
regulations. For development of a regulatory framework, the project would provide support for about 280
staff monithis of' internationally and nationially recruited specialists.  In addition assistance uould be
provided for support staff and installatioii of moniitoriig equipmenit. and computers.  Funding is also
ilided for about 250 staff monithis along withi support staff for the institutional setup to be established
for entorcing the regulationis. The project would also support training in groundwater modelinig and
regulation. and visits to othier countries whiere such measures have been implemented.
Improvement of Irrigation Facilities in Fresh Groundwater Areas (Base Cost US$33.6 million)
3.17    Watercourse Renovations (Base Cost US$30.5 million).  Watercourses generally command
between 75 to 200 ha anid serve about 25 to 70 farmers.  The communal watercourse (sarktiri khal)
represent about 20 percent of W/C lengthi with the remaininig 80 percent being farm  branchies anid field
ditches. With closure ot' STWs the water flow rate in W/Cs as well as pattern of flows would change
sign ificantly"v. Depending upon the location of CTW, the flow would be reduced to hialf in miost W/Cs.
Therelore, the W/Cs svould have to be remodelled to convey the new water flows efficiently and equitably.
3. 18           The communal W/C whicih conveys a large portion of the irrigation water would be
realigined and rebuilt with clean compacted soil to improve coniveyance of the canal as well as CTW  water.
Precast outlets (pIwcc  nlaC''cus) would be installed to reduce channel deterioration and water loss, arid to
improve water control. The critical sections of communal W/C prone to speedy deterioration would be
lhied with bricks or three-piece precast concrete slabs.29  Normally, head reaches of W/Cs. havilng
maximum  flows aiid hydraulic head wvould be Ihied. However, other reaches having excessive water
losses because of elevation, soil conditioiis. chronic sedimentation problems, and higih traffic areas around
villages and alonig the roads would also be eligible for pucca lining. The Iining would not exceed 15
percent of the total length of communal W/CI  lowever, greater flexibility would be allowed to FOs in
selecting the sections for Iining. Culverts would be installed at major crossings, and a limited nuimber of
check/drop strictures, animiial wallos/buffalo baths and laundry sites would be provided as required.
3.19            The project would only support renovation of W/Cs on which CTWs are installed. There
are about 5,266 W/Cs in the project area, however, the project would support renovation of about 1,500
W/Cs in the FGjW  areas. W/C renovationi would be undertaken after FO have successfully operated the
CTW  for at least one year. FOs would provide unskilled and skilled labor (masons) costs. They would
contribute at least 30 percent of the material costs up-froiit and 45 percenit of the material cost would be
payable in six histalniiients over a three year period (Annex 2 Table 5). FOs could use the income from
sale of CTW  water to finaiice cash contributions. The SOs would provide assistance in deterniining
appropriate sales price for CTW  water in order to fund cash contributions; and training in collection,
keep ing separate records and operation of bank accounts.
The STNs generalls pump three time the discharge from canals in the W/Cs. With the closure of STW and without CTW at the head
ot the WIC the discharge will be reduced to one third in most cases, furthermore, the pattern of flows would differ depending upon
the location as well as number of CTWs on a watercourse.
'    The liing will be a rectangular double-brick masonry wall (23 cm) and a brick masonry bed (7 cm) plastered inside and on top of
the wall. Three-piece precast slabs. costing about the same, could be a substitute where good quality bricks are difficult to hioid.

3.22   Rehabilitation of Canal Structures (Base Cost US$3.1 million).    With the closure of STWs,
a large numilber of farmers in the project area would rely on canal water alone until the CTWs become

physical obstructions in the delivery of canal water. This would include repair of distributary gates,
rehabilitationi of diversion structures, desilting and improving critical sections of the canial and other
adjustments to enable delivery of authorized disclharge. Before closing STWs in ain area, a survey would
be caried out and a plaii 'Aould be prepared for rehabilitation of the distribution system. These works
would not cover complete remodelling of the distributaries and should cost below Rs 200 per acre (fiscal
near 1995). The works would be identified and proposed by Social Mobilization Field Teams and the PID
durinig tile project implemenitation. The canal lining consultants would review and prepare detailed designs
for the rehabilitation works in consultations with the FOs and Social Mobilization Field Teams.
Prevention of Saline Groundwater Intrusion (Base Cost US$24.7 million)
3.23          Even in tile FGW SCARP areas the groundwater quality varies over a wide range witlh
fresh and saline areas interminigled at some locations. SCARP 11 have a large saline zone where the water



- 23 -
table is higher than the adjoining fresh groundwater areas and saliie intrusions are occurring. The project
would support measures to control spreading of the saline groundwater aquifer iito fresh areas. This
would be achieved by rehabilitating and improving the drainage facilities in the saline areas bordering the
freshwater aquifers. as well as by reducing drainage requirements through preventive measures and by
improving delivery efficiencies of canals and watercourses.
3.24   Improvement of Drainage Facilities (Base Cost US$0.2 million).   In SCARP 11, about 35
saline tubewells (with a pumping capacity of 2 cusecs). would be installed to replace the pumpage of
existing drainage tubewells whichi have either deteriorated to a very low capacity or have been abandoned.
These drainage wells would be installed in the vicinity of Shah Jiwana and Ara units of SCARP-II, and
effluent would be pumped into existing surface drains. Bordering Shall Jiwana unit, the replacement
tubewells would be provided along Faqarian-Sillanlwali drain (8 tubewells) and Sobagha drain (12
tubewells); and in Ara unit along lower Raniwah drain (15 tubewells). This capacity, together with
proposed preventive measures, would be sufficient to balance recharge and extractions and maintain the
grounidwater tables at an appropriate level in the saline areas bordering with FGW, and help prevent their
spread.
3.25           While the O&M of all STWs has generally been plagued by problems arising from poor
management and budgetary shortfalls, the salile STWs are the worst hit, primarily because, unlike the
fresh groundwater STWs. farmers are not interested in their O&M. Most of the saline tubewells are either
permaniently closed or in highily deteriorated conditioni. To avoid a similar fate of the saline STWs
proposed to be rehabilitated or replaced in the SCARP 11 area, the O&M of these saline STWs would be
contracted out to the private sector under "performance contracts". In addition efforts would be made
throughi tihe social mobilization teams to involve farmers in watch and ward, monitoring of hours of
operation and general O&M of these tubewells.
3.26   Canal Improvements (Base Cost US$9.2 million).  In addition to rehabilitation of drainage
facilities, water conservation measures (by improving the delivery efficiency of canals and watercourses)
would be used for controlling the groundwater tables and reducing the drainage requirements in saliie
areas at the interface with the fresh groundwater areas. About 26 channels in SCARP 11 with capacity
upto about 100 cusecs (2.8 m3/sec) capacity would be provided with improved water control structures
as well as lining where found appropriate30 (Annex 2. Table 7). All of the chaniels selected il SCARP
11 area are supplied by Lower Jehlum Canal. The total length of the liied sections would be about 180
kim. Several of these chanlnels cross a depression running parallel to the branch canal therefore their
middle section is significanitly highier than the ground level. Breaches often occur at these sections
crossing the depression resulting in significant loss in water and crop failures in the tail. Lining these
reaches would result in water savings as well as improved distribution of water towards the tail.
3.27           The liied section would be designed to carry the actual discharge, and adequate freeboard
would be provided for safe operation. Moghas would be remodelled, canal structures would be improved,
and control structures would be provided to meet water management requirements. The lining would
conisist of concrete placed on compacted subgrade. Trapezoidal sections with side slope of 1:1 for up to
0.85 cumecs and 1.5:1 for higher discharge with non-silting velocities would be used. Parabolic sections
would be considered for small size sections (less than 0.5 cumecs).
3.28           PID would consult the farmers benefiting from these improvement at the start of the
project. The channels on which farmers agree to pay at least 5 percent of the capital costs of works up-
front, and 50 percent of the capital cost over a period of 8 years would be considered for improvements.
'" Seepage tests would be carried out before finalizing the canal lining designs.



- 24 -
IPreference wouild be given to those channels where farmiers would make higher up-fronit contributions.
3.29           These improvemiients wvould reduce thc losses significanitly and, as the area is uliderlain
by SGA'. would coniserve valuable sulface water. Improvemiient in ~Nater conitrol and remaodellinig of
tnuoghav would reCSlt in an overall increase in water use efficiency and equitable water delivery to
watercourses, especially towards the tail-end reaches of the canals.

IV. COST ESTIMATES, FINANCING AND DISBURSEMENTS
A. Project Costs Estimates
4.1                 Total Project costs are estimated at US$104.8  million (Rs. 4,139  million) equivalent,
includinig  physical and price contingenicies, and US$10 millioni (Rs. 389 million) in local taxes.  The
foreigii excliange componenlt is estimated at US$10.2 milliol (Rs. 398 million), or about 10 percent of
project costs.  Project cost summmaries are given  in Tables 4.1  and 4.2 and detailed  costs are given  in
Aninex 7.  Project base costs are expressed in April 1996 prices (negotiations period).  Base costs were
derived from  detailed cost analysis of recent works undertalken in ongoinig projects and market surveys.
The cost of tubewells are based  on  field  surveys anid  market prices of equipment.  The watercourse
renovationi  costs are  based  oii similar works carried  out in  PuLnjab  under oin-going  on-farm   water
maniagemilenit projects. The drainage improvemenits and canal lining costs are based on average quantities
and recenit contracts for similar works. Similarly. velicles and equipment costs, and consultancy services
costs are based oni recent contracts for comparable services in Punjab and Pakistan.  The cost estimates
include physical contingenicies applied to the base costs at the rates of 10 percent for canal lining and
drainage civil works.

B. Project Financing
4.3           Table 4.4 shows the proposed project financing plan.  Farmers' Organizations (30
percent), GOPunjab/GOP (16 percent), and IDA (54 percent) would jointly finance the project. FOs
would provide about Rs. 1,256 million (US$31.8 million), of which roughly Rs. 657 million (US$16.8
million) would be in civil works and materials and Rs. 599 million (US$15 million) in skilled and
unskilled labor. IDA would provide a credit of SDR 38.5 million (US$56 million, equivalent to Rs.
2,220 million), and GOPunjab/GOP the remaining Rs 663 million (US$17 million). IDA's credit would
finance 54 percent of project costs, including 100 percent of foreign exchange costs and 48 percent of
local costs. IDA's financing is about 77 percent of the project costs excluding FOs share, and 89 percent
excluding duties and taxes.  GOPunjab/GOP would provide its share of project finances to the

implementing agency out of annual budget appropriations. The GOP (federal Ministry of Finance) would
allocate the project credit funds to GOPunjab under standard arrangement for development assistance to
the provinces. Full approval by the Executive Committee of the National Economic Council (ECNEC)
of the project s Planining Commission Form No. I (PC-I) and sanctioning of project expenditures would
be a condition of credit effectiveness.

C. Procurement
4.4           Procurement arrangements are sunimarized in Table 4.5.  Civil works for canal
improvements (item le) would be procured through a single contract following international competitive
bidding (ICB) in accordance with the Bank guidelines. Civil works for drainage works (item Id) would
be procured through a single contract following national competitive bidding (NCB). Vehicles and
equipment (including office furniture) for all components of the project (item 2) would be procured by
PMU through ICB as a single bid package with sub-packages consisting of homogenous items. However,
out of the total, office, survey and flow measuring equipment and furniture (item 2) estimated to cost
US$1.000 equivalent or less per contract, up to an aggregate amount not exceed US$50,000 equivalent,
may be procured under contracts awarded on the basis of national shopping procedure.
4.5           FOs would procure CTW equipment following commercial practices of private sector
(item la). There is a variety of equipment available in the market. W/C construction material (item lb)
will be procured through NCB acceptable to the Bank. All skilled (masons) and unskilled labor would
be provided by FOs. Small works for rehabilitation of canal structures in FGW (item Ic), widely
scattered over the project area, would be procured through NCB acceptable to the Bank. Procurement
exceeding US$200,000 equivalent for equipment, vehicles, and for works would be subject to prior
review by the Bank. The Bank's Standard Bidding Documents (BSBDs) for procurement of ICB works
and goods would be used following Bank's guidelines of January, 1995. The Consultants for various
project components would be selected following Bank Guidelines for use of consultant.

Special Account and Retroactive Financing
4.8           ro facinitate IDA disbursements, PMU would open a special account for IDA project
funds with the National Bank of Pakistan with an authorized allocation of US$3.0 million representing
reimbursable portion of estimated expenditures for four months. The authorized allocation shall be
limited to an amount equivalent of US$1.0 million until the aggregate disbursements from the credit are
equal to or exceed the equivalent of SDRl1.0 million. This account would be used for all local and
foreign expenditures of all categories. Only large expenditures, more than 20 percent of the Special
Account, would be submitted directly to IDA for payment without using special account funds. PMU
would submit applications for replenishment of the account to IDA at monthly intervals, or whenever the
amount to be replenished exceeds 20 percent of the authorized allocation. The project completion date
would be June 30, 2001, and Closing date of the Credit will be December 31, 2001.
4.9           To expedite project start-up, IDA would finance retroactively project expenditures
incurred after September 30, 1995 to the date of Credit signing, up to a limit of SDR 200,000 equivalent,
provided the procurement procedures acceptable to IDA were followed. Expenditures on vehicles,
equipment, consultancy services, incremental staff salaries and operating expenditures, training, other
preparatory expenditures would be eligible.

E. Project Accounts and Audit
4.10          Project accounts will be maintained by PMU. The accounts would identify all project
transactions on an on-going basis, including contributions from FOs, GOPunjab/GOP, and IDA.
Assurances vere obtainedfrom GOP, and GOPunjab that the project accounts, including Special Account,
would be maintained and audited annually in accordance with sound auditing standards consistently
applied by indepentdent and qualified auditors acceptable to IDA. The auditors would issue separate
opinions on the project accounts, disbursementsfrom the special account, and SOEs and supporting
documents used as a basis for disbursementsfrom the credit. PMU would provide project accounts to
its auditors unaudited within three months after each fiscal year (by September 30). and would submit an
audited report of project accounts to IDA withlin six months (December 31).
V. ORGANIZATION, MANAGEMENT AND COST RECOVERY
A. The Roles of Implementing Agencies
5.1          The project would be implemented by a Project Management Unit in the Planning and
Development (P&D) Department, PID. PAD and FOs in collaboration with WAPDA. The existing
Provincial Policy Committee (PPC) and the Project Coordination Committees (PCC) established under
the ongoing Second SCARP Transition Project would provide policy guidance, assist with inter-
Departmental coordination, and hold periodic reviews of progress at the provincial and field levels,
respectively. The proposed organization is shown in Charts I and 2, and the roles of implementing
agencies are described below. The PMU, PPC and PCC would be maintained with composition and TOR
acceptable to IDA until the project completion.
The Project Management Unit (PMU)
5.2          The PMU would have minimal staff only for the duration of the project and it would be
supported mainly by the consultants. PMU would be composed of a full time Project Director (PD), two
ex-officio Members (one each from the Punjab Irrigation Department (PID) and the Directorate General
of Water Management (DGWM), and three Directors as well as support staff. The Directors would be
recruited from the open market or supplied by a management consulting firms on fixed-term contracts
for the duration of the project. The PD would be a senior official of the P&D Board of the level of
Member of the Board. The PMU would be supported by a multi-disciplinary team of consultants who
would provide project implementation assistance (PIA) in project management, community mobilization,
engineering design and construction supervision, and development of groundwater regulatory framework
(terms of reference are in Annex 3). The PMU would also employ an independent organization or
consulting firm to carry out project impact evaluation studies (PIES) (TOR are in Annex 6). The PMU
would be responsible for:
a.    overall project management and coordination;
b.    development of groundwater regulatory framework;
c.    community development for formation of FOs and establishment of CTWs, including
processing and disbursement of financial assistance to FOs;
d.    performance monitoring and evaluation, including development of an MIS for the project;
e .    impact evaluation studies;
f.    planning, budgeting, maintenance of consolidated project accounts, including the IDA
Special Account, and ensuring timely submission of audit reports; and



- 32 -
g.      procurement of vehicles and consulting services required for the project.
GOPunjab would delegate necessary administrative and financial powers3' to the PD PMU to carry out
these responsibilities. The responsibilities of various members of the PMU would be as follows:
5.3     The PD  PMU  would be responsible for overseeing overall project implementation.  Main
responsibilities would, inter alia, include:
a.      managing overall project implementation and coordinating with PID and the DGWM;
b.      acting as the chairperson of the PCC and as the secretary of the PPC and reporting

deviations to the PPC in a timely manner.

(Community Development).

5.6    The Director [Regulation] would be a groundwater specialist with experience in legal aspects
of groundwater management and regulation, as well as monitoring and evaluation of groundwater
projects. The main responsibilities of this positioni would include:
a.     Managing and supervising the PIE consultants, and the PIA consultants team responsible
for developing groundwater regulatory framework;
b.     developing and maintaining a database on groundwater table and quality in different areas
of Punjab;
c.     classifying aquifers in Punjab in different Groundwater Management areas;
d.     developing a database consisting of basic information for each management area and
updating it on a continuous basis;
e.     identifying in each management area the sources of aquifer recharge as well as
extractions;
f.    identifying critical areas requiring extensive management;
g.     developing and calibrating groundwater models to simulate the groundwater behavior in
the long-term, including wet and dry cycles;
I.     developing proposals for establishing rights to groundwater by distributary command
area, watercourse command (chak) and by farm;
i.     estimating safe aquifer capacity by watercourse command, as well as by distributaries;
j.     developing proposals for a program for licensing the groundwater tubewells with the
objective of achieving safe installed capacity;
k.     developing proposals for detailed institutional setups for groundwater management in each
management area, according to the type and the severity of the problem; and
I.     developing proposals for new  legislation that may be required for facilitating
implementation of the regulatory framework.

5.9    NGO's Role.   Currently there are no NGOs in Punjab with experience in establishing FOs and
capable of assuming full responsibility for managing an extensive social mobilization program needed for
the project. However, some local service NGOs have working relationships with local community based
organizations (CBOs) and/or local activists who could help mobilize farmer participation in the project.
Wherever feasible, the PMU will seek cooperation of local service NGOs and CBOs either directly or
through the PIA consultants to assist with community mobilization. In selecting NGOs/CBOs, it would
be ensured that they: (a) are legally registered and maintain accounts that are audited annually; (b) are
involved in rural development as their main objective and they are not affiliated with any political
organization; (c) have adequate number of field staff conversant with local languages and customs, and
have good knowledge and understanding of the socio-economic conditions in the area; and (d) are
supportive of the project objectives and can work in harmony with the rest of the project team.
5.10   FO's Role.    FO's would play a critical role in project implementation. They would be eligible
for receiving project assistance for establishing CTWs if they have membership of at least one-third of
the farmers within the command of an STW. FOs wishing to establish CTWs would be required to
register themselves as a water users association or its sub-group (e.g. a tubewell committee) under the
Punjab Water Users Associations Ordinance of 1981. The FOs at the CTW level would, inter alia, havc
the following responsibilities: (a) interacting with the field teams of PMU and/or OFWM staff to prepare

work plans for transfer of STWs; (b) determining the best option for replacing STW pumpage, in terms

members' share ot capital, operation and maintenance and replacement costs; (e) maintaining capital and
O&M accounts and log books; (f) improving water management in the watercourse command; and (g)

5.11          The watercourses with working FOs where the majority of farmers agree to cooperate
for improving the watercourse and managing water deliveries at the chak level would be considered for
improvement.  In the fresh groundwater areas these FOs would qualify for project assistance for
watercourse improvements after they have effectively operated and maintained their CTWs for a period
of one year. During this period they would: (i) elect their executive committee, including a Chairman,
a General Secretary, and a Treasurer; (ii) improve distribution of canal as well as CTW water, and the
coimnmunlity and farm channels: (iii) open a bank account, generate funds for up-front contribution for
W/C improvement, from sales of CTW water and contributions from FO members; (iv) collect farmers'
shares for repayment of material costs to the government; and (v) participate in improving water
distribution at the distributary level, and collecting contributions for the capital cost of improving
distributaries.
5.12   WAPDA's Role.        WAPDA would provide electric connections to FOs who are close to
electric supply and decide to install electric CTWs. Where STWs are converted to CTWs as such under
the transferinode, WAPDA will transfer the title of electrical connection to FOs against normally needed
security deposits. It would also assist FOs in removing unnecessary electrical wiring and equipment.
B. Project Coordination
Project Coordination Committee
5.13                 At the project level, an existing Project Coordination Committee (PCC) would
be responisible for ensuring coordination among various agencies and facilitating project implementation.
The PCC would be chaired by PD of PMU and have the following members: (i) from PID: SE's of
concernedJ SCARP, canal and drainage Circles; (ii) from the PAD: the Area Coordinator of OFWM; and
the Deputy Director Agriculture; (iii) a representative of District Administration; and (iv) representatives
of selected FOs. The PCC would meet once every month. The PMU would also act as its secretariat.
Provincial Policy Committee
5.14                 The existing Provincial Policy Committee (PPC) would provide policy guidance
and steer project implementation by providing inter-agency coordination at the highest level. The PPC
would be chaired by the Chairman P&D Board and the Secretaries of Irrigation, Agriculture, and Finance
would be its members. The PD of PMU would act as its secretary. The PPC would meet at least every
six months. or whenever issues requiring resolution are submitted for its consideration. The main
functions of the PPC would be to: (i) resolve issues not settled by the PCC; (ii) ensure provision of
adequate budgetary allocations for project implementation as well as operation and maintenance (O&M);
(iii) ensure adequacy and continuity of PMU staff; (iv) ensure that the project complies with legal
covenants; and (v) recruit and supervise consultants to carry out full supervision of the project twice a
year, independently of the PMU.

C. Implemnentation and Supervision
5.15   Project Start-up Activities.   The project would be implemented over a five year period
(FY1997-FY2001). The project works require relatively simple engineering designs for watercourse
improvements and canal lining, that are prepared routinely by PID, OFWM in close cooperation with
farmners. Similarly. farmers in Punjab have considerable experience in installing as well as operating and
maintaining tubewells. Drilling facilities. tubewell equipment (pumps, prime movers, screens, pipes,
etc.) and repair workshops are available locally at the village level in most areas. Project activities are,
therefore, expected to pick up as soon as the community mobilization activities are initiated. Staff for
implementationi of canal, drainage and W/Cs improvement works are already in position. Immediately
following appraisal in June 1995, GOPunijab notified the establishment of the PMU and assigned part-
time staff Assurances would be obtained at during negotiations that recruitment of full time core staff
*for the PMU (U'ull time Project Director and Directors and support staff) would be completed by September
30, 1996. The acting PD PMU has initiated procurement of consultants and equipment. GOPunjab have
approved a PC-lI that would authorize project start-up expenditures. IDA would retroactively finance
eligible start-up expenditures incurred after June 1995. The project PC-I would be submitted to PDWP
for before negotiations and its approval be a condition for the Board presentation. The implementation
plan for various project components and key performance indicators are given in Annex 9.
5.16   Project Launch Workshop. To facilitate project start-up, the PMU in collaboration with PID
and PAD, would hold a project launcil workshop sooIn after Credit signing. The workshop would, inter
atia, familiarize project staff (as well as officials of conicerned implementing and cooperating agencies)
with the project objectives, components, implementation schedule and institutional arrangements,
procurement and disbursement procedures, and monitoring and evaluation aspects of the project.
5.17   Procurement Workshops.    Several procurement workshops would be conducted to provide
assistance in the procurement planning, the use of Banks Standard Bidding document, preparation of bid
documents, evaluation of bids and award of contracts. These workshops would be organized to train local
staff in procuremenit with the aim to enhance general capability in this area and, in addition to the project
staff will be open to staff of other agencies involved in procurement under large projects.
5.18   Annual Work Plans and Progress Reporting.       The MIS of the PMU would be designed
to meet the monitoring and reporting requirements of all project components. The PIA consultants would
design an appropriate MIS system to track key project indicators and develop an "issues and actions
oriented" progress reporting format acceptable to the PMU and IDA. The PMU. with inputs from PID
and PAD, would also prepare detailed annual plans/work programs and budgets, as well as requests for
allocation of funds in the federal/provincial annual development programs. The annual plans would duly
take into account feedback from project impact evaluation studies. The PID and PAD would submit
monthly progress reports to the PMU. The PMU in turn would integrate them and submit periodic
progress reports covering all project components to the P&D Board, PID, PAD and IDA.  The PMU
would also periodically prepare working papers for the PCC and PPC nmeetings. Assurances would be
obtained that by March 31 each year, the PMU would submit to IDA for its review and approval, a draft
Alnnual Plan, includinig work program and the proposed budgetary allocation for the following fiscal year.
5.19   Enhanced Governument Supervision of the Project.  In line with the decision taken during the
1995 Country Portfolio Performance Review (CPPR). the project provides resources to support enhanced
project supervision by GOPunjab. The P&D Board would hire, independently of the PMU, services of
specialists in various fields to carry out detailed supervision of the project on behalf of GOPunjab from
time to time (cn average two times a year) and submit detailed suipervision reports. TOR of these
specialists as well as their reports would be shared with IDA.

5.20   IDA Supervision.    IDA would supervise the project twice a year on average. 11 required
supervision would occur more frequently during the first two years to help steer it through the start-up
stage. The project launch and procurement workshops would also be organized during this period. The
IDA supervision would comprise of one mzain supervision mission annually in the second semester of the
fiscal year, supplemented by a shorter mission in the first semester, and correspondence ani( clearances
as needed. Intensity of supervision missions would be reduced after the project is successfully past this
start-up stage. and a regime of enhaniced Government supervision has been established. Intenisive IDA
supervision during the start-up stage is justified given the nature of the project which, unlike convenitional
irrigation projects, is "software" intensive rather thani "hardware" intensive. Community mobilization
is a crucial elemenit of the project. This is a relatively new area in irrigation projects in Pakistan and
requires greater supervision inputs by IDA. Annex 9 contains IDA's proposed supervision plan. and
details of staff inputs and skill mix requirements for supervision missions. IDA supervision would be
facilitated by the proposed arrangements for enhanced Government supervision.

D. Operation, Maintenance and Cost Recovery
Operation and Maintenance
, 922         FOs would be responsible for O&M of tubewells as well as watercourses improved under
the project, while PID would be responsible for O&M  of distributary canals and drainage facilities.
Punjab annual O&M  budget for irrigation and drainage systems is about Rs. 3.9 billion (in FY 1996,
Table 5.1). The O&M expenditure is lower than the requirements. The gap between the requirements
and O&M expenditures varies over years. In 1994-95 it was about 13 percent compared to around 30
percent in 1993-94. Presently. O&M requirement of public tubewells represent about 40 percent of total
budget requirements for O&M of the irrigation and drainage systerms in the Punjab. With divestment of
STWs, GOPunjab's lhabi,ity for O&M would decrease significantly (Table 5.1), thus improving its
capacity to provide adequate budget to PID for O&M  of provincial irrigation and drainage systems.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that GOPunjab will discuss wvith IDA by February, 15 of
each fiscal vear the lev'el offunding requiredfor operation and .iaintenance of the irrigation and drainage
svsrenis in Puninb.for the following fiscal vear, and basea on mutually agreed funding levels, provide
adequate budgetary allocations.
5.23          While these measures would provide relief in the short-term, it would still be difficult to
maintain the enhanced level of O&M expenditures on the surface irrigation system in the long-run
because: (i) there would be competing demands for funds from other sectors; and (ii) the revenues
collected from water charges are less than 40 percent of O&M expenditures. Therefore, for the long-
term self-sustainability of the irrigation and drainage, GOPunjab would initiate implementation of pilot
projects for decentralizing management at the canal command level and enhancing beneficiaries
participation in the O&M of the system at distributary/minor level. To this end, the GOPunjab would
appraiseJiirst pilot project during fiscal year 1996-97 and start its implemnentation by July 1, 1998.

and mutually agree with IDA, by February 15, each fiscal year, the level of funding required for O&M,
and provide such funds for the following fiscal year (para 5.22); and (c) enhance cost recovery so as to
achieve by July 1, 1998 and then after maintain full recovery of O&M costs through improving revenue
collection as well as increasing water charges.

Recovery of Capital Costs
5.25          The capital cost recovery arrangements would vary for different types of works undertaken
under the project. For SCARP Tubewells transferred as such to FOs, GOPunjab would charge them Rs.
10,000 for each STW. GOPunjab would provide a one time cash incentive of Rs. 20,000 to FOs for
installing modified CTW and Rs. 30,000 for installation of new CTW. These amounts represent about 50
percent of ihe cost of an average modified CTW and 45 percent of a new CTW. FOs would meet the
remaining capital costs. For watercourse improvements, FOs would provide skilled (masonis) and
unskilled labor, and a minimum of 30 percent of the materials costs up-front.  FOs total up-front
contributioni would thus be about 53 percent of the capital cost in FGW areas and 49 percent in saline
areas. In addition, FOs would pay back 45 percent of materials cost in six installments over a three-year
period. This results in 77 percent recovery in net present value terms, assuming an aniual interest rate
of 12 percent. Similarly, for distributaries and minors lined under the project, the beneficiaries would
contribute 5 percent of the capital cost up-front and pay another 50 percent of capital cost in installments
over a period of eight years. This results in 36 percent recovery in net present value term taking an
aniual interest rate of 12 percent. Assurance were obtained from GOPunjab for implemlentation of the
proposed capilal cost recovery plan.



- 39 -
5.26           About 1,500 watercourses would be improved in fresh groundwater areas and about 500
in saline areas. The saline watercourses involve some exteinalities as reduction in drainage and salinity
also benefits to the farmers outside the command of the watercourses. Similarly, the canal lining works
are proposed to be carried out in areas with saline groundwater for reducing the drainage and salinity
problems and protecting fresil groundwater at the interface of fresh and saline groundwater areas. The
farmers at the tail of the lined canal would have additional water supply but a signiificant amount of
benefits would be outside the command area. Considering these externalities and the current practice of
very low and in some cases no cost recovery of capital works in irrigation and drainage sector, the
proposed capital cost recovery plan is a considerable break-through in Pakistan. In case of watercourse
improvements 77 percent recovery is a significant improvement over the current practice of recovering
only 47 percent. Similarly, in case of canal lininig works for whlich presently there is no cost recovery.
A significanit amount of up-front recover) would enhlance the real recovery levels while ensuring
participationi of farimers before the carrying out the improvemiienit works.
5.27           The capital cost recovery levels are well witliln the farmers capacity to pay. Tlley would
pay about Rs 133. Rs. 253 and Rs. 493 per ha for CTWs installed by STWs transfer, modification and
for new diesel ttibewells respectively. These cost represenit about 2, 4 and 8 percent of net annual income
from  crops of large farms and slightly less for small farms. The farmers could finance watercourse
renovationis either throughi sale of CTW water or through cash contributions. A sale price of about Rs 47
per hour (Rs. 375 per acre feet) would be enoughi to provide required funds for initial contribution and
ahout Rs. 17 per hour (Rs. 134 per AF) for installments during remaining three years. These are much
below the market price of Rs. 60 per hour for 1.5 cusec tubewells. Alternatively, the beneficiaries could
finanice these works through cash contributioni. Average zontributions are shown in Table 5.2. These
represenit a small portion of present annual farn income from crops.

VI. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION, MARKETING, PRICES AND FARM INCOMES
A. Agricultural Production
6.1    The project would increase agricultural production over about 1.3 million ha, of which 1. I millionl
ha have fresh and remaining 0.2 million ha has saline groundwater. In these areas. crop production would
iicrease with project due to improved water supply and conjunctive use of surface and groundwater,
thereby helping to alleviate the deficit crop water requirements, expand the cropped area, and improve soil
conditions.
Water Availability
6.2            The condition and effectiveness of STWs would continue to decline, as it has been in the
past, and their delivery of water would likely fall to virtually nothinig over the next ten years. In the fresh
groundwater areas this would mean an overall reduction in irrigation supplies by about 26 percent. In
saline areas which depend on canals only, the irrigation water delivery at the farm level would contilnue
to decline due to deteriorating infrastructure. A significant number of large farmers already have PTWs.
After closure of STWs others who can afford them might install PTWs to replace the lost supply. This
would result in excessive installed capacity than safe aquifer yields, higher investment costs and increased
incidence of secondary soil salinity. Small farmers, on the other hand, would not be able to install PTWs.
Some would purchase PTW water from the large farms, and some small farmers, especially with
fragmented land holding, would not be able to get PTW water and their groundwater use would reduce.
With the closure of STWs the distribution of canal water would be affected adversely towards the tail of
the watercourses. The total amount of water available to small farms would fall, with a consequent
reduction in yields and cropping intensities. There are several limitations to distributing PTW water over
wider areas.:  These include: location of PTW; absence of a distribution network to other farms; higi
conveyance losses of small flows through open temporarily constructed chaninels; and difficulties in
schedulinig PTW  water during critical periods35. Although PTW  water is commonly traded among the
neighborilng farms, due to the above factors replacement of STW water with PTWs over the whole W/C
command would not be feasible without installing jointly managed and suitably located CTWs.
6.3            With the project, in areas where STWs are to be taken over by the FOs, or replaced with
modified and new CTWs, the water supply would be maintained at present levels. This, along with
improvements in the surface system, would result in an increase in aggregate water supply in the project
Recent studies show that utilization of PTWs ranges from 4 to 13 percent indicating limitation to water distributioii fromil PTWs over
a wide area.
During critical periods the PTW owners apply water to their own farms before giving to others.



- 41 -
area by over 10 percent. The timeliness of water supplies would improve due to less reliance on the
public grid as well as O&M system. The water quality would improve due to pumping from shallow
depths and better conjunctive use of surface and groundwater supplies, and reduced saline groundwater
intrusions.
Methodology of Project Impact Assessment
6.4           For the purpose of project impact analysis, it is assumed for the future base-case that after
closure of STWs the lost pumpage would be replaced with PTWs (future without project). Large farmers
would install PTWs. use water for themselves and sell water to small farmers. PTWs pumping costs are
much below the STWs but much highier thani the CTWs (Aniex 10, Table 2). It is further assumed that
there will not be any chanige in production due to transfer of STWs pumpage to PTWs or CTWs. The
project benefits arise from reduction in pumping cost with CTWs instead of PTWs, mainly due to better
utilization of the installations (future with project). In addition, the system improvements carried out in
fresh and saline areas would result in increased production mainly due to higher cropping intensities and
reduced pumnpage costs because of higher delivery efficiency (future with project and system
improvements). It is assumed that 75 percent of the farms in the project area would participate in the
project, or in other words 75 percent of the STWs pumpage would be replaced with CTWs, while farms
not participating may (or may not) replace pumpage by existing or new PTWs.  Surface system
improvements would take place in the areas/watercourses participating in the CTW program. Based on
the field experience, these assumptions are very conservative, hence the resulting benefits estimates may
be understated.
Impact on Production
6.5    Crop Yields.  Over the next ten years the crop yield in the project area would increase by about
five percent. It is assumed, however, that crop yields would be the same without or with STWs transfer
as well as with system improvement. (Annex I1, Table 2).
6.6    Cropping Intensities. Presently, average cropping intensities in different SCARP areas range
from 1 17 to 145 percent with small farms having a higher average intensity (about 140 percent) than the
large farms (121 percent). The same cropping intensity is assumed for future without or with transfer of
STWs. The cropping intensities would, however, increase by about 10 percent with surface system
improvement due to additional water availability from canals and tubewells resulting from improved
delivery efficiency (Annex 1 1, Table 3).
6.7    Cropping Patterns.   Major changes are not expected in the cropping patterns, except in areas
where surface systems are improved. In these areas, there would be a shift towards cotton and fruits in
Cotton-Wheat and Sugarcane-Wheat cropping systems, and towards Basmati, in Rice-Wheat systems.
6.8    Production.   Overall production in the future, without or with transfer of STWs, is assumed
to be the samc. However, production would increase in the systems improvement situation on the
participating farms. The increase in Basmati, Cotton, and Wheat is expected to be about 7,590, 12,770,
and 6,71 0 tons, respectively, on participating small farms and 15,150, 16,090, 4,650 tons on participating
large farms respectively (see Annex I 1, Table 5 for details).
B. Markets and Prices
6.9    Markets.      The project area, which covers some of the most productive agricultural areas in
the country, has a wetl developed transport network and there are a large number of rice, flour, sugar and



- 42 -
cotton ginniniig mills as well as milk processing plants. The Provincial Food Departmenit and the Pakistan
Agricultural Supply and Services Corporation are involved in procuremenit of rice, wheat, cotton and
sugarcane. Farmers also have easy access to a number of major agricultural markets in large towins nearby
such as Lahore, Faisalabad, SheikIlupura, Multan and Sarghoda.  The relatively small increase in
productic n would be either consumed on-farm or easily sold.
6.10   Input Supply. 'Ilhe incremenltal requirements would be readily available throughi the norimial input
supply mechanism.  Similarly, no difficulty is anticipated in supplying the very limiited increased
requirements for planting materials and pesticides.
6.11   Prices. Prices for various agricultural goods are determined by domestic supply and demanld, as
well as by Government policies with regard to interniationial trade, and taxes and subsidies. Until the
early- I 980s, agricultural output prices were kept low in order to containi the cost of living, while fertilizers
were subsidized to promote greater use. Since theni, output prices have been raised althoughi they are
generally still below international levels. Also, fertilizer subsidies are being removed and have already
been eliminated in the case of urea. Farn budget analysis is based on prevailing market prices (Aninex
10, Table 1 ) which may inciease in the future as the Government continiues to more closely align domestic
and internationial prices.
C. Farm Income
Farm Models
6.12          Farm models were developed ftor the three prevalent production systems found in the
project area: the Cotton-Wheat system representative of SCARP Ill Rangpur Ullit; the Sugarcanie-Wlheat
system representative of SCARP V, and most of SCARP 11 and Shalipur; and the Rice-Wheat system
representative of SCARP IV. Withinl each production system, models were developed for small farmers
(2.5 ha) and large farmers ( 10 ha). The small farn models represent 80 percent of farmers with less than
6 ha, who farm about 50 percent of the land; while the large farm models represent 20 percent of farmers
with more than 6 ha have the remaining 50 percent of the land. Crop budgets of major crops in financial
prices and economic returns are given in Annex I1, Table 2. In line with the prevailing situation, larger
farmers would: have higher yields; use more fertilizer; have lower cropping intenisities and employ hired
labor; and use tractors rather thanl bullocks. The farm models have been used to analyze the impact of
CTWs alone, as well as in combination with improving the surface systemiis. They are suLmiiiarized in
Aniex  I Table 3 and Table 4.
Impact on Farm Incomes
6.13          With the transfer of STWs to CTWs the cost of water for participating small farms would
be reduced by 64 percent (from Rs. 2,650 to 970) hence their net income from crops would increase by
about 8 percent. The cost of water to participating large farms would reduce by 40 percent (from Rs.
634,000 to 387,000 ) and their net incomes would increase by about 4 percent.  With system
improvements, net incomes (from crops) of participating small and large farms would increase by 9 and
10 percent respectively. Since, both small and large farmers would benefit from the project. they would
participate in the CTWs program. The detailed results of' farm11 modlels arc sho%% n in Annex I. 'I ahles

VII. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Benefits and Justification
Quantified Benefits
7. 1             As described in the preceding chapter (para 6.2 to 6.4), in the future without the project
the PTWs are expected to replace the pumpage of STWs as these become derelict. The project would
generate benefits through installation of CTWs which are a lower cost alternative to the operation of
PTWs (Annex 10, Table 2). Although there is a likelihood that agricultural production would decline
with the closure of STWs, the benefits due to avoiding this loss with the project have not been quantified.
However, the benefits due to increased agricultural production with watercourses and canals
improvements in fresh and saline groundwater areas have been quantified. The project benefit streams,
therefore, include: (a) the benefits due to reduction in pumping costs --CTWs instead of PTWs; and (b)
the benefits due to increased agricultural production from increased water supply with system
improvements in fresh and saline groundwater areas. The benefits due to reduction in waterlogging and
salinity with measures under the Prevention of Saline Groundwater Intrusion component, as well as with
the development of a groundwater regulatory framework, and benefits of pilot projects and training under
the Technical Assistance component are not included.
7.2              The analysis based on these conservative assumptions shows that the project would
generate substantial economic benefits in the short- and long-term. The cost of pumping groundwater
with STWs is about Rs. 685 million (pumping about 2.32 MAF of groundwater in June 1995 economic
prices). By closing the STWs and replacing the same pumpage with PTWs the cost of pumping would



- 44 -
reduce by Rs. 114 million. With the project, assuming 75 percent participation in the CTW program,
the cost of pumping would reduce further by Rs. 174 million at full development. The returns to closing
the STWs are extremely high and even greater by replacing those with CTWs. With surface system
improvements, the value of production would increase by about Rs. 883.4 million (Annex 11, Table 6).
The project would directly benefit some 380,000 farm families, particularly the 80 percent with small
farms. On-farm employment would increase slightly, by about 3 percent in situations with CTWs only
and about 15 percent with CTWs and surface improvements.
Other Benefits
7.3            The project would also generate substantial environmental and other benefits. The high
capital investment required to set up PTWs and anarchic development of groundwater would be avoided.
The increased availability of electricity resulting from the shut down of STWs would also contribute to
overcoming some of the chronic electricity shortages in the country. It would encourage the participatory
irrigation management and development of FOs; and would lead to forming sustainable participation of
farmers at the higher level of the canal system. The introduction of groundwater regulations would
promote and guide private investments, and ensure environmentally sustainable exploitation of
groundwater. CTWs would improve the distribution of ground and surface water. Since CTWs are
easier to nmonitor and have self-regulating characteristics, costs of monitoring and regulation would be
lower. Finally, privatization of highly inefficient STWs would substantially reduce the O&M burden on
the Government. This would allow to re-direct resources to improve O&M of canals and drainage
systems, as well as increase investments in activities where the private sector cannot take a lead, such as
off-farm drainage, large canals and inter-river transfer facilities.
B. Economic Analysis
7.4            The economic viability of the project has been tested through estimation of an economic
rate of return (ERR).  The economic value of project benefits has been calculated by evaluating
incremental outputs and inputs at economic prices and, in the case of non-traded commodities, by using
a standard conversion factor (SCF) of 0.9 (Annex 10, Tables I and 2). Economic costs have been
calculated from total project costs, including physical contingencies, with the local cost component being
adjusted by the SCF and excluding taxes and duties.
7.5            The ERR for the CTWs component is estimated at 19 percent, for the surface
improvements component the ERR is 26 percent and for the overall project 24 percent (Annex 11, Tables
7),3  As explained above, the returns on the CTWs component are due to reduction in operating and
capital cost of CTWs compared with PTWs having low utilization rates. The returns on the surface
improvements, are due to increased water supply and improved conjunctive use of surface and
groundwater resulting in increased agricultural production.
C. Enviromnental Effects
7.6            The project would have a number of positive effects on the environment. It would control
waterlogging and the rising water table in that area. By promoting conjunctive use of surface and
groundwater, it would improve water quality and reduce risks of soil salinity. Lastly, it would help
control the intrusion of saline water into the fresh groundwater aquifer. The monitoring program
Benefit build up for STWs transfer is assumed to be 5. 25. 50. 85, 100 percent starting from year 2 of the project and
5,15,35.55,75.85 and 100 percent of full development starting from project year 3 for surface system improvements.



- 45 -
supported by the project and the development of a regulatory framework for province wide groundwater
use would be a major step towards sustainability of groundwater aquifers.
7.7            The effluent from drainage facilities to be rehabilitated under the project would not be
significant, and it would be disposed in the existing drainage systems. The detailed analysis carried out
under the Sectoral Environmental Assessment --National Drainage Program studies demonstrates that the
effluent quantity and quality disposed by the systems under consideration are far below the level that
could impact downstream  adversely. The impact of pollutants from diesel tubewells, as opposed to
electrical tubewells, would be insignificant because they would be scattered over a wide area.
7.8    Land Acquisition.    There are no on-going or anticipated future resettlemenit and rehabilitation
(R&R) is-.ues related to project activities.
7.9    Program Objective Category.           The project would support IDA's poverty relief objective,
through ;ts impact on growth of income of small and marginal farmers, private sector development,
public sector management and environmental objectives.  Farmers' participation in the irrigation
management would be instituted by development of effective FOs, therefore, the project would advance
IDA's private sector development objectives as well. The public sector management objectives would
be met by rationalizing the public sector O&M  expenditures. Lastly, it would support environmental
objectives by putting in place a moniitorinig program for groundwater aquifers, developing a regulatory
framework for their use, controlling the spread of saline aquifers, and improvinig the efficiency of usinig
the water resources.
D. Risks and Sensitivity Analysis
Project Risks
7.10           There are three main risks to the project. Firstly, lower participation by the farmers
lxcause: (a' farmers may prefer to have their own PTWs; (b) the public tubewells are a source of cheap
it rigation water for many farmers, who may, especially the influential ones, pressure the Government to
stop or delay their closure; and (c) the PID staff which is presently engaged in the O&M of STWs would
become redundant with the divestment of public tubewells and may try to retard the project progress.
This risk would be minimized by: (a) launchiiig an extensive social mobilization program  for
demonstrating to the farmers the benefits of participatory irrigation management, and for developing
effective FOs; (b, providing incentives for installation of CTWs; and (c) assigning the responsibility of
privatization of tubewells to the PMU and by providing a training program and severance package for
redeployinent of PID staff. Participation in the CTWs is not mandatory, in fact it is a choice and a
superior sohluion for sustainable use of groundwater resources.
7.11           Secondly, there is risk of waterlogging and salinity in some areas when the operation of
public tubewells is stopped and replacement CTWs are not yet installed. This risks would only be in
limited project areas where water table is very shallow. This aspect would be minimized by careful
scheduling of the project activities especially the farmers support program.
7.12           The third risk relates to implementation of the investment program in a timely manner.
Apart from initial start-up delays, the GOPunjab demonstrated an improvement in the implementation of
water sector projects in the recent years. Punjab's pace for implementing Second Irrigation System
Rehabilitation Project (CR. 1 888-PAK) was considerably faster than other provinces. The implementation
of On-farm Water Management Program, consisting of small works jointly constructed by farmers and
the Government, is generally better than other projects with large civil works. Nevertheless, in order



- 46 -
to ensure timely completion, several start-up actions are being taken before project approval. PMU has
been established, the project implementation consultants have been selected, PC-I has been prepared,
recruitment of M&E consultants is underway, and bid documents for procurement of vehicles and
equipment have been finalized. In addition to these actions, the review and coordination arrangements
proposed for the project and support to PMU for building of project MIS would minimize this risk.
Sensitivity Analysis
7. 13         The project ERR is very robust and not very sensitive to variations in project costs or
benefits. The sensitivity analysis, based on the above risks, shows that it is highly unlikely that
unfavorable developments would reduce the project ERR to opportunity cost of capital (OCC) --taken as
12 percent. Even if the participation is only half of the anticipated level (37 percent), ERR of CTW
component would be equal to OCC while overall project ERR would be about 17 percent (Annex II
Table 7b). The benefits from surface system improvements would have to fall by more than 60 percent
for ERR to decline to OCC. Alternately, ERR would be equal to OCC if costs increase by about 100
percent. ERR response to different combinations of changes in costs and benefits ranging from -50 to
50 percent is shown in Annex 11, Figure 1.  Annex 11, Figure 2 shows combinations of changes in
overall costs and benefits where ERR would switch from being acceptable to falling below the OCC. A
few points fi-om this curve are given in Table 7. 1. It shows that if costs uniformly increase by 20
percent, the project's performance would still be satisfactory as long as the reduction in benefits is less
than 40 percent. Delays of one or two years in realizing benefits are not expected to affect the project's
performance significantly.

VII. Agreements and Recommendations
8.1    Assurances have been obtained from GOP and GOPunjab as appropriate that:
(a)    GOPunjab would formally close all Governrment-owned irrigation tubewells by June 30,
1998. The GOPunjab would also: (i) eliminate the provincial program for subsidizing
individually owned private tubewells in areas supplied by canal water by September 30,
1996; and (ii) initiate a study for preparation of a plan to phase out subsidies to the
individually-owned private tubewells in areas not supplied by canal water and where
private tubewells are the sole source of irrigation water, and implement such plan by
December 31, 1997 (para 3.9);
(b)    GOPunjab would finalize the training program and severance packages by December 31,
1996 in consultation with the STWs staff. The GOPunjab would make provisions in its
operating budget for retirement/severance benefits (as appropriate) for the staff whose
employment would cease during the following fiscal year and ensure that such benefits
are madit available to the eligible staff. The staff receiving severance benefits would not
be re-hired by the Government within a period of seven years from the date they cease
to be employed by the Government (para 3.14);
(c)    proper specifications and design criteria would be observed for all civil works constructed
under the project and measures would be taken to control quality of construction
acceptable to IDA (para 3.33);
(d)    PMtI would furnish proposals for staff training under the project to IDA for its prior
review and approval (para 3.35);
(e)    project accounts including Special Account, would be maintained and audited annually
in accordance with sound auditing standards consistently applied by independent and
qualified auditors acceptable to IDA. The auditors would issue separate opinions on the
project accounts, disbursements from the special account, and SOEs and supporting
documents used as a basis for disbursements from the credit. PMU would provide
project accounts to its auditors unaudited within three months after each fiscal year (by
September 30), and would submit an audited report of project accounts to IDA within six
imonths (December 31) (para 4. 10);
(f)    the PMU, PPC and PCC would be maintained with composition and TOR acceptable to
IDA until the project completion (para 5.1);
(g)    by March 31 each year, the PMU would submit to IDA for its review and approval, a
draft Annual Plan, including work program and the proposed budgetary allocation for the
following fiscal year (para 5.18);
(h)    the PMU would prepare a mid-term review report by December 31, 1998 and detailed
mid-termii review (MTR) would be carried out jointly by GOPunjab, GOP and IDA by
March 31. 1999 (para 5.21);
(i)    GOPunjab will discuss with IDA by February, 15 of each fiscal year the level of funding
required for operation and maintenance of the irrigation and drainage systems in Punjab
for the following fiscal year, and based on mutually agreed funding levels, provide



- 48 -
adequate budgetary allocations (para 5.22);
(j)    GOPunjab would initiate implementation of pilot projects for decentralizing management
at the canal command level and enhancing beneficiaries participation in the O&M of the
system at distributary/minor level. To this end, the GOPunjab would appraise first pilot
project during fiscal year 1996-97 and start its implementation by July 1, 1998 (5.23);
(k)    for recovery of capital cost of works constructed under the project the following recovery
plan would be implemented. For each SCARP tubewell transferred to the farmers as
such for operation as CTW, the Government would charge Rs. 10,000. For CTWs
installed by modifying a SCARP tubewell, the Government would provide one time
financial assistance of Rs. 20,000 and Rs. 30,000 for installation of a new CTW, while
the remaining costs would be borne by the FOs. For watercourse improvement, FOs
would contribute skilled (masons) and unskilled labor and 30 percent of the materials cost
up-front. In addition, FOs would pay back 45 percent of material costs in 6 installments
over a three-year period. Similarly, for canal lining, farmers would pay 5 percent of the
cost up-front and another 50 percent in installments over a period of eight years (para
5.25); and
(l)    the Planiing and Development Board would hire, independently of the PMU, services
of specialists in various fields to carry out detailed supervision of the project on behalf
of GOPunjab from time to time (on average two times a year) and submit detailed
supervision reports (para 5.19).
8.2    Condition of Effectiveness.   Following would be conditions of effectiveness:
(a)    full approval by the Executive Committee of the National Economic Council (ECNEC)
of the project's Planning Commission Form No. 1 (PC-l) and sanctioning of project
expenditures would be a condition of credit effectiveness (para 4.3);
(b)    the project implementation consultants are duly employed and are on location and ready
to commence operations (para 3.36); and
(c)    recruitment of full time core staff for the PMU (full time Project Director, Directors and
Support staff)(para 5.15);
8.3           With the above assurances and conditions, the project would be suitable for an IDA credit
of SDR 38.5 million (US$56.0 million equivalent) on standard terms with a 35 year maturity. The
Borrower would be the Islamic Republic of Pakistan

Annex 3
-69-                                  Page I of 12
PAKISTAN
Punjab Private Sector Groundwater Development Project
Terms of Reference
For
Project Implementation Assistance Consultants
1. INTRODUCTION
1.            The Government of Pakistan (GOP) has requested the International Development
Association (IDA) for a Credit to meet part of the cost of the proposed Punjab Private Sector Groundwater
Development. The project is being implemented by the Government of Punjab (GOPunjab). A Project
Management Unit (PMU) in the Planning & Development Board of GOPunjab is responsible for overall
project management and coordination. The Punjab Irrigation Department (PID) and the Directorate
General of Water Management (OFWM) in the Agricultural Departments are the co-implementing
agencies. These terms of reference are for consultancy services in various fields for providing
implementation assistance to the PMU, PID, and OFWM.
2.     Project Objectives.   The overall objectives of the PGWD Project are to increase the scope and
productivity of Punjab's irrigation and drainage subsector and increase farmer's incomes. Its specific
objectives are to: (a) redefine govemment's role in groundwater development with a greater role for the
private sector; (b) develop a regulatory framework and a monitoring program to ensure sustainable use
of the groundwater resources; (c) develop sustainable farmers organizations (FOs) which can efficiently
operate and maintain groundwater irrigation, improve surface irrigation, and establish a base for
participation in the management of the canal systems; (d) rationalize public expenditure on O&M of the
irrigation and drainage systems as well as increase the recovery of public expenditures on irrigation
infrastructure; and (e) avoid environmental hazard of saline water intrusion into fresh groundwater
aquifers.
3.     Project Description.  The proposed five-year (FY97-2001) project would cover the fresh
groundwater areas (FGW) of SCARP 11 (including Shahpur), SCARP III (Rangpur Unit), SCARP IV and
SCARP V (Shorkot Kamnalia) located in administrative divisions of Sargodha, Gujranwala, Faisalabad, and
Lahore. It also includes saline groundwater (SGWs) areas within or at the boundaries of FGW areas. The
gross area is about 1.455 million ha of which about 1.193 million ha have FGW, while the CCA is about
1.292 million hectares of which about 1.068 million hectares have FGW. The major project components
are: (a) privatization of SCARP tubewells (STWs); (b) development of groundwater monitoring and
regulatory framework; (c) improvement of irrigation facilities in fresh groundwater areas, including
improvement of watercourses and lining of minor canals; (d) prevention of saline groundwater intrusion
into fresh aquifers, including rehabilitation of drainage tubewells, and lining of watercourses in saline
groundwater areas; (e) monitoring and evaluation; (f) technical assistance and training; (g) project
management and supervision.

II. SCOPE OF CONSULTING SERVICES
4.            The project requires multi-/interdisciplinary consultancy services in the following four
categories:
A.    Project management support;
B.    Community mobilization and formation of farmer organizations (FOs) for privatizing
STWs, and promoting farmer participation in canal lining;
C.    Engineering services for surveys, design and construction supervision of civil works (canal
lining, watercourse improvement, and rehabilitation of drainage tubewells), including role
of the "Engineer" in contract management; and
D.    Preparation of groundwater regulatory framework.
A. Project Management Support
5.            This includes assistance to the Project Director (PD) PMU and other core staff of the PMU
for, inter alia: (i) procurement planning and processing and ensuring compliance with IDA procurement
guidelines at all stages (receipt of bids, evaluation and award of contracts); (ii) maintenance of
consolidated project accounts and arranging for timely annual audits; (iii) preparation of consolidated
annual work plans for all project components; (iv) preparation of annual budgetary requests; (v)
establishment of an MIS, and preparation of periodic progress reports and project implementation
completion report; (vi) preparation and implementation of training programs; and (vii) preparation of
project supervision plans for the P&D Board. These services would be provided by the Project Manager
of the consultants with inputs of MIS and Accounting and Financial Management Specialists and
support staff. Total input is estimated at 60 staff months (SMs).
B. Community Mobilization for Divestment of SCARP tubewells and Canal Lining
6.       The proposed project will privatize approximately 4,200 SCARP tubewells (STWs)'. The
project will support the formation of FOs, and, through them, the establishment of privately operated
community tubewells (CTWs) to replace govemment-operated STWs throughout the project area. The
objective of this project activity is to develop FOs that can efficiently operate and maintain surface and
groundwater resources for irrigated agriculture in Punjab on a sustainable basis. To achieve this objective,
the project will support the development of a social mobilization program within the Project Management
Unit (PMU). The PMU will have overall responsibility for the project's social mobilization efforts
through a Community Development Support Group (CDSG) recruited as consultancy services. The CDSG
team will consist of: (a) core staff responsible for CDSG field staff training and management, and social
mobilization program coordination; and (b) field staff of Social Organizers (SOs) working with the help
of NGOs and village level social activists to mobilize farmer participation in project activities. CDSG will
have responsibility for the privatization of approximately 2,700, or two-thirds of the STWs targeted for
privatization. The CDSG will also be responsible for assisting the PID in promoting farmer
participation in canal lining, and watch and ward as well as operation and maintenance (O&M) of
Of which 2,458 STWs are located in SCARPs II and Shahpur, 1,001 in SCARP IV, 570 in SCARP III (Rangpur unit), and 101 in
SCARP V.

drainage tubewell rehabilitated under the project. The Directorate of OFWM will be responsible for
privatizing the remaining 1,500 STWs in conjunction with a program of project-supported watercourse

privatization and FO development activities supported by the project, including those undertaken by

7.            The CDSG will report to the PD PMU through the Director (Project Management and
Community Development) of the PMU. It will comprise of international and national consultants and
provide management, technical and implementation services in support of the project's social mobilization
program. The consultancy team will undertake two interrelated activities: (a) to assist the PMU in the
management and coordination of the project's social mobilization efforts, including the training and
supervision of field-level technical and support staff, and coordination of field activities with those of
OFWM; and (b) to mobilize farmer participation in the privatization of STWs, and in the improved
management of surface and groundwater resources. The CDSG will also be responsible for engaging local
Non Governmental Organizations (NGOs) for mobilizing farmer participation in the project.

program of the project. This core group will:

adoption by all agencies involved in the project's social mobilization efforts (i.e.,

procedures and general reporting formats for documenting project performance;
g.     develop a set of criteria for the monitoring and evaluation of project impact in building
sustainable FOs that can be integrated into the overall project MIS.
