9.     Social Mobilization Activities.    The main objective of these activities is to establish viable
FOs that would install and subsequently operate and maintain CTWs. FOs would have the option to: (i)
takeover the SCARP tubewells as such and operate them as community tubewell (CTWs); (ii) modify
STWs and run them as CTW; (iii) install new CTWs; or (iv) replace the STWs' pumpage with existing
or new private tubewells (PTWs). The project would provide one time financial assistance of Rs. 20,000
for modified and new CTWs only. This amount represents approximately 30 percent of the capital cost
of a new CTW. The remaining cost will be borne by the FO membership. For the transferred STWs the
FOs would be charged Rs. 10,000 per STW. The project would support installation of one to two CTWs
on each watercourse (W/C), with a maximum of four CTWs per STWs (only for those STWs that serve
more than one watercourse). The discharge capacities of CTWs would range from 1-2 cusecs (average
1.5 cusecs). The project would have built in safeguards to ensure that the total capacity of CTWs, and
existing and new PTWs, does not exceed the present operational capacity of the STWs. The CTWs would
be located along the communal watercourse, on public land or on the land purchased/leased by the FOs.
The CDSG will undertake the following activities:
a.     carry out Watercourse Assessment Surveys in the entire project area, and make
recommendations to the PMU regarding the location and scheduling of project
implementation activities;
b.     deploy trained Social Organizers throughout the project area to mobilize farmer support
for the STW privatization and canal lining, and assist farmers in forming viable FOs and
installing CTWs;
c.     provide follow-on "after-care" services to FOs to ensure organizational sustainability. and
provide technical assistance in tubewell operation and maintenance, improved water
management and improved agronomic practices;
d.     coordinate and provide management supervision of all social mobilization activities
implemented as sub-projects by NGOs;
e.     provide regular progress reports on STW transition and FO formation efforts to the PMU.

in Lahore and housed in the offices of the PMU. In addition field offices of the CDSG will be established
at Mandi Bahauddin and Sargodha to serve the SCARP 11 and Shahpur areas, at Garh Maharajah to serve
SCARP III (Rangpur Unit) and SCARP V (Shorkot Kamalia), and at Sharqpur for the SCARP IV area.
Other core staff will comprise a Field Operations Management team, based in Lahore, four Watercourse
Assessment Survey Teams, and four social mobilization field teams whose size will be expanded as project
activities increase in geographical scope. In addition to support staff, each field team will typically
include: one Social Coordinator (SC), with responsibilities for managing the field office; Technical
Coordinators (TCs); Social Organizers (SOs), After-care Social Organizers (ASOs), and a Watercourse
Assessment Survey Team.

A senior Irrigation Engineer (4) with knowledge of tubewell technology, and a senior

experience may be considered in lieu of the requirement for an advanced academic
degree. SOs must be fluent in local languages, highly motivated, willing to work and live
under difficult physical conditions, and be capable of interacting effectively with farmers
and government officials from various departments.
d.     After-care Social Organizers (ASOs).      ASOs are required to support the ongoing
activities of each FO once it has installed a CTW. One ASO will provide support to two
SOs in the formation of FOs. However, the primary responsibilities of the ASO will be
to monitor the performance of each FO according to the conditions of its formation,
provide guidance to FO membership in the maintenance of records and management of
its financial resources, and provide technical support to farmers willing to adopt improved
water management and agronomic practices. ASOs should hold a Masters degree in
Agronomy or Agricultural Extension and have a minimum of two years experience
working in agricultural development in Punjab. Like the SOs, ASOs must be fluent in
local languages, highly motivated, willing to work and live under difficult physical
conditions and capable of interacting effectively with farmers and government officials
from various departments.

D. Preparation of Groundwater Regulatory Framework
20.          In Punjab, groundwater extractions have been increasing consistently over the last two
decades. As a result, the water tables are falling in some of the fresh groundwater areas. This is a matter
of considerable concern. Over exploitation threatens the quality of the resource --lower level aquifers are
generally more saline, and significant differences of groundwater levels between fresh and saline areas
induce intrusion of saline water.  As the rate of use of groundwater is approaching its potential
availability, determination of groundwater rights (at individual and community level) and regulation of
its use, is essential for sustainable and equitable use of this common resource. The project would support
the development and implementation of a regulatory framework. It would strengthen capacity for
intensive monitoring in critical areas, develop capacity for determining safe yields of aquifers and



-78-                                           Annex 3
Page 10 of 12
allocation of groundwater rights, as well as development and support for the institutional setup for
implementing groundwater regulations in critical areas.
21.            Currently, the SCARPs Monitoring Organization (SMO) of WAPDA  monitors the
groundwater aquifers especially in the SCARP areas. SMO's capacity for country wide monitoring would
be strengthened under the National Drainage Program.  The proposed project would strengthen
GOPunjab's capacity for intensive monitoring of the areas identified as critical for groundwater control
on the basis of SMO's general monitoring results. In addition to groundwater monitoring database,
simulation models would be employed in determining the safe yield of aquifers and its allocation by
distributary, watercourse and individual users.  Detailed rules for control and licensing as well as
methodology and institutional setup would be developed. The development work would be completed in
first three years of the project while the institutional setup for enforcement of regulation in critical areas
would be put in place during the remaining two years.
22.    Scope of Work.        The program of developing regulations and their implementation would
consist of three phases; (i) in the first phase, the groundwater management areas will be established based
on the existhig inforination ; (ii) in the second phase, critical areas for groundwater management would
be identified, detailed data collection and monitoring would be carried out in the critical areas, and safe
yields of aquifers and groundwater rights would be determined; and (iii) in the third phase, a procedure
for regulating the groundwater use in each critical area, and institutional arrangemiients would be
developed.
23.    Establishment of Management Areas.           The consultants will complete the following tasks
wvithin tde first year of their assignment:
a.     For ease in management of the groundwater basins, classify aquifers in Punjab in different
Groundwater Management areas, based on existing data, and considerinig behavior and
quality of aquifers, hydrological and meteorological data, topographic and geological
information, canal command areas, surface water application, efficiency of use, and
cropping patterns, etc.;
b.     Develop a database consisting of basic information for each managemenit area and update
it on a continuous basis; and
c.     Identify in each management area the sources of major aquifer recharge as well as
extractions.
24.    Identification of Critical Areas and Establishment of Groundwater Rights.         This shall
involve the following tasks which would be completed during the second year of the project:
a.     In parallel to establishing the Groundwater Management areas, identify critical areas
requiring extensive management;
h.     Develop and calibrate Groundwater models to simulate the groundwater behavior over a
long period including wet and dry cycles;
c.     carry out detailed data collection for calibration of the model, and develop a database for
eventual regulation of groundwater use for the critical areas; and



_79_                                    Annex 3
Page 11 of 12
d.    Using the simulation models and monitoring information, develop proposals for
establishing rights to the groundwater by distributary command area, watercourse
command (chak) and by farm.
25.   Development of Procedures for Regulation and Institutional Structure. The following tasks
would be completed during the third year of the project.
a.    Based on the safe yields of the aquifer in the long run (considering wet and dry cycles),
pattern of groundwater water use in the critical areas, utilization rates of private,
community and public tubewells, estimates safe installed capacity by watercourse
command, as well as by distributaries. (For foreseeable future the regulation will be
based on the installed capacity of the tubewells which will be correlated to the actual
groundwater extractions through empirical studies and simulation models);
b.    develop proposals for a program for licensing the groundwater tubewells with an objective
towards achieving safe installed capacity. (In issuing the tubewell licenses preference will
be give to community tubewells supplying to several users in any chak);
c.    develop proposals for detailed institutional setups for groundwater management in each
management area, according to the type and the severity of the problem;
d.    implement pilots to test and evolve effective methods of groundwater monitoring and
regulation, roles of local and regulating institutions, farmers and their organizations, and
NGOs before refining the regulation procedures; and
e.    develop proposals for any new  legislation that may be required for facilitating
implementation of the regulatory framework.

PAKISTAN
Punjab Private Sector Groundwater Development Project
Farmer Participation Plan
I1.            A growing body of evidence indicates that the privatization of public tubewells supplying
irrigation water provides farmers with better control over the joint use of surface and groundwater
resources. Improved control over these resources results in improved irrigation efficiencies and increased
agricultural productivity.4 When privatization strategies are designed to promote the organization of
farmers for the joint-ownership of pump irrigation schemes, improvements in the equity of irrigation water
distribution are more or less guaranteed. Joint-ownership leads to better management of both surface and
sub-surface water resources, establishes a foundation for the regulation of groundwater extraction (through
cooperative management) and sets the precedent , through the strengthening of participatory management
processes, for greater farmer involvement in increasingly more complex levels of resource management....
2.              The proposed project would transfer the ownership of approximately 4,200 public
tubewells -- installed in the 1960s and 1970s under successive Salinization Control and Reclamation
Projects (SCARPs) -- to farmer groups in four SCARP regions of Punjab Province. The project will
support the formation of approximately 6,300 Farmer Organizations (FOs) and their development of
privately operated community tubewells (CTWs) to replace govemment-operated SCARP tubewells
(STWs) throughout the project area. The objective of the project's farmer participation plan is to develop
FOs that can efficiently operate and maintain surface and groundwater resources for irrigated agriculture
in Punjab on a sustainable basis.
3.              Central to the success of public tubewell (STW) privatization would be the project's
promotion of farmer participation in the development and management of private groundwater extraction,
i.e., in the collective investment in community tubewells, the operation and maintenance of those
tubewells, and the effective and sustainable use of groundwater resources in conjunction with surface water
supplies. The encouragement of CTW  development is necessary to ensure that all farmers retain access
to sufficient supplies of groundwater to augment surface supplies when STWs are taken out of operation.
In addition, the project's successful promotion of CTW operation would to enhance the equity of water
resource distribution by improving water supplies to farmers with small land holdings at the tailend of
the tertiary conveyance systems.
4.              There has been a proliferation of private tubewell development in Punjab over the last two
decades.5    However, the costs of tubewell installation, and the marginal return on the investment in
4    See: Robert Chambers, N.C. Saxena and Tushaar Shah. To the Hands of the Poor. Boulder, CO: Westview Press.
1994: Linden Vincent and Peter Dempsey "Conjunctive Water Use for Irrigation: Good Theory, Poor Practice."
Water Resource Development, Vol. 9. No. 3, 1993.
The use of groundwater in agriculture in Pakistan was almost nil in 1960. Today, groundwater provides an
estimated 40 percent of the water reaching farmer's fields. Besides increasing the total amount of water available to
farmers for irrigation purposes, private wells have also eliminated much of the uncertainty associated with the
timing and volume of public irrigation supplies. See Robert Johnson. Factors Determining Groundwater Usefor
Irrigation in Pakistan's Punjab. Report prepared for the United States Agency for Intemational Development by
the Irrigation Support Project for Asia and the Near East. December, 1992.



Annex 4
-82-                                          Page 2 of 17
terms of crop yields on small landholdings, discourages the majority of farmers in Punjab from risking
the investment.6 In addition, while there is a well established system for the sharing of perennial surface
irrigation water (warabandi), Pakistani farmers have limited experience with the collective management
of groundwater extraction to augment surface supplies. Although there is evidence of informal "joint
ownership" and operation of small tubewells by farm families throughout Punjab, wide inequities in
patterns of land ownership, fragmentation of landholdings, particularly those of small landholders, a
relatively hierarchical social structure, the heterogeneity of many farming communities, and traditional
rivalries among, and between, lineages discourage widespread collaboration in capital investment to
improve agricultural production.
5.              The promotion of sustainable FOs and their operation of CTWs will require an intensive
institutional development effort. Evaluation of past and disappointing experiences in the promotion of
Water Users Associations for the improvement and maintenance of surface water conveyance systems in
Pakistan indicates that: (i) beneficiaries were not given sufficient autonomy in the planning, participation
and management of the watercourse improvements; (ii) the implementing agencies lacked the required
skills to build effective Associations; (iii) the amount of resources required to support sustainable
Associations, particularly in terms of institution building and follow-up visits by field staff, were vastly
underestimated; and (iv) incentives for farmer participation in the Associations were short-lived. The
farmer participation plan proposed for the Punjab Private Sector Groundwater Development Project takes
stock of this experience and incorporates the lessons learned into a new strategy for the development of
FOs. The approach is being tested under the on-going Second SCARP Transition Project in which 230
STWs were earmarked for transfer to FOs promoted through a recently developed farmer participation
program. The farmers' response has been good, nearly 800 FOs had been established and about 550
CTWs have been installed.
6.              Participatory approaches are gaining increased acceptance in donor-supported development
efforts as an effective means of promoting the sustainability of project benefits. However, participatory
approaches are not without their costs in terms of the time and effort required to recruit and train field
staff, to mobilize farmer support, and to carry out the socioeconomic research essential for effective
project implementation, monitoring and evaluation. Nevertheless, experience has demonstrated that these
additional costs are usually offset by subsequent improvements in the pace and quality of project
implementation, improved cost recovery rates, and sustainable systems of infrastructure operation and
maintenance.
7.              The proposed project will require a high level of staff resources over the five year life of
the project for the successful implementation of the farner participation plan. To place this level of
resource requirement in context, the following sections review the rational for farmer participation in the
irrigation sector, the experience of participatory approaches developed in Pakistan; the development of that
experience as it has been applied to the Second SCARP Transition Project; and the use to which the
lessons learned have been used to refine the farmer participation plan proposed for the Private Sector
Groundwater Development Project.
Based on data from the 1980 Agricultural Census, the average farrn size in the project area has been estimated at
4.4 hectares. Analysis of this data indicates that approximately 80 percent of all farms in the project area are
smaller than six hectares, while only ten percent are larger than ten hectares. Farms of less than six hectares
account for approximately 50 percent of the cultivated land area. The remaining 20 percent of farms account for
the remaining 50 percent of the land area. Landholdings are typically fragmented (especially in northeast and
central Punjab SCARP areas), with farmers cultivating an average of three non-contiguous plots. Average
household size is estimated at 7 persons. Considerable caution must be used in the use of the Agricultural Census
data which often disguise the failure of successive land reforms to break up the extensive landholdings.

Rationale for a Participatory Approach
8.             Community participation in rural development involves beneficiaries in the identification,
design, financing and implementation of their own development projects. Community participation
directly supports projects suited to the needs and abilities of the people whom the projects are intended
to benefit. In contrast, conventional approaches involving donor-to-government development grant or loan
assistance typically seek to strengthen the capacity of host-country institutions to deliver assistance.
Conventional projects are typically identified by outsiders (project managers, government officials,
technical experts) or project committees made up of local notables who represent their community to
outsiders. Neither of these groups is bound by community preference in the selection of projects. As a
result, conventional projects often fall short of providing the intended benefits to the community. Not
surprisingly, there is little incentive for the community to support or participate in a project that it did not
request and that may not serve its needs.
9.             The impact of participatory approaches has been particularly well documented in the
irrigation sector.7 Since the 1950s, large amounts of money have been invested to improve irrigated
agriculture worldwide. While initial investments in infrastructure contributed to a significant expansion
of food production, a growing share of investments in the irrigation sector over the past 20 years have
been targeted for the rehabilitation of irrigation infrastructure that the governments and water users have
failed to maintain. With fiscal crises in most developing countries, weaknesses of government delivery
of water services have become apparent. The deterioration of irrigation systems throughout the world has
exposed serious institutional deficiencies and resulting government failures in most water resources
agencies. This includes lack of motivation and accountability of agency staff, high levels of political
interference and rent seeking, as well as inadequate concern for needs of users. Consequently, without
reliable service, users are not willing to share in the investment, operation and maintenance costs.
Reliance on overextended government agencies that have neglected financial accountability, user
participation, and charging while not delivering services effectively to the users and particularly to the
poor is a major concern. The Bank's review of twenty-one irrigation projects found meaningful cost
recovery only in those projects in which water management and O&M had been entrusted to water users.
10.            Because of their limited financial and administrative resources, governments need to be
selective in the responsibility they assume for water resources. An operational principle is that nothing
should be done at a higher level of government that can be done satisfactorily at a lower level. Thus,
where local or private capabilities exist the water delivery service should be transferred to private sector,
financially autonomous public corporations, and to community organizations. The lessons of experience
suggest that an important principle in restructuring public service agencies is their conversion into
financially autonomous entities, with effective authority to charge and collect fees and with freedom to
manage without political interference. Such entities need to work under hard budget constraint that
enhances incentives for efficiency and revenue generation.  Participation empowers stakeholder to
influence the formulation of policy, the choice of design and investment, an management decision
affecting their communities. It establishes necessary sense of ownership, bringing user discipline, and
7    See for example: Bryan Bruns and Irchamni Soelaiman, "From Practice to Policy: Agency and NGO in Indonesia's
Programme to Tum Over Small Irrigation Systems to Farmers," in Overseas Development Institute Irrigation
Management Network, Network Paper No. 10, London, April 1992; Edward D. Martin and Robert Yoder,
"Institutions for Irrigation Management in Farmer Managed Systems in Nepal." Intemational Irrigation
management Institute Research Paper No. 3. Sri Lanka, 1987.; Kerry J. Bymes, "Case Studies of Water User
Associations in Pakistan." The World Bank, Washington, D.C., December 1992.; and Frances F. Korten and Robert
Y. Siy, Jr., Transforming a Bureaucracy: The Experience of the Philippine National Irrigation Administration.
West Hartford, CN: Kumarian Press, 1988.



-84-                                                 Annex 4
Page 4 of 17
accountability for service delivery. This would not only reduce public expenditures but ensure better
O&M by making water users responsible for the facilities from which they benefitted.8
11.              User participation in irrigation management is not new. Communal irrigation systems have
existed in other countries, such as Thailand, India, Sri Lanka, Bali, Indonesia, the Philippines, Nepal,
Mexico, Argentina, Columbia, and the Dominican Republic. Experience indicates that active farmer
involvement in irrigation is very cost effective in mobilization of local resources, improvement and
maintenance activities, reduction of irrigation department staff time, reduction in the destruction of
facilities, fee and fine collection, resolution of disputes, and provision of an organized means for extension
and farmer trainilng. Recognition of these benefits (and a realization that many of irrigation performance
problems were poor O&M, brought on by government institutional failures'") led to a marked increase
in the development of user associations in the past 20 years." Developing country experiences with
WUAs vary considerably, but valuable lessons can be drawn for Pakistan's strategy.  Histories of
successful indigenous system development, however, reveal a common pattern: successful user associations
develop simultaneously with the planning, construction and expansion or creation of the physical system--
often over a period of many years.'2
12.             One of the successful government efforts to promote user participation in irrigation
management is the Philippines. There, more than 5,600 communal irrigation schemes cover almost half
of the irrigated land. They conduct O&M  and operate on a self-financing basis with technical assistance
from  a network of Irrigation Community Organizers.
13.             In Indonesia, the government sought to turnover small-scale irrigation systems to WlJAs,
when under-financing of O&M began to threaten system sustainability. The WUAs were given legal
rights and technical assistance to take on management responsibilities. Since 1987, the government has
transferred more than 400 systems, covering more than 34,000 ha, to WUAs. Success of the program is
said to be due to early inclusion of farmers in design and creation of the WUAs.
14.              In Nepal, 70 percent of all irrigation is now  under farmer control.  Expansion of the
system  in recent years has been helped by a Bank-financed Irrigation Line of Credit Pilot Project. The
project extends credit to WUAs which contribute to capital costs for construction of new subsystems and
take over O&M.
15.              In Turkey, Irrigation Department's plans for completing transfer of irrigation system by
the turn of the century are progressing well with more than 200,000 ha transferred so far. Many states
Max Goldensohn. Participation and Empowerment: An Assessment of Water User Associations in Asia and Egypt.

of India, such as Tamil Nadu, Orissa, Maharashtra and Haryana are embarking on the same approach of
greater users participation.
16.           Interest in (and new approaches to) farmer-managed irrigation schemes has grown in Latin
America as well, most recently in Mexico where large irrigation systems have been transferred to the
farmers organizations and their federations. More than 2 million ha have been transferred and some of
the main and branchi canals are managed by the users. In Argentina, traditional user organizations (whose
members held a total of 100 to 500 ha) were found to be too small to meet the associated costs.
Maintenance was insufficient, water management was weak, and headenders benefitted disproportionately.
To capture the economies of scale, these associations merged into larger ones with 5,000 to 15,000 ha.
Twenty-onie new organizations were formed, covering 200,000 ha. Each organization is autonomous,
finances its own expenditures, and issues its own regulatioiis in accordance with the newly-enacted water
law. Each hires professional managers to take charge of for example, water delivery, cost recovery, and
maintenance. Not only are administrative costs are lower, but the larger organizations have increased
conveyance efficiency by 10 percent.
17.           In the Dominican Republic, management of irrigation and drainage systems is transferred
to WUAs in two project areas, Azua (YSURA) and Santiago (PYRN Contract 1), with total area of 14,400
ha, serving 6,000 farm  families.  A water users group (Nucleos) was formed on each sublateral.
Distribution of irrigationi water is controlled by Nucleos after it leaves the lateral canal. The Nucleos on
an irrigationi lateral subsequently formed a WUA (Junta) that encompassed a complete lateral. After
transferring the system to the WUAs the cost recovery and O&M of the system has improved significantly.
18.           In Colombia, management of two large irrigation districts is in the hands WUAs. WUAs
recover O&M costs through a combination of fixed and volumetric charges, and partly from equipment
rentals and bank interests.
19.            The World Bank has played a major role in promoting participatory approaches  to
irrigation system development and management throughout the world. A recent review of that experience
has summarized the benefits of an increase in user participation as follows:'3
(a)    Improved system performance -- clear gains in efficiency and in the standard of service
are achieved wheni system design and management are transferred to farmers;
(b)    Reduced governmenr expendilure -- farmer associations have proven to be more effective
collectors of user fees than government agencies;
(c)    Sustainability -- farmer participation in the design and construction of irrigation systems
promotes their sense of "ownership" of the system, and gives farmers a greater level of
incentive to maintain systems than those constructed by the government; physical and
financial sustainability of systems are enhanced when O&M costs are met from user fees
rather than government subsidies;
(d)    Equity -- a more equitable distribution of water resources to potential beneficiaries is
achieved in participatory systems than in non-participatory systems;
'   Ruth Meinzen-Dick, Richard Reidinger and Andrew Manzardo. Parlicipation in Irrigation. Environmental Department
Ilarticipation Series Paper No. 3. Washington, D.C.: The World Bank. February, 1995.



Annex 4
-86-                                       Page 6 of 17
(e)    Multiplier Effects -- skills developed by farmers in the participatory framework can be
expanded to take on greater levels of administrative responsibility and resource
management; these same skills can be applied to community relations with other
developmental agencies.
20.            The community management of groundwater development in conjunction with public
surface water irrigation schemes has enormous potential for increasing irrigation efficiencies and
improving agricultural production by putting resource management more directly in the hands of the
resource users. However, the kinds of institutions required in situations of conjunctive use are very
different from the Water User Associations conventionally promoted for the management of surface water
conveyance structures. In conjunctive use situations, individual farmers who have their own tubewells
have fewer incentives to participate in groundwater development than those dependent on surface irrigation
water. Nevertheless, farners can derive benefits from common management of groundwater extraction
by reducing their investment costs, reducing risk of over exploitation and by pressuring the government
to amend surface water allocations to take advantage of new crop market options available to them.'4
The proposed project attempts to capitalize on Pakistan's accumulated experience in promoting
participatory development to build those institutions required for effective irrigation water management
Farmer Participation: The Pakistan Experience
2].            The participatory development approach in Pakistan is characterized by the organization
of small farmiers into decentralized, broad-based and homogeneous interest groups called Village
Organizations (VO). The approach has its origins in the small farmer development initiatives conceived
during the cooperative movement of the 1970s. These included the Comila Project in what is now
Bangladesh and the Daudzai Project near Peshawar implemented by the Pakistan Academy of Rural
Development. The approach assumes that villagers with relatively homogeneous resources, who own their
crop land, and who collectively manage the distribution of public resources such as irrigation water and
common grazing land, will cooperate to finance, manage, and maintain village-level infrastructure projects
that benefit all villagers.  As the approach has evolved, in the context of donor-supported rural
development initiatives, local communities establish their own institutions, identify their development
priorities, organize their resources, manage their development agenda, and create the necessary links for
continuing technical and financial assistance by outside agencies. The supporting agency -- either
governmental or nongovernmental -- provides technical and financial assistance, but it does not infringe
on the autonomy of the village organization. Decision-making rests with the community, which can reject
the advice and judgement of the project experts."
22.            The Village Organization approach has had its most long-lived application in the
development efforts of the Agha Khan Foundation in Pakistan's remote Northern Areas. Since 1982, the
Agha Khan Rural Support Program (AKRSP) has been developing a model of village self-reliance that
has been adapted to a wide variety of developmental activities throughout the country.
23.    The Agha Khan Rural Support Program.   The strategy of AKRSP has been to promote the
development of village institutions for the financing and management of essential local infrastructure

supplement labor costs. All subsequenlt village organizationi activities, including maintenance of the

tile local governm11eint for improvements in public services."6
25.            The success of the AKRSP is largely responsible for the growing emphasis on community
participationi in developiment initiatives in recent years throughout Pakistan. For example, the Government
of Nortlh West Frontier Province, witth assistance from USAID, sanctioiied the creation of the Sarhad Rural
Support Corporation in 1990 to assist the provincial governlment in the implementation of donor-funded
area development projects."7  A number of donor-supported natural resource management projects in
S'indlh, B3alochistani, Punjab and Azad Jammu and Kashmir have also been designed to incorporate villager
participation in tlleir implementation.  In 1992, the AKRSP approach was adopted at the national level
with the creation of the National Rural Support Program to implement the Government's multi-sectoral
Social Action Plan on a nation-wide scale.
26.            At the time that the AKRSP VO approachi was being developed in Pakistan's remote areas,
donors were attempting to apply the growinig world-wide experience with participatory development in
the irrigation sector to the improved delivery of surface water for irrigated agriculture in Pakistan's
densely populated Indus Basin. The cornerstonie of this approach was the organization of farmers into
Water User Associations for the improvement of canal infrastructure and the improved management of
irrigation water.
27.    Water Users Associations and On Farm  Water Management.                       In the late 1970s,
irrigation development policy in Pakistan began to shift from construction to system rehabilitation and
improvemenit of water managemenit on existing projects down to the farm level. This shift was influenced
See tlhc c\aluations of' AKRSI' carried out h\ the World Bank's Operations Evaluation Department in 1987 and

Page 8 of 17
by the United States Agency for International Development (USAID)/Pakistan On-Farm Water
Management Pilot Project (1976-81) in which farmers supplied all unskilled labor for watercourse
improvement -- cleaning, realignment, and lining with bricks and mortar -- while the government provided
materials and technical assistance. Systems restored under this project deteriorated rapidly because farmers
were not organized to maintain the improved watercourses once they were improved. The World Bank
built upon the lessons learned from the USAID-supported effort through the development of the On-Farm
Water Management Program (OFWM). The general aim of OFWM program has been to promote the
formation of local-level Water User Associations (WUA) that can assume the operations and maintenance
of tertiary irrigation channels, or watercourses. The program works through WUAs to mobilize labor and
funds for the improvement and maintenance of watercourses, with watercourse lining serving as the major
incentive for farmer participation in the program.  The Bank required that each of the four provinces
implementing the program establish legal ordinance formalizing the procedures for the organization and
registration of WUA at the watercourse command level. OFWM programs also attempt to link farmers
with specialized public services that introduce cultivation practices for maximizing available water use.
These services include contract grading, precision land leveling and improved agricultural extension.
28.           In Punjab, a Directorate of On-Farm  Water Management was created within the
Agriculture Department to provide training to WUA leadership and field-level assistance to help farmers
organize WUAs. WUAs proved to be effective institutions for mobilizing labor to improve watercourses
and more than 17,000 have been registered. However, because the farmers were legally organized and
empowered solely to improve the physical infrastructure of the watercourse, there was little incentive
beyond the immediate inputs of the project for farmers to maintain the WUA or the watercourses. As a
result, the improvement of civil works alone had little or no positive effect on equity of water delivery,
even though there has been some increase in supply at tailend farms. Seepage losses were diminished if
not eliminated with canal lining but there remain considerable water losses along channels because of
operational waste and theft of water through outlet tampering.'8
29.           The disappointing results of OFWM efforts to date are largely due to organizational
inequities both within the beneficiary communities and the public agencies mandated to serve those
communities.  Watercourses are a classic common property resource shared by 20 to 100 farmers.
However, there are many different categories of farmers in the irrigated areas of Punjab. They are defined
by such factors as: the size and tenure arrangements of their land holdings; social and economic status;
ethnic and kinship identities; political affiliations; varying access to bureaucracy; and linkages with the
national economy. Farmers may have too few common interests to sustain multipurpose joint action.
Conversely, sub-groups within a WUA (e.g., extended families and lineages, or biradari) have interests
that are not common to the entire community of water users.
30.           Moreover, WIUAs have little influence as institutions aside from the mobilization of labor
to achieve the objectives of the OFWM program. While the Punjab Water User Association ordinance of
1981 established the WUAs as legal entities vis-a-vis the Irrigation Department and the Directorate of
OFWM, other government agencies are not bound to recognize WUAs, and the WUAs have no authority
to deal with other government departments. Yet, even minor water management issues and repairs to
watercourse may involve other government agencies, for example, the Revenue Department, which levies
taxes on WUA members, not the WUA itself. In effect, the WUA has no legal mandate for systems
operation and water resource management. While scarcity helped stimulate the formation of WUAs, once
'I  Associates in Rural Development. "Command Water Management Project Final Report." Report prepared for the
United States Agency for Intemational Development, Islamabad, Pakistan. Burlington, VT: Associates in Rural
Development, Inc. 1991.

the surface supply of water improved as a result of improvements to physical infrastructure, the
organizations became dormant.'9 As one analyst notes, the OFWM experience demonstrates that, to
ensure sustainable farmer organizations, a working agreement and its legal framework must provide
farmiers with rights and benefits as well as with duties and responsibilities.20
3 1.           The proliferation of affordable lift technology for the private extraction of groundwater
has revolutionized small-farmer production systems in Punjab.  It has also called into question the
continuing efficiency of public institutions in the management of the irrigation sector. However, the
relatively unrestricted pumping of groundwater by private farmers poses enonnous economic and
environmental risks for irrigated agriculture in Punjab. As the following section points out, participatory
development of groundwater resources represents a balanced strategy for the sustainable management of
irrigation water.
The Transition from Public to Private Development of Groundwater Resources
32.            Over the last three decades, approximately 13,500 public tubewells have been installed
by the Government of Pakistan to control waterlogging and saline soil conditions throughout the irrigated
areas of the Indus River Basin under a succession of Salinity Control and Reclamation Projects (SCARP).
Nearly 75 percent of these tubewells were installed in Punjab Province. In saline groundwater areas,
SCARP tubewells pumped water into surface drains or main canals. In fresh groundwater areas, water
from STWs was normally pumped into tertiary watercourses and used to augment surface irrigation water
supplies. The SCARP projects were generally successful in meeting the objectives of controlling the depth
of water tables and supplementing surface irrigation water supplies. However, STW performance has
declined over tine due to poor management, political interference and inadequate funding for O&M. Half
of all STWs in Punjab operate at below 50 percent capacity, and only 15 percent operate at better than
80 percent capacity.
33.            Poor STW  performance has in a number of areas been compensated by informal farmers
groups that have formed spontaneously. These groups look after daily operations of the pump and its
routine maintenance. These groups also finance major repairs using local mechanics, electricians and other
services from local markets. However, farmers find it difficult to carry out repairs involving the main
shaft of the turbine because such repairs require specialized equipment available only with Punjab
Irrigation Department (PID) workshops. Nevertheless, farmers follow-up and assist PID in expediting such
repairs.
34.            SCARP Transition    Beginning in 1991, with a series of SCARP Transition Project,
the Bank sought to counter the impact of the deteriorating  STW  performance by supporting the
privatization of groundwater extraction and application. By offering subsidies and credit to farmers for
the installation of private tubewells (PTWs), the Bank sought to: (i) increase agricultural production
through improved and timely provision of supplementary irrigation water; (ii) control water table levels
so that they do not adversely effect production; (iii) reduce the PID's budgetary burden by closing all
STWs and replacing them with privately-operated and maintained tubewells. However, the closure of the
STWs posed a significant problem for farmers at the tailend of watercourses, particularly those farmers
Goidensohn, 1994, op.cit. Kapil Goonesekere, A.R. Saleemi, Tariq Husain and Max Goldensohn. "Trip Repon --
The Performance and Sustainability of Water User's Associations in Pakistan." Report prepared for the USAID
Irrigation support Project for Asia and the Near East. February, 1994,
'i   Meinzen-Dick, et al., 1995. op. cit



-90-                                         Annex 4
Page 10 of 17
who cannot afford to install PTWs. The supplemental flows that the STW contribute to surface water
delivery systems increase the reliability of water reaching the tailend of the watercourse. With the closure
of the STWs, surface water supplies would no longer reach the tail. Consequently, tailend farmers have
to rely more or less entirely on groundwater. However, even though the number of PTWs is quite high
in some of the project areas, farmers without PTWs do not have an assured supply of groundwater.
Instead, they must rely on those that do to sell surplus water at high rates. In areas where groundwater
is saline. exclusive dependence on groundwater for irrigation inevitably leads to deterioration of soil
conditions and decrease in crop yields.
35.           The Bank determined that the promotion of PTW development was inappropriate because
it led to farmers installing excessive pumping capacity, which in turn led to them incurring excessive
operations and maintenianice costs. Excess pumping capacity leads to the over exploitation of groundwater,
promotes greater inequity in water supply, and the improper conjunctive use of canal and groundwater.
The Mid-Term Review of the Second SCARP Transition Project (SSTP) determined the best remedy to
this situation was to eliminate continued subsidies for PTWs.  Instead, the Mid-Term  Review
recommended the promotion of collective investment by farmers in Community Tubewells (CTWs) to
replace STWs at the head or middle reaches of the watercourse, and the continued improvement of
conveyance efficiencies throughi watercourse lining.2
36.    The Introduction of Farmer Participation.  Following  the Mid-Term  Review, a social
mobilization strategy was developed for the SSTP to establish Farmer Organizations (FO) throughout the
project area capable of installing, operating and maintaining CTWs. In the absence of significant
experience mobilizling community participation, four different approaches for establishing FOs were
developed.  One approach relies on the SSTP implementation consultants to train and field Social
Organizers who work intensively with farmers to build FOs. Another approach utilizes a national rural
service NGO, the National Farm Guides, that works under the supervision of the of the SSTP consultants
to establish FOs. A third approach utilizes the services of the Irrigation Agronomy Teams of the
Directorate of OFWM to establish FOs in those areas of SSTP where it is implementing watercourse
improvement in conjunction witil the projects groundwater privatization efforts. Finally, a fourth approach
utilizes the services of the canal paItwari or field-level revenue agents of the Provincial Irrigation
Department (PID).
37.           Under the SSTP farmer participation strategy, FOs are required to have a membership of
approximately one-third of the water users (shareholders) on a targeted watercourse. The FOs may choose
to register as a formal legal entity or as a subset of a Water User Association (if one exists on the
watercourse) under the Provincial Water User Association Ordinance. FOs have three options for the
establishment of a CTW: (I) purchase the original STW from the PID and utilize the original equipment;
(ii) replace the bore and pump of the existing STW with project (partial) financial and technical
assistance; (iii) or install a new tubewell at another site along the watercourse. FOs must nominate a
management committee, establish a bank account, and deposit funds for their share of CTW installation
costs plus the estimated cost of one year's operation and maintenance.  A fund for the eventual
replacement of the CTW is generated from the sale of water to FO members or to non-members. In the
case of option iii, FOs must also secure an agreement from the landowner on whose property the new
CTW will be installed for unrestricted access by the FO members. Once the CTW is installed, each
member pays a fixed rate -- established by the FO to meet operation and maintenance costs -- to the
management committee for each hour of pumping required to water his fields.
Second SCARP Transition Project (Cr. 2257-PAK). "Mid-Term Review, Follow-up Mission: Aide-Memoire."
Islamabad, Pakistan: The World Bank. November, 1994.



Annex 4
-91-                                      Page 11 of 17
38.            Under the revised SSTP, a Project Impact Evaluation Study (PIES) was funded to monitor
and evaluate the formation of FOs. The Interim  Report of the PIES consultants evaluated the farmer
participation approach implemented by the SSTP consultants most favorably in terms of SO training,
reconnaissance and familiarizing farmers with the aims of the project, efforts to facilitate formation and
provide continiuotis support to FOs, and overall performance in meeting targets (i.e., number of FOs
established and CTWs installed against the number of STWs for which they were responsible).22  The
evaluationi results were based on only three months of field implementation. Thus, the results cannot be
considered an effective indicator of sustainability of project initiatives, nor the sustainability of the FOs
themilselves. Hlowever, the study results indicate that a large-scale and effective farmer participation effort
can be mobilized in a relatively short period of time. The success of Social Organizers in meeting
physical targets also suggests that there is considerable receptivity among farmers to the development of
CTWs.
39.            During the Appraisal mission for the proposed project -- after more than six months of
SSTP's efforts to promote farmer participation -- the Appraisal Team assessed SSTP's social mobilization
approacih througil a review of project monitorinig and evaluation reports, interviews with Social Organizers
and( field visits to Farmer Organizations formed utilizing each of the four approaches supported by SSTP.
The most frequently cited obstacles that SOs faced in mobilizing farner participation in CTW  schemes
iniclude the Following:
(a)    Conflicts and rivalries based in traditional political, class, communal, and inter-fanilial
alliances in rural Punjab typically discourage local-level collaboration.
(b)    Opposition of farmers with large landholdings who derive the most significant benefits
from functioniing STWs -- both in terms of low cost water and high application rates --
to the closing of STWs. These farmers attempt to use political influence to prevent the
STW  closing, and to dissuade smaller farmers who share the watercourse from organizing
and taking advantage of project benefits.
(c)     Fanner demand for larger subsidies and concessions from the project to compensate for
the sharp increase in the cost of groundwater that results from the closure of STWs.
(d)    Opposition of farmers with PTWs who fear that a proliferation of CTWs will undernine
their market for surplus groundwater.
40.            There is ample evidence of collective action among farming households in Punjab to
suggest that these conflicts and rivalries are not an insurmounitable obstacle to FO  formation and
sustainabilitv. Warabandi, the system by whicih farilers share surface irrigation water supplies in turn,
has been in continuous operation throughout the irrigated areas of the Indus Basin for well over 100 years.
Farn families throughlout the Punjab typically exchange labor during peak agricultural production periods
in a system known as warang. The evidence of informal farmers groups that have taken over the
operation and maintenance of STWs, and of private tubewells operated by small numbers of farm families
in the project area also indicates that there exists a potential for collective capital investment and enduring
cooperation in the operation and maintenance of capital goods at the farm level. SOs can build on these
traditional and spontaneous institutions of cooperation to promote support for the FO
Multi Dimensional Consultants. "Project impact Evaluation Study for the Second Scarp Transition Project;
Monitoring Farmers Organizations (FOs)". Interim Report submitted to the Irrigation and Power Department,
(iovcrnment ot Punjab. Lahore. Mav. 1995.



Annex 4
Page 12 of 17
41.    Equity Issues. With regard to the influence of large landholders, or those who have heretofore
monopolized capital investment and water markets, there is evidence to suggest that this influence may
effect the equity of the water distribution, which the proposed project attempts to promote in a number
of areas. From the Appraisal Team's field investigations, it was evident that certain members of a group
were financing the membership of poorer shareholders by subsidizing their cash contribution to the FO.
It is possible that more influential and wealthy farmers may use their advantage to dominate the FO and
monopolize some, or all, of the resources of the CTW. In these circumstances of a very hierarchical
social structure and an inequitable distribution of assets and resources, it may be unrealistic to expect that
truly equitable and democratic Farmer Organizations can be supported throughout the project area. As
the recent World Bank assessment of participation in the irrigation sector notes, participation may be at
odds with equity objectives where some groups have more influence than others. In a number of areas,
powerful farmers are likely to have more influence than others in irrigation associations -- ironically, they
may function more effectively as the result of the influence of powerful farmers. While inequities cannot
be eliminated altogether, efforts can be made to structure organizations and target project resources to
maximize the potential for equitable distribution of resources.23 For example, the proposed project will
attempt to target tailend farners by encouraging FOs to cite CTWs in the middle of the watercourse, rather
that at the head, in an attempt to improve the reliability of water delivery to the tailend.24
42.             A key element in the success of the farmer participation plan implemented by the SSTP
consultants is the extent that the SOs serve as intermediaries between the FO membership and the
government functionaries who sanction FO formation. SOs effectively "level the playing field" for all
farmers to participate in project activities by intervening on behalf of an FO's membership in activities
that would ordinarily cost farmers considerable time and money. These activities include the opening of
an FO bank account, registering the FO, and attesting the FO's registration and bank balance to the project
authorities to secure financial assistance for CTW installation.
43.             Another key element in the initial success of the SSTP farmer participation approach is
the promotion of farmer collaboration in productive investments, i.e., a CTW that provides FO
membership greater control over the application of irrigation water.  As the AKRSP experience
demonstrates, collective investment activities show the most promise for improving the sustainability of
farmer organizations. In contrast, OFWM efforts to build sustainable WUAs by providing improved
agricultural extension services fell short. Recent research indicates that Pakistan's farmers use available
water supplies with maximum efficiency. While OFWM interventions may have improved the reliability
of surface water deliveries, they did not improve that level of reliability enough to enable middle and
tailend farmners to increase the profitability of their farms in the way that PTWs and CTWs do.25
21   Meinzen-Dick, et al. 1995. op. cit.
2    An example of the impact of more equitable distribution of water resources can be found among FOs established
under the SSTP in Jaranwala Tehsil, Faisalabad District. Two CTWs were installed at a bifurcation of the main
watercourse supplied by the upper branch of the Rakh Canal and supplemented by water pumped from STW No.
JW 118. Because of improved water supply, each member of the FO situated at the tailend of the watercourse
reported sowing a rice crop on land that had previously not been cultivated due to lack of irrigation water.
25   Goldensohn, 1994. op. cit. The ADB-supported Second Community Irrigation Project in Muzzafarabad District of
Punjab has applied the principal of productive investment in the promotion of FO organization and installation of
CTWs. Under this project, FOs are organized among watercourse shareholders and provided incentives to take over
the operation of the existing STWs. In addition, the project provides farm implements to the FO, which rents these
implements to members.

Lessons Learned
44.           From the review of farmer participation in Pakistan, and specifically in the irrigation
sector, the following lessons can be drawn:
(a)    The conjunctive use of surface and groundwater resources improves irrigation efficiencies
and the equity of resource distribution.
(b)    In comparison with public and private (individual) systems of pump irrigation, inigation
efficiencies and equity of water distribution improve when farming communities
collaborate to operate and maintain tubewells and the conjunctive use of surface and
groundwater resources.
(c)    The sustainability of farmer organizations is directly proportional to the extent that they
are organized around productive investment and they have true control over the
management of those resources to maximize return on the investment.
(d)    The formation of enduring farmer organizations requires continuous support at the social
as well as the technical level to ensure that the organization endures potential social,
economic and political conflicts that discourage collective action.
(e)    Under current circumstances in Punjab, consultants are likely to provide the best level of
support to FOs. Government departments do not have the capacity or experience to
support FOs, and ingrained procedures prevent government bureaucracies from readily
adapting to what has been considered more effective procedures.
(f)    NGO capacity in Punjab is oriented towards the promotion of social welfare and not
participatory rural development. However, the their social activism and familiarity with
rural social relations can be tapped and used effectively when consultants provide capacity
building and administrative support to NGO staff.
45.           These lessons have been incorporated into the proposed plan for farmer participation in
the proposed Project described in the following section.
Farmer Participation Under the Project
46.    The Institutional Framework.       The proposed project aims to convert approximately 4,200
STWs in four SCARP regions of Punjab Province over a period of five years. The project will support
the formation of approximately 6,300 village-level Farmer Organizations (FOs) and their development of
privately operated community tubewells (CTWs) to replace government-operated STWs throughout the
project area. The objective of this project activity is to develop FOs that can efficiently operate and
maintain surface and groundwater resources for irrigated agriculture in Punjab on a sustainable basis. The
achievement of this objective will require considerable human and financial resources. A grass roots effort
of this type requires an organization with specific skills and experience in social mobilization and a
network of local level contacts. The Bank's pre-appraisal mission explored the possibility of using NGOs
to assume responsibility for managing the social mobilization aspects of the project. However, it was
mission's assessment that NGO capacity in Punjab is too limited to handle the extensive task. Therefore,
the preferred alternative is to establish an institutional framework consisting of a Project Management Unit
(PMU); social mobilization consultants and local level NGOs with the participation of PAD and PID.



Annex 4
-94-                                  Page 14 of 17
47.           The project will support the development of a farmer participation program within the
PMU. The PMU will have overall responsibility for the project's social mobilization efforts through a
Community Development Support Group (CDSG) recruited as consultancy services. The CDSG team will
consist of: i) a core staff responsible for CDSG field staff training and management, and social
mobilization program coordination; and ii) a field staff of Social Organizers (SOs) working with the help
of NGOs and village level social activists to mobilize farmer participation in project activities. CDSG will
have responsibility for the privatization of approximately 2700, or two-thirds of the STWs targeted for
privatization. The Directorate of On Farm Water Management (OFWM) will be responsible for the
privatization of the remaining targeted STWs in conjunction with a program of project-supported
watercourse improvement. The PMU will have responsibility for coordination and supervision of all STW
privatization and FO development activities supported by the project, including those undertaken by
OFWM
48.            The CDSG core staff will be comprised of internationally recruited consultants.  The
core team will include a Team Leader (45 sm), a Deputy Team Leader (60 sm), a Social Mobilization
Specialist (15 sin) and a Training Specialist (5 sm). The core consultants will be based in Lahore and
housed in the offices of the PMU. The core team will be responsible for training SOs in farmer group
formation and social assessment, as well as develop farmer training packages for SOs in basic literacy,
financial management (bookkeeping, credit management, savings), legal requirements of FOs, operations
and maintenance (including water sales), interaction with Government agencies, and dispute resolution.
49.           The consultants would provide up to 445 staff years of services over the five year life of
the project. These consultants would comprise a Field Operations Management team, based in Lahore,
four Watercourse Assessment Survey Teams, and four social mobilization field teams whose size will be
expanded as project activities increase in geographical scope. In addition to support staff, each field team
will typically include: one Social Coordinator (SC), with responsibilities for managing the field office; two
Technical Coordiniators (TCs); up to ten Social Organizers (SOs), up to five After-care Social Organizers
(ASOs). and a Watercourse Assessment Survey Team. Wherever possible, SOs and ASOs will be
recruited through local NGOs throughout the project area to promote the development of effective
relationshilps with potential beneficiaries.
50.    Social Mobilization Strategy.      The project will promote the formation of at least one FO
per watercourse supplied by each STW targeted for privatization. The participation of at least 30 percent
of the farmers who are watercourse shareholders is required to form an FO. Once established, the FO will
be offered financial assistance amounting to Rs. 20,000 towards the modification/rehabilitation of an
existing STW or the installation of a new CTW. This amount represents approximately 30 percent of the
capital cost of a new CTW. The remaining cost will be borne by the FO membership. FOs choosing to
assume ownership and operation of the existing STW will be charged Rs. 10,000 for the capital transfer.
51.           Based on the SSTP's experience with promoting farmer participation, it is estimated that
one trained SO can complete the privatization of 15 STWs per year. It is also estimated that the STW
transition -- from the initial point of contact between SO and potential members of an FO to the
completion of CTW installation -- will require as much as 16 weeks of ongoing support from CDSG field
office staff, at least in the introductory stages of the social mobilization program in the target areas. An
additional 14 to 24 months of field staff effort will be dedicated to supporting the ongoing functions of
each FO. Each SO will be assigned a cluster of 30 STWS within which he/she will concentrate his/her
efforts for a period of eighteen months to two years. The scope of field staff activities required to support
FO formation can be divided into three distinct stages -- pre-STW transition, transition and post-transition.
An illustrative scope of these activities is as follows:



Annex 4
-95-                                   Page 15 of 17
Pre-Transition Stage
Week I        Watercourse Assessment Team  and SO survey targeted watercourse; socioeconomic
profile and maps prepared; baseline data tabulated.
Weeks 2-3     SO and ASO visit watercourse on a daily basis to apprise shareholders of imminent STW
closing and explain CTW program. The SOs would identify their counterparts and
provide information to farmers on:
(a)    the closure of the STW;
(b)    the benefits of a CTW  such as lower financial costs of installation of a CTW
versus a PTW --perhaps 10 to 15 times less depending upon the number of share
holders; lower O&M costs; benefits of conjunctive use of CTW  with surface
supplies, which are not available with a PTW, greater equity in distribution of
surface water due to installation of CTW at the head of the watercourse; access
to further project assistance, watercourse renovations, etc.;
(c)    the menu of options available to the group --replacement of STW with CTW(s),
reconfiguration of the existing STW;
(d)    estimation of costs associated with the various options available;
(e)    the nature of project financial assistance requirements of project participation (one
year's O&M deposited in bank account);
(f)    further project assistance available to FOs operating CTWs and required
contributions of the FO;
(g)    legal requirements and status of FOs (minimum  number of residents of
watercourse needed to form a project-assisted FO; legal formation of FO or as
legal sub-group of WUA if one already exists);
(h)    roles and responsibilities of various members in a typical FO-operated CTW;
(i)    training available to FOs.
Transition Stage
Weeks 4-8     SO and ASOs assist in the organization of shareholders into FOs to take advantage of the
project's financial incentives for developing CTWs. This assistance, provided through
weekly or more frequent visits, as required, includes: verification of each FO member's
share holding; the designation of FO office bearers (President, Vice-President, Secretary
and Treasurer); the opening of an FO bank account with donations made by each member
pro-rated against the size of his/her landholding; development of an administrative
capacity within the FO through counseling in organizational methods, conflict resolution
and training in record and accounts keeping; and technical advice (from the TCs) on
tubewell location, installation, and selection, operation and maintenance of pumpsets.
Weeks 8-16    SOs and ASOs supervise CTW  installation and facilitate the processing of project's
financial assistance payment to the FO.
Post-Transition Stage
Weeks 17-24  ASOs make regular visits to the FO to monitor continuity of farmer participation; discuss
and promote improved agronomic and water management practices; mediate disputes and
collect quantitative and qualitative data to monitor project impact.

54.    Training.      The CDSG core staff will develop curriculum and materials for training the social
mobi I ization field staff in the principles of participatory community development, and appropriate methods
for mobilizling farmer participation in FOs. Core staff will also develop training modules for FOs in
record keeping and fiianicial manageinent that can be taught by SOs in the field. The core staff will
organize and facilitate all project start-up traininig workshops and semilars, and subsequent refresher
courses for project field staff. In addition, the core staff will also work with the Department of Irrigation
and OFWM  to develop short introductory courses in hydrology, irrigation engineering, tubewell
mechianiics, watcr resource management and agronomy for the social mobilization field team members.
55.           Semin1ars will be organized for field staff every three months. These seminars will create
an opportunity for SOs to share experiences and lessons learned in their efforts to organize farmers. The
seminiars will also provide an opportunity to improve field training materials and to fine-tune the project's
overall farner participation approach. SOs will also bring together a number of FO leaders three times
per year for discussions on water resource management, problem solving, and the sharing of information
and experience. These discussions will serve as a foundation for developing a model for the establishment
of federations of FOs to manage the secondary levels of the canal systems such as distributaries and minor
canals.
56.    The Farmer Organization Development Plan.           The Government of Punjab has established
PMU and its staffing would be completed by the negotiations. The recruitment of social and management
consultanlts has started and it would be completed by June, 1996. Social mobilization consultants will be
in the field by end July, 1996 and start recruitment of SOs. Recruitment of SOs (about 20 SOs and
support staff for transferring first batch of STWs) and establishment of field offices will be completed by
the end September, 1996 (Chart- I ). Training of SOs will be a continuous process, however, the first batch
will be completed by the end of November, 1996. Social mobilization will start fully from January, 1997
and about 300 STWs would be transferred by June 30, 1997. The cumulative transfer progress would
be about 1,300 by June 30, 1998, 2,300 by June 30, 1999, about 3,300 by June 30, 2000 and 4,233 by
Junie 30. 2001.

57.           Local consultancy services in project monitoring and evaluation will be required to study
and assess the success of the project in meeting the objectives cited above, and to assess the physical,
social and economic impact of the project on the project area and its inhabitants.  The Project
Implementation and Evaluation (PIES) consultanits will assess: (i) impact of the project on water use and

performance of technical assistance contractors in supporting the management and implementation of all

project initiatives.
58.           PIE consultants will be required to develop a case study methodology to monitor and
assess the effectiveness of both CDSG and OFWM FO formation efforts, and the impact that the transition
from reliance on STWs to CTWs installed by FOs has on the equitable distribution of water resources and
the incomes of participating farmers.  Specifically, the PIE consultants will consider:
(a)    The effectiveness of CDSG training and support to Social Organizers responsible for the
implementation of its social mobilization program, particularly in the formation of FOs
and the subsequent provision of technical services to improve on farm water management;
(b)    The extent to which FOs are formed and successfully install, operate and maintain CTWs.
(c)    The impact of transition from STWs to CTWs on the conjunctive use of water for
irrigation, and the consequences for water supply reliability for watercourse tailend users.
(d)    Changes in cropping patterns, including a shift towards production of higher value crops,
increased cropping intensities and improved crop yields that result from project activities;
59.           PIE studies will combine both quantitative and qualitative methods. Quantitative methods
will include socio-economic baseline surveys of a purposive sample of targeted watercourses . The
watercourse survey sample will include watercourses targeted by the project as well as watercourses that
lie outside of the project area. These non-project area watercourses will constitute a control that provides
project managers with data on socioeconomic, agronomic and hydrological developments independent of
project inputs. Sample watercourses will be monitored on a biannual basis during the course of the rabi
and kharif cropping seasons over the life of the project to measure project impact against baseline data.
60.           Qualitative data gathering methods will be employed to compile more detailed and
continuous data on project impact and sustainability than is generally derived from quantitative survey
instruments, and as a check on the reliability of survey data. Data gathering methods will include regular
semi-structured interviews of key informants and focus groups, and participant observation (e.g., overnight
stays in villages and participation in FO activities).
