PAKISTAN
Punjab Private Sector Groundwater Development Project
Groundwater Management and Regulation
L. BACKGROUND
Agriculture is the main economic base of Pakistan. Because of climatic conditions
irrigatioii is essential for sustained agricultural production.  Even though irrigation has existed for
centuries. the relatively recent expansion of surface water delivery systems has resulted in significant
hydrological changes. The additional supplies of surface water to the irrigated land without appropriate
drainage facilities has resulted in increased groundwater levels causing either waterlogged areas or saline
intrusioni or both. The Salinity Control and Reclamation Projects (SCARP) were commenced in the
1960's to conitrol waterlogginig and salinity in irrigated areas.  Large size tubewells operated and
maintained by the Government were installed under these projects. While the SCARP tubewells were
largely successful in controlling waterlogging and salinity their performance declined due to lack of funds
for O&M and poor management. In addition to drainage, tubewells in fresh groundwater area provide
substantial irrigation supplies, supplementing the canal water. Because the farmers saw the viability and
advantages of groundwater development, a very large number of private tubewells (PTW) have been
installed. This had the affect of lowering groundwater levels, and in some locations, encouraging saline
water intrision inito fresh water zones.  There are several reasons for excessive pumpage from
grounidwater. Due to inequitable distribution of surface supplies in some areas groundwater is the only
source for irrigation specifically towards the tails of the surface systems. Lack of knowledge about crop
water requirements and the use of flat rate charges for electricity are also contributing factors to the
overdraft.
2.            The groundwater records indicate that the water tables in fresh areas in general are falling
slowly. This coupled with the continued proliferation of PTWs could potentially damage the groundwater
aquifers. A plan for managinig the groundwater resources should be considered at this stage to avoid
problems whiich could arise due unregulated development.  Therefore, as a part of the project, a
groundwater management plan applicable to all aquifers in Punjab would be developed. This annex
provides an outlile of methodology for developing groundwater management and regulation and plan for
its implementation.
11. EXISTING LEGISLATION & REGULATIONS
I.     Punjab Soil Reclamation Act, 1952
Applicable Provisions
defines tube-well as "well worked with electric or thermal power to draw out sub-soil
water" (Chapter I para 2).
authorizes the Board, to prevent waterlogging and salinity, "to provide for the installation,
management and maintenance of tubewells and disposal of underground water by other
means"; "the draining of villages and land"; "replacement of canal water supply by



-99-                                      Annex 5
Page 2 of 5
tubewell or open well in part or whole"; "the temporary increase or decrease of water
allowance to any area or total curtailment of water supply"; "the prohibition of growing
of any crops or laying down any specific crop rotation". (Chapter IV, para 17).
"As soon as a scheme ... is sanctioned as notified ... the use of underground waters in that
area except the water used for domestic purposes or for watering livestock shall come
Linder control of the Board". (Chapter IV, para 26).
In the area for whichi the scheme has been sanctioned the Board may order any tubewell
for using groundwater, to be closed down either temporarily during specified period of
the year or permanently, provided that such shall order not prohibit use of underground
waters by any owner or occupier of land for domestic purposes and for watering livestock.
For the improvement, reclamation and lowering of sub-soil water table in any local area...
the Board may - direct in respect to any area - the permitting by the owners and occupiers
of setting tubewells and borings and use of water therefrom in place of that canal".
(Chapter V, para 28)
Discussion
Although the main objective of the Soil Reclamation Act at the time of its enactment was
controlling waterlogging and salinity, the Act has provision for controlling the
groundwater use when such use has adverse effect on the resources.
T he Punjab Government issues a series of rules (1965) for safe exploitation of
groundwater aquifers. These rules included requirement for a license for installing
tubewells in the areas declared as project by the Government, procedures and forms for
obtaining license, and established license fees.
Powers of the Board changed from time to time. The executive powers are now exercised
by the Secretary, Irrigation and Power Development (effective January 3 1, 1973).
T here is no evidence that the Rule above was ever implemented.
2.     West Pakistan Water and Power Development Authority, Act 1958.
Applicable Provisions
-      "subject to the provision of any other law for the time being in force, the Authority --shall
have control over the underground water resources of any regime in West Pakistan" (para
11).
-      "before the Authority exercises control ... the area over which and the extent to which and
the extent to which control is intended to be exercised shall be agreed to and notified by
the Government in the official Gazette" (para I I).
Discussion
No evidence of any issuing of required rules.
WAPDA is a Federal agency and since the Pakistan Constitution reserves the control over
water to the Provinces there is some question as to the actual authority of WAPDA to
regulate groundwater.



-100-                          ~~~~~~~Annex 5
-100-                                  Page 3 of 5
3.     Punjab Local Government Ordinance, 1979
(Similar Ordinances for the Provinces - both rural and urban councils).
Applicable Provision
-      "All private sources of water supply within the local area of the urban local council shall
be subject to control, regulation and inspection by the urban local council".
Discussion:   No known regulation or enforcement.
Related Matters
4.     The Preamble to the WAPDA Act provides for the unified and coordinated development of the
water and power resources of Pakistan. It also extends WAPDA's control over the groundwater aquifers,
however, this control is in the context of controlling waterlogging and salinity. In accordance with the
Preamble WAPDA collects, analyzes and publishes surface and groundwater data. Although water quality
data collection is now limited to SCARP areas with increased staff the program could cover the whole
country. WAPDA also collects climatology data nationally.
Conclusions
5.     Neither the Government of Pakistan nor any Provincial Government has undertaken groundwater
regulation except for spacing of wells in SCARP areas. The control provisions in WAPDA Act may be
limited by the Constitutional provision of water being the subject of Provinces. The Soil Reclamation Act
of 1952 with its amendments appears to be most specific legislation about controlling installation of
tubewells where these have ill effects oii the resources.
6.    In any event WAPDA would be responsible for overall monitoring of the basins' surface and
groundwater resource, inter-provincial transfers of surface, ground and drainage waters. Even though
WAPDA could perhaps be designated as the groundwater regulating agency with some relatively minor
legal changes, a conflict could arise between the development arm of WAPDA and the regulatory element
which is better suited for provincial and local Governments. Therefore, separate institutional setup with
representative of the Govermnent and water users or Farmers' Organizations should be considered for
enforcing groundwater regulations. In addition regulatory framework should have a mechanism for self-
management at the local level. The detailed institutional structure for implementation of regulations would
be developed during the project as part of the groundwater regulatory program.
III. DEVELOPMENT OF REGULATORY FRAMEWORK
7.            No work has been done in Pakistan about developing a regulatory framework to manage
the groundwater aquifer with falling water tables. This is due to the reason that until now rising and not
falling water tables were the major problem affecting the agricultural production. Considerable amount
of data gathering, modelling and analysis is needed before starting the enforcement of regulations
controlling the groundwater use. The program of developing regulation and their implementation would
consist of three phases; (i) in the first phase, the groundwater management areas will be established based
on the existing information ; (ii) in the second phase critical areas for groundwater management would
be identified, detailed data collection and monitoring would be carried out in the critical areas, and safe
yields of aquifers and groundwater rights would be determined; (iii) in the third phase a procedure for



-101-                                      Annex 5
Page 4 of 5
regulating the groundwater use in each critical areas, and institutional structure would be developed.
8.     Establishment of Management Areas.        For ease in management of the groundwater basins
the groundwater aquifers in Punjab will be divided into different Groundwater Management areas, based
on existing data, and considering behavior and quality of groundwater aquifers, hydrological and
meteorological data, topographic and geological information, canal command areas, surface water
application, efficiency of use, and cropping patterns, etc. During this phase a database consisting of basic
information for each management areas would also be developed which will be updated on a continuous
basis. The sources of major aquifer recharge as well as extractions will be identified in each management
area. This phase would be completed in a period of one year.
9.     Establishing Critical Areas and Groundwater Rights.     In parallel to establishing the
Groundwater Management areas, the critical areas requiring extensive management would be identified.
The groundwater models would be developed, and calibrated to simulate the groundwater behavior over
a long period including wet and dry cycles. Using the simulation models and monitoring information,
rights to the groundwater will be established by distributary command area, watercourse command (chak)
and by farm. Detailed data collection will be carried out for calibration of the model, and developing a
database for eventual regulation of groundwater use for the critical areas. This phase will be completed
by the second year of the project.
10.    Procedures for Regulation and Institutional Structure.   In preparing the groundwater
regulation procedure the emphasis would be given to simple rules, and self regulating systems tested
around some pilot operations. Basing on the safe yields of the aquifer in the long run (considering wet
and dry cycles), pattern of groundwater water use in the critical areas, utilization rates of private,
community and public tubewells, estimates will be made for safe installed capacity by watercourse
command, as well as by distributaries. For foreseeable future the regulation will be based on the installed
capacity of the tubewells which will be correlated to the actual groundwater extractions through empirical
studies and simulation models. A program for licensing the groundwater tubewells with an objective
towards achieving safe installed capacity will be instituted. In issuing the tubewell licenses preference will
be give to community tubewells supplying to several users in chak. The detailed institutional setup for
groundwater management in each management area will vary depending upon the type and the severity
of the problem. Overall responsibility for regulation of groundwater would be given to an independent
body. This phase would be completed in third project year.
11.    Pilots for Groundwater Management.        As part of developing effective groundwater
regulation strategy a few pilots would be implemented before refining the regulation procedures. The
objectives of the pilots would be to test and evolve most effective methods for groundwater management,
assessment of the extent of regulation required, effectiveness of allocation and distribution of groundwater
rights, and roles of the regulating and monitoring agencies, surface water and drainage agencies, the
farmers and their organizations, local governments and NGOs in implementation of the groundwater
regulations.
12.   Implementation.       The implementation strategy for groundwater management would depend
on the type of groundwater problem in each management area. In areas where water table is still rising,
installation of community tubewells will be encouraged by forming effective FOs through social
mobilization programs, dissemination of the groundwater monitoring information to FOs and teaching
effective water conservation measures to reduce the surface water losses to the groundwater. In areas with
marginal quality groundwater, techniques of improved conjunctive use and saline irrigation would be
disseminated to the FOs. A complete solution would be developed in such areas as the capability of the



-102-                          ~~~~~~~Annex 5
-102-                                   Page 5 of 5
FOs build up, its size becomes larger, covering more than a watercourse command (preferably a
distributary), major problems of inequity in surface water distribution are addressed (to some extent), as
well as provincial capability for regulation in these areas builds up.
13.          In areas where a balance have been achieved or water tables are falling, a program of
licensing the groundwater tubewells will be started during the last two years of the project. At the same
time, suitable mechanisms for increasing the recharge to the fresh groundwater aquifers would be
investigated. A comprehensive public information program would be developed outlining the current
groundwater situation and consequences if the present situation continues. Forming FOs would be a
condition for issuing tubewell licenses. For these areas a local as well as umbrella regulating agency
would be developed. Other important elements of regulation would include, development of self-
regulating systems, and regulation of drilling operations.
14.    Self Regulating Systems.    The best way to a create suitable environment for the development
self regulating system is to install community tubewells (CTWs) instead of individually owned tubewells.
With CTWs excessive installation could be avoided, taking better advantage of conjunctive surface and
groundwater use, and pumpage would be regulated by the availability and the capacity of the surface
system itself. In order to make the system fully self regulating the FOs should be large enough so that
over pumping or saline water intrusion has a direct impact on their cost of operation. This would be a
longer term objective and it has to be achieved through reforms in the surface water distribution systems,
where canal command level management would be entrusted to the Public Utilities and effective FOs
would be developed to management the O&M of the distributary systems.
15.    Drilling Operations.  Licensing drillers could include a testing of the driller's knowledge of
tubewell drilling, requirements of regulatory agency and groundwater protection. To begin with as an
alternative to licensing the drillers, regulating agency would maintain a register of drillers in the
management area. In the longer run, however, the drillers would have to be licensed. In any event,
drillers would be required to provide certain information (depth, formations, depth to water, water samples,
etc.) to the regulatory agency for the continuing purpose of evaluating the groundwater basins.
16.    Permit to Install tubewell.   A permit to drill could be accommodated before the drilling
operation phase and a permit to withdraw handled later. However, in most cases a water user would
accomplish both prior to drilling. Permits could be conditioned on discharge capacity limits, depth to
water limit, size of casing, size of pump, and type electric or diesel. However, with the possibility of self-
regulation the FOR may want the individual users to measure or estimate pumping (keeping logbooks,
measuring drawdown, etc.). Tubewells for domestic, and livestock purposes would be exempted from
permits.

Page I of 6
PAKISTAN
Punjab Private Sector Groundwater Development Project
Terms of Reference
for
Project Impact Evaluation (PIE)
I. INTRODUCTION
1.            The Government of Pakistan (GOP) has requested the International Development
Association (IDA) for a Credit to meet part of the cost of the proposed Punjab Private Sector Groundwater
Development (PGD) Project.  The project is being implemented by the Government of Punjab
(GOPunjab). A Project Management Unit (PMU) in the Planning & Development Board of GOPunjab
is responsible for overall project management and coordination. The Punjab Irrigation Department (PID)
and the Directorate Gieneral of Water Management (OFWM) in the Agricultural Departments are the co-
implementing agencies. These terms of reference are for professional and technical services in various
fields for carrying out project impact evaluation studies (PIES).
2.     Project Objectives.   The overall objectives of the PGD Project are to increase the scope and
productivity of Punjab's irrigation and drainage subsector and increase farmer's incomes. Its specific
objectives are to: (a) redefine government's role in groundwater development with a greater role for the
private sector; (b) develop a regulatory framework and a monitoring program to ensure sustainable use
of the groundwater resources; (c) develop sustainable farmers organizations (FOs) which can efficiently
operate and maintain groundwater irrigation, improve surface irrigation, and establish a base for
participation in the management of the canal systems; (d) rationalize public expenditure on O&M of the
irrigation and drainage systems as well as increase the recovery of public expenditures on irrigation
infrastructure; and (e) avoid environmental hazard of saline water intrusioni into freshi grounidwater
aquifers.
3.     Project Description.  The proposed five-year (FY97-2001) project would cover the fresh
groundwater areas (FGW) of SCARP 11 (including Shahpur), SCARP Ill (Rangpur Unit), SCARP IV and
SCARP V (Shorkot Kamalia) located in administrative divisions of Sargodha, Gujranwala, Faisalabad, and
Lahore. It also includes saline groundwater (SGWs) areas within or at the boundaries of FGW areas. The
gross area is about 1.455 million ha of which about 1.193 million ha have FGW, while the CCA is about
1.292 million hectares of which about 1.068 million hectares have FGW. The major project components
are: (a) privatization of SCARP tubewells (STWs); (b) development of groundwater monitoring and
regulatory framework; (c) improvement of irrigation facilities in fresh groundwater areas, including
improvement of watercourses; (d) prevention of saline groundwater intrusion into fresh aquifers, including
rehabilitation of drainage tubewells, aild lining of watercourses in saline groundwater areas; (e) monitoring
and evaluation; (f) technical assistance and training; (g) project management and supervision.
4.            The proposed project will privatize approximately 4,200 SCARP tubewells (STWs)26.
The project will support the formation of FOs, and, through them, the establishment of privately operated
community tubewells (CTWs) to replace government-operated STWs throughout the project area. The
objective of this project activity is to develop FOs that can efficiently operate and maintain surface and
Ofwhich 2,458 STWs are located in SCARPs 11 and Shahpur, 1,001 in SCARP IV, 570 in SCARP III (Rangpur unit),
and 101 in SCARP V.



Annex 6
-104-                                   Page 2 of 6
groundwater resources for irrigated agriculture in Punjab on a sustainable basis. To achieve this objective,
the project will support the development of a social mobilization program within the Project Management
Unit (PMU). The PMU will have overall responsibility for the project's social mobilization efforts
through a Community Development Support Group (CDSG) recruited as consultancy services. The CDSG
team will consist of: (a) core staff responsible for CDSG field staff training and management, and social
mobilization program coordination; and (b) field staff of Social Organizers (SOs) working with the help
of NGOs and village level social activists to mobilize farmer participation in project activities. CDSG will
have responsibility for the privatization of approximately 2,700, or two-thirds of the STWs targeted for
privatization. The CDSG will also be responsible for assisting the PID in promoting farmer
participation in canal lining, and watch and ward as well as O&M of drainage tubewells
rehabilitated under the project. The Directorate of OFWM will be responsible for privatizing the
remaining 1,500 STWs in conjunction with a program of project-supported w/c improvement.
II. SCOPE OF PIE CONSULTING SERVICES
Description of Assignment
5.            The PMU will engage a consulting firm or an independent organization, following IDA
guidelines, to carry out project impact evaluation studies (PIES). The main objectives of PIES are to
evaluate the success in meeting the project objectives, and to assess its physical, agricultural, social,
financial/fiscal, and economic impact. The PIES would also provide independent and continuous feed
back to the implementing agencies on the project's performance and impact of its various components,
so that corrective action or fine tuning could be undertaken in a timely manner. The PIE studies would
include, but not be limited to, monitoring and evaluating the following:
a.     Physical Aspects:
i.    The capacity utilization of private tubewells (PTWs), and Community tubewells
(CTWs), the impact of the project on total groundwater abstraction, the
groundwater table and sustainability of aquifer;
ii.    the types, capacities, costs, and operating factors of STWs, PTWs, CTWs; and the
relative efficiencies of use of water from STWs, PTWs, and CTWs;
iii.   the nature and extent of water markets, seasonal variations in water sales by
PTWs and CTWs, and their relative impact on equity of groundwater distribution;
iv.    the impact on shallow as well as deep water quality and the sustainability of
groundwater use for agriculture;
v.    the impact on soil salinity (surface and profile);
vi.   the efficacy of the project interventions --canal and watercourse lining, and
rehabilitation of drainage wells-- in preventing saline water intrusion in fresh
groundwater aquifers; and
vii.   the impact of watercourse improvements on conveyance efficiencies and savings
of canal and CTWs water



-105-                                        Annex 6
Page 3 of 6
b.     Agricultural Aspects:
i.     The impact on cropping patterns, intensities, yields, and shifts towards higher
value crops;
ii.    the changes in the use of agricultural inputs;
iii.    comparison of agricultural productivity of farms receiving canal plus SCARP
tubewells (STWs) water, with those receiving canal plus PTWs water, and canal
plus CTWs water;
iv.    the impact of watercourse improvements, and drainage tubewells rehabilitation on
cropping patterns, intensities, and yields; and
v.     the impact of canal lining on reliability of surface water supplies, particularly in
the tail reaches.
c.     Sociological Aspects:
i.     Case studies and random monitoring of FOs would be carried out to assess:
(I)    their financial viability and sustainability prospects;
(2)    the equity of CTWs water distribution amongst members of FOs, and cost
sharing and O&M arrangements;
(3)    the success or failure of FOs in operating and maintaining CTWs for the
benefit of all its members;
(4)    the impact on small farmers and contribution to poverty alleviation;
(5)    the size and composition of FOs benefiting from CTWs, and those that
rely on canal and canal pus PTWs water only, following the closure of
STWs;
(6)    the potential of FOs to play an increasing role in management of surface
water supplies, O&M of watercourses, minor and distributary canals,
drainage tubewells, and small surface drains; and collection of canal
water charges.
ii.    the effectiveness of the project's approach to community mobilization and the
appropriateness, and timeliness (or otherwise) of the delivery of the project's
incentive package;
iii.   the performance of the community mobilization consultants, OFWM  staff, and
NGOs in establishing viable FOs;
iv.    the impact of transition from STWs to CTWs on the conjunctive use of canal and
groundwater for irrigation, and the consequences for water supply reliability for



-106-                         ~~~~~~~Annex 6
-106-                                   Page 4 of 6
tail-end users.
d.     Financial and Fiscal Aspects:
i.    the impact on the gross and net farm incomes of farmers, including both members
and non-members of FOs;
ii.    the capacity of farmers to pay their share of the costs of CTWs, watercourse
improvement, and canal lining;
iii.   the reduction in the fiscal burden of GOPunjab as a result of the privatization of
STWs; and
iv.    the effectiveness of the altemate use of the funds that become available as a result
of privatization of STWs (i.e whether or not saved funds have been allocated for
O&M of canals).
e.     Economic Aspects:
i.    estimation of the project's overall economic rate of return (ERR) on "before and
after" as well as "with and without project" basis;
ii.    assessment of CTWs versus PTWs as the least cost solution to replacing the
STWs;
iii.   comparison of economic costs of water pumped by PTWs and CTWs;
iv.    assessment of prospects of long term sustainability of fresh groundwater aquifers
in the "with" and "without" project scenarios; and
v.     assessment of the secondary impact of the project on economic activity in the
project area.
Study Methodology
6.            The PIE consultants would make the best use of the MIS and data base developed by the
PMU and PIA consultants. However, they would be responsible for developing their own data base for
appropriate key indicators of the project's impact. They would, augment the PIA consultants' data base,
as necessary, through field observations and studies. The PIE consultants would develop their own
methodology for monitoring and evaluating the impact of various project components. This will include
design, development, testing, and operationalizing a system for monitoring and evaluating the project
impact.

Outputs and Reporting Schedule
8.            The consultants will prepare the following reports, inter alia
a.     An inception report within two months of commencing the assignment;
b.     a baseline/benchmark report within six months of commencing the assignment;
c.     bi-annual reports by cropping season;
d.     quarterly reports on the performance of FOs based on selected case studies as well as
random monitoring;
e.     Annual summary reports;
f.     special reports as may be necessary from time to time on specific assignments;
g.     a preliminary project impact evaluation report to be issued before the Credit Closing date.
III. IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS
9.            A Project Management Unit (PMU) in the Punjab Planning and Development Board is
responsible for coordinating overall project implementation, and directly implementing some of the project
components. The PMU is composed of a full time Project Director (PD), two ex-officio Members (one
each from the Irrigation Department and the Directorate General of Water Management), and three
Directors as well as support staff.  The PMU would be supported by the PIA consultants for whose
The short-listed consultants should submit their own estimates of staff inputs and team composition.



-108-                                     Annex 6
Page 6 of 6
selection a separate invitation for proposals is being issued. The PMU is responsible for: (a) overall
project management and coordination; (b) development of groundwater regulatory framework; (c)
community development for formation of FOs and establishment of CTWs, including processing and
disbursement of financial assistance to FOs; (d) project performance monitoring & evaluation, including
development of an MIS system; (e) impact evaluation studies; (f) planning, budgeting, maintenance of
consolidated project accounts, and ensuring timely submission of audit reports; (g) procurement of vehicles
and all consulting services required for the project.
10.                 The PID through the Member (Irrigation) of PMU, is responsible for STWs
closures and retrieval of machinery from the bore holes and their capping, canal rehabilitation/lining,
drainage rehabilitation and O&M contracts, and re-deployment of redundant staff.
I I.                The Directorate General of Water Management is responsible for improving
watercourses, and establishing FOs and CTWs in designated areas. Area and field teams would be
established for this purpose. The Member (Water Management) would, inter alia, be responsible for
(i) ensuring close coordination between the PMU and the Directorate General of Water Management.
12.                 The PMU will sign the contract with the selected PIE consultants and hold
responsibility for all matters relating to the management of this contract. The Team Leader of the PIE
consultants will report to the PD PMU through the Director (Regulation) who is responsible for monitoring
and evaluation. The consultants will also report to the Members for (Irrigation) and (Water Management)
for providing PIE feed back relating to canal lining and watercourse improvement and community
mobilization aspects.

PAKISTAN
Punjab Private Sector Groundwater Development Project
Project Implementation Plan
A. The Project
The overall project objectives are to increase the scope and productivity of Punjab's
irrigation and drainage subsector and increase farmers' incomes. Its specific objectives are to:
(a)    redefine government's role in groundwater development and provide assistance to
facilitate change;
(b)    develop a monitoring program and regulatory framework to ensure sustainable use of the
groundwater resources;
(c)    develop sustainable farmers' organizations (FOs) which can efficiently operate and
maintain groundwater irrigation, improve surface irrigation and establish a base for
participation in the management of the canal systems;
(d)    increase beneficiaries' incomes and alleviate poverty;
(e)    rationalize public expenditure on O&M of the irrigation and drainage systems as well as
increase the recovery of public expenditures on irrigation infrastructure; and
(f)    avoid environmental hazard of saline water intrusion into fresh groundwater aquifers.
2.     The major project components are:
(a)    privatization of SCARP tubewells;
(b)    development of a groundwater monitoring and regulatory framework;
(c)    improvement of irrigation conveyance facilities. Improvement of watercourses and canal
structures in fresh groundwater areas;
(d)    prevention of saline groundwater intrusion in fresh aquifers.  Improvement of
watercourses and canals and construction/rehabilitation of drainage facilities in the saline
areas;
(e)    monitoring and evaluation of the project impact; and
(f)    project management, and technical assistance and training.



-126-                                     Annex 9
Page 2 of 14
C. Implementation Arrangements
3.     General.     The project would be implemented by a Project Management Unit (PMU) in the
Punjab Planning and Development (P&D) Department, Punjab Irrigation (PID), Agriculture (PAD), and
FOs in collaboration with WAPDA. The existing Provincial Policy Committee (PPC) and the Project
Coordination Committees (PCC) established under the ongoing Second SCARP Transition Project would
provide policy guidance, assist with inter-Departmental coordination, and hold periodic reviews of progress
at the provincial and field levels, respectively. The proposed organization is shown in Charts I and 2,
and the roles of implementing agencies are described below.
4.     The Project management Unit (PMU).       The PMU would have minimal staff only for the
duration of the project and it would be supported mainly by the consultants. PMU would be composed
of a full time Project Director (PD), two ex-officio Members (one each from PID and the Directorate
General of Water Management (DGWM), and three Directors as well as support staff. The Directors
would be recruited from the open market or supplied by a management consulting firms on fixed-term
contracts for the duration of the project. The PD would be a senior official of the P&D Board of the level
of Member of the Board. The PMU would be supported by a multi-disciplinary team of consultants who
would provide project implementation assistance (PIA) in project management, community mobilization,
engineering design and construction supervision, and development of groundwater regulatory framework
(terms of reference are in Annex 3). The PMU would also employ an independent organization or
consulting firm to carry out project impact evaluation studies (PIES) (TOR are in Annex 6).
5.    The PD PMN  would be responsible for overseeing overall project implementation. The Director
[MIS, Administration, Budget and Accounts (MABA)I would be an experienced Chartered Accountant
(or equivalent) with experience in maintaining an MIS, administration, preparation of budgets and
maintenance of accounts. With the assistance of the MIS, as well as budget and accounts specialist
supplied by the PIA consultants, the Director (MABA) would establish an MIS for the project and submit
periodic progress reports on all project components to PMU, and IDA, be responsible for administrative
matters relating to the PMU, and prepare annual budgets and requests for appropriation of funds in the
Annual Development Programs.  The Director [Community Development (CD)] would be a
sociologist/anthropologist with experience in community mobilization and participatory irrigation
management, preferably in groundwater management. With the assistance of the CDSG teamn supplied
by the PIA consultants, the Director (CD) would, inter alia, be responsible for managing and supervising
the operations of the project's four field offices, the CDSG team of PIA consultants, and the NGOs
involved in community mobilization, maintaining consistency of community mobilization methodology
and approach followed by the PIA consultants, OFWM  field teams and NGOs.  The Director
[Regulation] would be a groundwater specialist with experience in legal aspects of groundwater
management and regulation, as well as monitoring and evaluation of groundwater projects. The main
responsibilities of this position would include managing and supervising the PIE consultants, and the PIA
consultants team responsible for developing groundwater regulatory framework.
6.    The PID through the Member (Irrigation) of PMU, would be responsible for STWs closures
and retrieval of machinery from the boreholes and their capping (this would be done through open
auctions), canal rehabilitation/lining, drainage rehabilitation, performance contracts for O&M, and re-
deployment of redundant staff. For this purpose, PID would put all the SCARPs included in the project,
and one canal construction division under the jurisdiction of the Member (Irrigation). The PAD,
through the Directorate General of Water Management, would be responsible for improving
watercourses, and establishing FOs and CTWs in designated areas. The DG Water Management would
designate a Project Manager, and assign four Area and twenty five Field teams to carry out these



-127-                                      Annex 9
Page 3 of 14
activities. The Member (Water Management) would, inter alia. be responsible for: (i) ensuring close
coordination between the PMU and the Directorate General of Water Management; (ii) drafting necessary
bye laws for WUAs and FOs to allow ownership and/or leasing of CTWs/land; and (iii) preparing
innovative designs for layout of CTWs so that they could be installed on the public land along the
watercourses.
7.     NGO's Role.   Currently there are no NGOs in Punjab with experience in establishing FOs and
capable of assuming full responsibility for managing an extensive social mobilization program needed for
the project. However, some local service NGOs have working relationships with local community based
organizations CBOs and/or local activists who could help mobilize farmer participation in the project.
Wherever feasible, the PMU will seek cooperation of local service NGOs and CBOs either directly or
through the PIA consultants to assist with community mobilization. In selecting NGOs/CBOs, it would
be ensured that they: (a) are legally registered and maintain accounts that are audited annually; (b) are
involved in the rural development as their main objective and they are not affiliated with any political
organization; (c) have adequate number of field staff conversant with local languages and customs, and
have good knowledge and understanding of the socio-economic conditions in the area; and (d) are
supportive of the project objectives and can work in harmony with the rest of the project team.
8.     FO's Role.    FO's would play a critical role in project implementation. They would be eligible
for receiving project assistance for establishing CTWs if they have membership of at least one-third of
the farmers within the command of an STW. FOs wishing to establish CTWs would be required to
register themselves as a water users association or its sub-group (e.g. a tubewell committee) under the
Punjab Water Users Associations Ordinance of 1981. The FOs at the CTW level would, inter alia, have
the following responsibilities: (a) interacting with the field tearns of PMU and/or OFWM staff to prepare
work plans for transfer of STWs; (b) determining the best option for replacing STW pumpage, in terms
of type, size and location of CTWs; (c) installing, operating and maintaining CTW, and distributing water
to its members according to agreed shares and schedules; (d) maintaining bank accounts and collecting
members' share of capital, operation and maintenance and replacement costs; (e) maintaining capital and
O&M accounts and log books; (f) improving water management in the watercourse command; and (f)
participating in watercourse and canal improvement programs.
9.            Watercourses would be improved in areas where the majority of farrmers on a watercourse
agree to cooperate and manage water deliveries at the chak level. In fresh groundwater areas these FOs
would qualify for project assistance for watercourse improvements after they have effectively operated and
maintained their CTWs for a period of one year. During this period they would: (i) elect their executive
committee, including a Chairman, a General Secretary, and a Treasurer; (ii) improve distribution of canal
as well as CTW water, and the community and farn channels; (iii) open a bank account, generate funds
for up-front contribution for W/C improvement, from sales of CTW water and contributions from FO
members; (iv) collect farmers' shares for repayment of material costs to the government; and (v)
participate in improving water distribution at the distributary level, and collecting contributions for the
capital cost of improving distributaries.
10.    WAPDA's Role.        WAPDA would provide electric connections to FOs who are close to
electric supply and decide to install electric CTWs. Where STWs are converted to CTWs as such under
the transfer mode, WAPDA will transfer the title of electrical connection to FOs against normally needed
security deposits. WAPDA would also assist FOs in removing electrical equipment and wiring no longer
needed.



Annex 9
-128-                                 Page 4 of 14
Project Coordination Committee
11.                 At the project level, an existing Project Coordination Committee (PCC) would be
responsible for ensuring coordination among various agencies and facilitating project implementation. The
PCC would be chaired by PD of PMU and have the following members: (i) from PID: SE's of concerned
SCARP, canal and drainage Circles; (ii) from the PAD: the Area Coordinator of OFWM; and the Deputy
Director Agriculture; (iii) a representative of District Administration; and (iv) representatives of selected
FOs. The PCC would meet once every month. The PMU would also act as its secretariat.
Provincial Policy Committee
12.                 The existing Provincial Policy Committee (PPC) would provide policy guidance
and steer project implementation by providing inter-agency coordination at the highest level. The PPC
would be chaired by the Chairman P&D Board and the Secretaries of Irrigation, Agriculture, and Finance
would be its members. The PD of PMU would act as its secretary. The PPC would meet at least every
six months, or whenever issues requiring resolution are submitted for its consideration. The main
functionls of the PPC would be to: (i) resolve issues not settled by the PCC; (ii) ensure provision of
adequate budgetary allocations for project implementation as well as operation and maintenance (O&M);
(iii) ensure adequacy and continuity of PMU staff; (iv) ensure that the project complies with legal
covenanits; and (v) recruit and supervise consultants to carry out full supervision of the project twice a
year, independently of the PMU.
C. Implementation Schedule
13.          A summary of implementation schedule for the project is given in Table 1. Details by
project components is given in their respective sections and annexes. A summary description is give
below.
14.    Documents Processing and Approvals.      The project was jointly appraised by IDA and
FAO/CP in June/July, 1995. The Yellow cover was finalized in December, 1995. The credit negotiations
with would be held in April, 1996 and the project is expected to be approved by Board in May, 1996.
On the Government side the preparation of PC-I started in November, 1996 and approved by the Punjab,
Irrigation, Agriculture departments in February, 1996. The P&D would process the PC-I and submit for
the approval of the Provincial Development Working Party (PDWP) in April, 1996. The PDWP approval
would be obtain in May, 1996. The ECNEC would give full approval of PC- I by September 30, 1996.
15.    Recruitment of Consulting Services. The short-list of consulting firms for the project
implementation have been completed by the PMU and cleared by IDA. The Letter of Invitation for
technical and financial proposals have been launched on March 3, 1996. The proposals would be
submitted by the short-listed firms by March 18, 1996. The evaluation of the technical and financial
proposals would be completed by the May 31, 1996. The consultants selections would be completed and
a consultancy contact would be signed by June 30, 1996. The consultants would mobilize in July, 1996
and establishment of the field offices would be completed by October 30, 1996.
16.          For recruitment of M&E consultants, in December, 1995 the Government have invited
expression of interest from the consulting firms for preparation of a long-list. The short-list would be
completed by April 15, 1996. Technical and financial proposals from the short-listed firms would be
invited by June 30, 1996 after IDA clearance of the invitation package. The consultants selection would



Annex 9
-129-                                 Page 5 of 14
be completed November 30, 1996 and the consultants would mobilize and establish the field offices by
December 31, 1996.
17.    Procurement of Vehicles and Equipment.   Contrary  to  previous project in  Pakistan,
procurement of all vehicles and equipment needed for the project would be procured by one agency, PMU
through a single bid package. The package would be launched well in advance so the vehicles and
equipment would be available at the time of mobilization of staff and the consultants. The procurement
package have been prepared with several sub-packages consisting of homogeneous items. The package
would be launched by March 31, 1996 and the supply contracts would be signed by June 31, 1996. The
procuremenit would be completed by September 30, 1996.
18.    Disinvestment of SCARP Tubewells. All field offices would begin to function fully by
December, 1996/January 1997. All the social mobilization staff both field and headquarter would start
functioning by January 31, 1997 except the social organizers who will be recruited as required by the
speed of implementation. In the first fiscal year only 20 SOs would be in place the number would
increase to 60 or more (as needed) in the following years. By June, 1997 about 800 FOs would be
formed for installation of CTWs and about 200 STWs would be replaced with 300 CTWs. In project
years (PY) two to four about 1,590 CTWs would be installed annually and target of 6,360 would be
completed in project year 5.
19.    Watercourse (W/C) Improvements.  In fresh groundwater area the about 400 watercourse level
FOs would be formed by June 30, 1997. Watercourse improvements in fresh area would start from the
second year of the project on watercourse where CTWs have worked successfully for one year. It is
estimated that about 100 W/Cs would be improved in PY2. The number would increase to 300 in PY3,
600 in PY4 and 500 in the last year. In the saline areas the OFWM staff will concentrate on developing
W/C level FOs in the first project year. In the PY2 about 50 watercourse would be improved, about 200
in PY3 and PY4, and about 50 in PY5.
20.    Canal Improvements. Preparation of detailed canal designs would start in January, 1996 and
would be completed by November 30, 1997. The tender documents would be finalized by December 31,
1997. The bids would be invited through ICB in January, 1998 and the work would start in July, 1998.
About 30 percent of the works would be completed in PY3 followed by 40 and 30 percent in PY4 and
PY S.
21.    Drainage Works.    About 10 saline tubewells would be rehabilitated in the saline unit of
SCARP-II in PY3 and remaining 15 in PY4.
D. Key Performance Indicators
22.           The performance indicators which would be monitored during the project implementation
to evaluate the performance of the project are classified in four groups, the input indicators, the output
indicators, process indicators and the impact indicators. These would be monitored by the PMU through
field visits and observations, third party supervision missions, and the monitoring and evaluation studies.
The IDA missions would also monitor these indicator during field visits project supervision missions. The
summary of key indicators are provided in Table 2.



Annex 9
-130-                                  Page 6 of 14
E. Supervision Plan
IDA Supervision Input and Key Activities.
23.           IDA would supervise the project twice a year on average. If required supervision would
occur more frequently during the first two years to help steer it through the start-up stage. The project
launchi and procurement workshops would also be organized during this period. The IDA supervision
would comprise of one main supervision mission annually in the second semester of the fiscal year,
supplemented by a shorter mission in the first semester, and correspondence and clearances as needed.
Intensity of supervision missions would be reduced after the project is successfully past this start-up stage,
and a regime of enihanced Government has been established. Intensive IDA supervision during the start-up
stage is justified given the nature of the project, which unlike conventional irrigation projects, is
"software" intensive rather than "hardware" intensive. Community mobilization is a crucial element of
the project. This is a relatively new area in irrigation projects in Pakistan and requires greater supervision
inputs by IDA. Table 3 contains IDA's proposed supervision plan, and details of staff inputs and skill
mix requirements for supervision missions. IDA supervision would be facilitated by the proposed
arrangemenits for enhanced Government supervision.
Borrowers Contribution to Supervision
24.           The PMU would have a lead role in supervision the project implementation, and
responsible for ensuring that all project components are appropriately monitored, based on the key
indicators and regularly measured to give an idea of the project's impact and success in meeting its
objectives. The PMU would have primary responsibility for re-directing and restructuring of the project,
chaniginig the levels and types of inputs, and processes to ensure that the project objectives are met and
implemenitationi proceeds smoothly and according to the schedule. The PMU would make use of the
moniitoring and evaluation studies, Management Information Systems and budgeting and accounting
meclianiisins and third party supervision for rigorous superior of the project.
25.    Third Party Supervision.    In line with the decision taken during the 1995 Country Portfolio
Perfornmance Review (CPPR), the project provides resources to support enhanced project supervision by
GOPunjab. The P&D Board would hire, independently of the PMU, services of specialists in various
fields to carry out detailed supervision of the project on behalf of GOPunjab from time to time (on
average two times a year) and submit detailed supervision reports. TOR of these specialists as well as
their reports would be shared with IDA.
26.    Annual Work Plans and Progress Reporting.        The MIS of the PMU would be designed
to meet the monitoring and reporting requirements of all project components. The PIA consultants would
identify the key progress indicators and design an appropriate MIS system, including an "issues and
actions oriented" progress reporting format acceptable to the PMU and IDA. The PMU, with inputs from
PID and PAD, would also prepare detailed annual plans/work programs and budgets, as well as requests
for allocation of funds in the federal/provincial annual development programs. The annual plans would
duly take into account feedback from project impact evaluation studies. The PID and PAD would submit
monthly progress reports to the PMU. The PMU in turn would integrate them and submit periodic
progress reports covering all project components to the P&D Board, PID, PAD and IDA. The PMU
would also periodically prepare working papers for the PCC and PPC meetings. Assurances would be
obtained that by March 31 each year, the PMU would submit to IDA for its review, a draft Annual Plan,
including work program and the proposed budgetary allocation for the following fiscal year, cleared by
the PPC.



Annex 9
-131-                                 Page 7 of 14
27.    Project Launch Workshop. To facilitate project start-up. the PMU in collaboration with PID
and PAD, would hold a project launch workshop soon after Credit signing (in early FY97). The
workshop would, inter alia, familiarize project staff (as well as officials of concerned implementing and
cooperating agencies) with the project objectives, components, implementation schedule and institutional
arrangements, procurement and disbursement procedures, and monitoring and evaluation aspects of the
project.
28.    Procurement Workshops.    Several procurement workshops would be conducted to provide
assistance in the procurement planning, the use of Banks Standard Bidding document, preparation of bid
documents, evaluation of bids and award of contracts. These workshops would be organized to train local
staff in procurement with the aim to enhance general capability in this area and, in addition to the project
staff will be open to staff of other agencies involved in procurement under large projects.
29.    Mid-Term Review.   To evaluate the project's progress, and prospects of meeting its objectives,
a detailed mid-term review (MTR) would be carried out jointly by GOPunjab, GOP and IDA during the
third year of implementation, not latter than March 31, 1999.  The MTR would evaluate the overall
progress and assess need for adjustments in the project scope and design, implementation approach and
methodology, and policy aspects, to ensure timely completion and achievement of project objectives. The
PMU would submit draft TOR for the MTR for the P&D Board's and IDA's review by September 30,
1998, and an MTR Preparation Report compiled with the assistance of the PIA and PIE consultants, or
other specialists by January 31, 1999.

PAKISTAN
Punjab Private Sector Groundwater Development Project
Table 2: Proposed Key Performance Indicators
Input Indicators
1.    Staffing of the project implementation units, Headquarters and Field staff, number of SOs in the
field.
2.     Number of farmers approached for installation of CTWs and watercourse improvements.
3.    CTWs incentive disbursement targets and achievements.
4.     Budget required, allocated and released by GOPunjab.
5.     Project expenditures planned and actually achieved.
6.     Key findings and feed back from M&E studies and steps taken to address issues raised.
Output Indicators
1.    Number of FOs formed, number of meetings held, collection of O&M and capital investment
funds, and opening of Bank accounts.
2.     Number of CTWs installed, coverage of STW share holders (%), CCA served (%), per cent of
share holders by size of holding, location. (head, middle, tail); average cost of PTWs and CTWs;
average depth of bore holes, % CTWs located on communal or Government land.
3.    Number of watercourse level FOS formed, watercourses improved, contribution by FOs for
improvements of watercourses.
4.     Cost recovery due and achieved (arrears and current).
5.    Electric power capacity released due to closure of STWs.
6.     Number of STWs for which notifications for withdrawal of double abiana issue.
7.    Reduction in O&M expenditure of PID.
Process Indicators
2.     Staff deployed on various activities
2.     Surveys required and completed
3.    Studies undertaken and completed
4.     Training planned and implemented (type and coverage)
5.     Number of after-care visits undertaken and messages delivered
6.     Designs of civil works, and preparation of bidding documents.
Impact Indicators
I.    Average water table depth and trend in project area as compared to project/base line situation;
percent of area waterlogged, 0-5', 5-10', 10-15, and > 15.'
2.     Average utilization rates of CTWs.
3.     Shallow and deep water quality (Ec, RSC, SAR and overall assessment: usable, marginal,
hazardous.
4.     Soil quality as compared to baseline.
5.    Water savings due to watercourse and canal improvement, conveyance efficiencies achieved as
compared to baseline (pre-improvement).



Annex 9
-136-                                Page 12 of 14
6.     Reduction in GOPunjab's O&M budget and expenditure due to closure of STWs.
7.     Equity of water distribution among FO members, and sale of CTW water to non-members; status
of O&M of CTWs; and reserves generated through sale of CTWs water for O&M and
replacement of CTWs; and average life of CTWs.
8.     Percent of FOs with outstanding performance; percent of FOs functioning smoothly without
disputes and with chances of long term sustainability, percent of FOs having disputes; percent
of non functioning FOs.
9.     Financial sustainability of FOs, balance in their Bank account, amount received from water sales
annually, contributions towards O&M of the tubewells and the surface system.
10.    FOs participating improvements of surface system such as canal lining etc.
11.    Average cropping intensities, cropping patterns and yields of main crops in project area for FO
members and non-members as compared to baseline/controls.
12.   Changes in farm incomes (gross and net) as compared to baseline and controls.
13.    Estimated economic rate of return of the project.
